Wetness: Privation or Positive Quality?
Definitional classification of wetness as privative or positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the classification of wetness as privative or positive depends on its definition.

Core Idea
Wetness is not inherently positive; its nature depends on definition.

This brief passage addresses a foundational conceptual question in kalam: whether qualities like wetness are real existent attributes or mere absences. The author presents two competing definitions from the philosophers, showing that the answer shifts based on how we define the term. Let's read carefully to grasp this subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal English translation. Notice the question structure and the two conditional clauses.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives two definitions of wetness: (1) non-resistance (la mamani'a) according to philosophers, making it privative; (2) ease of adhesion (suhulat al-ilsaq), making it positive. Note that dryness is the opposite.
The classification changes entirely based on definition.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction is not merely semantic. It affects how we understand attributes in theology and philosophy. Ask yourself: which definition seems more intuitive and why?
This connects to broader debates about the reality of qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: هل الرطوبة عدمية أو وجردية) الرطوبة إن كانت عبارة عن اللاممانعة على ما يقوله الفلاسفة كانت عدمية وإن كانت عبارة عن سهولة الإلصاق كانت وجودية واليبوسة فى مقابلتها .
Question: Is wetness privative or positive? Wetness, if it is defined as non-resistance according to the philosophers, is privative; and if it is defined as ease of adhesion, it is positive, and dryness is its opposite.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification. It raises the question of whether wetness (al-rutuba) is a privative or positive quality. It presents two definitions: the philosophers' definition of wetness as the absence of resistance (la mamani'a) makes it a privation (adamiyya). Alternatively, if wetness is defined as ease of adhesion (suhulat al-ilsaq), it is a positive (wujudiyya) quality. Dryness (yubusa) is the opposite of wetness under either definition.

Key Terms
الرطوبة
اليبوسة
عدمية
وجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wetness is always a positive quality.
Correction: The passage shows that wetness can be considered privative if defined as non-resistance, depending on the definition adopted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is wetness considered a privative quality?
A) When defined as ease of adhesion.
B) When defined as non-resistance.
C) When it is the same as dryness.
D) When it is sensed.

Key Takeaway
Wetness can be either privative or positive depending on whether it is defined as non-resistance or ease of adhesion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'wetness', do you instinctively assume it is a positive quality? Are you willing to let a logical definition change your immediate assumption? This lesson confronts you: what other simple, everyday concepts might you be misclassifying because you never paused to define them?
?
What does it mean for your own beliefs if you realize that their classification as true or false depends on how you define the terms? Are you prepared to re-examine your core convictions to see if they hold under different definitions? Or do you prefer the comfort of unexamined assumptions?
?
If it is so easy to switch between ‘wetness is privative’ and ‘wetness is positive’ by simply changing the definition, how can you be certain about any quality’s nature? Will you commit to always asking 'By which definition?' before making a judgment about reality?

Weight as an Attribute Beyond Motion
Argues that weight is a separate quality not reducible to motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author argues that weight is a distinct attribute that cannot be reduced to motion.

Core Idea
Weight is not just motion; it is a separate quality sensed even without motion.

This lesson examines a concise proof from classical kalam that weight (thiqal) is an attribute over and above motion. The author presents two empirical observations: a heavy object suspended in air still feels heavy, and an inflated skin forced underwater feels light despite its motion. These examples show that weight and lightness are intrinsic qualities, not mere effects of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author states a claim ('weight is additional to motion') and then gives two examples.
The text is corrupted but the logic remains clear.
2
Analyze the first example
Consider the heavy object placed in air. Even when it is stationary, we sense its weight. This suggests weight exists independently of motion.
3
Analyze the second example
Consider the inflated skin forced underwater. It has motion (being pushed down) but we sense lightness (khiffah) rather than weight. So the sensation of lightness is also independent.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The two examples together show that weight (and lightness) are qualities that can be present without motion or even contrary to the direction of motion. Therefore, they are additional attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الثقل أمر زائد على الحركة ) لان الثقيل الممكن في الجوةسرا نحس بثقله والزق المنفوخ المسكن تحت المساء قسرا نحس بخفته مع عدم
Question: Weight is an additional attribute to motion, because the heavy thing [placed] in the air we sense its weight, and the inflated skin placed under water by force we sense its lightness with absence [of motion].
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof. It argues that weight is an attribute over and above motion. The proof uses two examples: a heavy object suspended in air is still sensed as heavy despite lack of motion; an inflated skin forced underwater is sensed as light though it has motion. The conclusion is that weight cannot be reduced to motion; it is a distinct positive quality. The text breaks off but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
الثقل
الحركة
الخفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That weight is just a form of motion or caused by motion.
Correction: The author shows that weight is sensed even when there is no motion, and lightness is sensed even when there is motion, proving they are independent attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for weight being separate from motion?
A) Heavy objects always move downwards.
B) Weight is sensed even when there is no motion.
C) Motion and weight are the same thing.
D) Lightness is a form of weight.

Key Takeaway
Weight and lightness are intrinsic qualities that are not reducible to motion, as shown by sensory experiences of stationary heavy objects and moving light objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel frustrated by your own spiritual heaviness (laziness, sin), do you assume it will disappear once you start moving—praying, fasting? What if that heaviness is an intrinsic state that requires deeper change, not just motion? How will you address the weight itself, not just the activity?
?
You often judge people by their actions—their motion—calling them 'good' or 'bad' based on what they do. But this lesson says qualities like weight can be sensed without motion. What hidden quality in someone have you ignored because their outward motion was misleading? Will you look beyond actions to see their true state?
?
The inflated skin is forced underwater but still felt light. Think of a moment when you were forced into a situation that should have felt heavy, but actually felt light because of your intrinsic faith. Are you leaning on that faith now, or are you letting external circumstances dictate your spiritual sensation?

Definition of Softness (al-Layn)
Defines softness as absence of resistance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise technical definition of softness (al-layn) in kalam terminology as the absence of resistance to a pressing object.

Core Idea
Softness means the absence of resistance to a pressing object.

Imam Razi begins with a concise definition of softness. In kalam, physical properties are defined functionally. Here, softness is defined not as a feeling but as a lack of resistance. This definition is used in arguments about atomism and bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the single Arabic sentence defining softness. Notice the term 'ghamir' (pressing object). This is a technical term.
The definition is very short but precise.
2
Understand 'absence of resistance'
Reflect on what 'absence of resistance' means. It is a relational property: the soft body does not push back against something pressing into it.
This contrasts with common notions of softness as a feel.
3
Contrast with other properties
Keep in mind that this definition is distinct from smoothness and roughness, which are defined by surface evenness rather than resistance.
These will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : اللين معناه عدم ممانعة الغامر ( مسألة: الملاسة عبارة عن استواء وضع الأجزاء والخشونة عبارة عن كون بعضها أرفع وبعضها أخفض )
Question: Softness means the absence of resistance to the pressing object. Question: Smoothness is the evenness of the positions of parts, and roughness is that some of them are higher and some lower.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage contains two brief definitional statements. The first defines softness (layyin) as the absence of resistance to an object that presses upon it. The second defines smoothness (malasa) as the evenness of the arrangement of surface parts, and roughness (khushuna) as variation where some parts are elevated and others lower.

Definitions of Smoothness and Roughness
Defines smoothness and roughness as evenness vs unevenness of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of smoothness (al-malasa) and roughness (al-khushuna) as based on the evenness or unevenness of the positions of surface parts.

Core Idea
Smoothness is evenness of parts; roughness is unevenness.

After defining softness, Imam Razi turns to two surface properties: smoothness and roughness. These are defined in terms of the relative positions of the smallest parts of a body. This distinction is important for understanding how bodies interact.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition sentence
Read the sentence that defines both smoothness and roughness. Note that they are defined together as opposites.
The sentence uses 'عبارة عن' (is a phrase for) to introduce each definition.
2
Understand 'evenness of positions'
Smoothness means that all parts of the surface are at the same level. There is no part jutting out or sinking in.
This is a purely spatial definition.
3
Understand 'some higher, some lower'
Roughness is the opposite: the surface parts are not aligned; some are elevated and others depressed.
This definition does not involve any tactile quality.

Philosophers on Colors and Sounds
Philosophers' views on colors and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of colors as opposites with intermediates, and their analysis of sound qualities beyond mere letters.

Core Idea
Colors have two opposites and a spectrum of intermediates; sounds have multiple qualities beyond letters.

The philosophers (hukama') approached sensible qualities with systematic categories. For colors, they identified white and black as opposites, with a range of intermediate shades. For sounds, they expanded beyond speech sounds to include pitch, volume, and other distinguishing features. This passage presents these classifications, which the author will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on colors and sounds
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the philosophers' own classification come through.
Notice that the author is reporting their views, not yet critiquing.
2
Identify the key categories for colors
Find the two opposites and the list of intermediate colors. Understand that the philosophers see these as transitions from one opposite to the other.
3
Identify the key categories for sounds
Note that sounds have qualities beyond letters: pitch, lightness, loudness, softness, and qualities that distinguish voices.
This shows the philosophers considered more than just linguistic sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الحكماء الضدان هما البياض والسواد والانجاه من أحدهما إلى الآخر يكون بطرق كالغبرة والزرقة والصفرة والحمرة وأمثالها ، وكيفيات الأصوات ليست هى الحروف وحدها بل النقل والخفة والجهارة والخفاءة من كيفياتها ، وكذلك كيفيات أخر يميز الإنسان صوت شخصر من صوت شخص آخر ، وأما الطعوم التسعة قالوا تتولد من تأثير ثلاثة أشياء هى الحرارة والبرودة والكيفية المتوسطة بينهما فى ثلاثة أشياء الكثافة واللطافة والحالة المتوسطة بينهما والثلاثة فى ثلاثة تسعة ، ويرد عليه أن العفوصة والقبض مختلفان بالشدة والضعف الشدة والضعف موضوعيتهما نوعين لكان كل واحد من هذه الأنواع نوعين وأوصير • والحق : أن الطعوم فيها اختلافات كثيرة كما بين حلاوة العسل وحلاوة السكر وحلاوة الدبس وحلاوة البطيخ وغيرها والمركبات منها أيضاً كثيرة لا حد لها ولم يذكر المحسوسة بالشم ، والذين ذكروها قسمرها إلى الملائمة وغير الملائمة وفيها اختلاف لاحد له كما في سائر المحسوسات ، وعدوا الخشونة والملاسة فى المملوسات بدل الصلابة واللين وبالجملة الكلام فى هذا الموضع كثير لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب .
And the philosophers said: The two opposites are whiteness and blackness, and the transition from one to the other occurs through ways like dustiness, blueness, yellowness, redness, and their likes. And the qualities of sounds are not only the letters, but also pitch, lightness, loudness, and softness are among their qualities, as well as other qualities by which a person distinguishes the voice of one person from another. As for the nine tastes, they said they are generated from the effect of three things: heat, cold, and the intermediate quality between them, on three things: density, subtlety, and the intermediate state between them; three times three is nine. And it is objected to this that astringency and constriction differ in intensity and weakness; if intensity and weakness made them two types, then each of these types would be two types. And the truth is that tastes have many differences, like between the sweetness of honey, the sweetness of sugar, the sweetness of molasses, the sweetness of watermelon, and others. Their compounds are also many without limit. And he did not mention what is perceived by smell. Those who mentioned it divided it into pleasant and unpleasant, and there are endless differences in it as in other perceptibles. And they counted roughness and smoothness among tactile qualities instead of hardness and softness. In summary, the discussion on this topic is extensive and cannot be contained in this book.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the views of the philosophers (hukama') on sensible qualities and then offers critique. First, on colors: they say the two opposites are black and white, with intermediate colors like grey, blue, yellow, red, etc. Second, on sounds: they include not just letters but also pitch, lightness, loudness, softness, and other qualities distinguishing voices. Third, on tastes: they claim nine tastes arise from the interaction of three factors (heat, cold, and intermediate) on three substrates (density, subtlety, intermediate), producing nine. An objection is raised: astringency and constriction vary in intensity; if that variation were to multiply types, each would become two. The author then presents the truth (al-haqq): tastes are vastly diverse, with many subtle differences, and the philosophers omitted smell. Those who discuss smell divide it into pleasant and unpleasant, but it also has endless variety. Finally, the author notes that philosophers substitute hardness and softness with roughness and smoothness in tactile qualities, but concludes that detailed discussion exceeds the scope of this book.

الضدان
الأصوات

Misconception: That the philosophers only considered black and white as colors, ignoring intermediates.
Correction: They acknowledged intermediates but considered them transitions rather than pure colors; the passage lists specific intermediate colors.

Key Takeaway

Philosophers on Tastes and Critique
Philosophers' nine-taste theory and author's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' theory of nine tastes and the author's objections to its completeness.

Core Idea
The philosophers' neat nine-taste system fails to capture the real diversity of tastes.

The philosophers proposed that nine tastes arise from the interaction of three factors (heat, cold, intermediate) with three substrates (density, subtlety, intermediate). But the author challenges this, pointing out that tastes like astringency vary in intensity and that countless differences exist even within one type of sweetness. He also notes the omission of smell and the replacement of hardness/softness with roughness/smoothness in tactile qualities. This critique sets the stage for a more nuanced understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taste generation
Read the Arabic passage on tastes and the objection. Follow the philosophers' three-by-three matrix and then the author's critique.
2
Understand the generation theory
Identify the three active qualities (heat, cold, intermediate) and the three passive substrates (density, subtlety, intermediate). This yields nine tastes.
3
Analyze the author's critique
Notice the objection about intensity (astringency/constriction) and the argument that tastes are far more diverse, using examples like different sweet items.
Also note the omission of smell and misclassification of tactile qualities.

Razi's Critique: How Privation Can Be Sensed
Razi critiques the argument that absence cannot be sensed and defends the reality of coldness as a quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that a privative state can be sensed indirectly through its displeasing effects, and how this critique challenges the view that coldness is merely the absence of heat.

Core Idea
Privation can be sensed – not directly, but through its unpleasing consequences.

Razi examines the claim that absence cannot be sensed at all. Using examples like hunger, thirst, and the sensation of cold, he shows that privative states can indeed be perceived when they produce a displeasing effect. This passage invites us to reconsider whether coldness is truly a positive quality or a privative one — and what that means for how we understand sensory experience.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's opening challenge
Notice that Razi begins by questioning a previous statement. Identify his main objection: privative matters can be sensed.
The Arabic text is partially corrupted; focus on the English translation.
2
Analyze the example of coldness
Follow Razi's hypothetical: if coldness were absence of heat, and the organ needs heat, then the lack of heat creates a displeasing sensation — that sensation is felt as cold.
3
Understand Razi's positive view
Razi concludes that coldness is a quality opposite to heat, with distinct effects like contraction and heaviness.
The passage cuts off; this is part of a larger argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : في قوله العدم لا يحس به نظر ، لأن الأمر العدى إذا كان مقتضيا الأمر غير ملائم يحس . من جهة مقتضاه كفريق الاتصال والجوع والعطش ، فإن كانت البرودة عدم الحرارة وكانت الحاسة محتاجة إلى حرارة تعدل مزاجها فعدم تلك الحرارة يقتضى أمرا غير ملائم فيها فيحس به ، ولم يقل أحد أن عدم الحرارة هو الجسم حتى يكون الاجناس بالجسم أجناسا بالبرودة ، والحق أن البرودة كيفية ضد الحرارة، فإن مقتضياتها كمالتكاتف والنقل وأمثالهما ضد مقتضيات الحرارة كالتخلخل والخفة وأمثالهما ، والفلاسفة لم يقولوا إن
I say: In the statement that 'absence is not sensed' there is consideration, because a privative matter, if it necessitates something unpleasing, is sensed by virtue of its necessitating, like the separation of connection, hunger, and thirst. If coldness were the absence of heat, and the sensory organ needed heat to balance its temperament, then the absence of that heat would necessitate something unpleasing in it, so it would be sensed. And no one said that the absence of heat is a body such that sensing the body would be sensing coldness. The truth is that coldness is a quality opposite to heat, for its implications like contraction and heaviness and the like are opposite to the implications of heat like rarefaction and lightness and the like. And the philosophers did not say that...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is Razi's commentary on a previous argument. He begins by questioning the premise that 'absence cannot be sensed.' He argues that a privative state (amr 'adami) can be sensed indirectly when it causes a displeasing effect, like the sensation of separation (furq al-ittisal), hunger, or thirst. Applying this to coldness: if coldness were merely the absence of heat, and the sense organ requires heat for balance, then the lack of heat would produce a displeasing sensation, which is indeed felt as cold. Therefore, the argument that coldness must be a positive quality because it is sensed is not conclusive. He then clarifies that no one claimed absence of heat is a body, so the earlier reductio ad absurdum is invalid. Finally, he states his own view: coldness is a quality opposite to heat, with distinct effects (contraction, heaviness) versus heat's effects (rarefaction, lightness). The passage cuts off, but Razi seems to be rejecting both the privation view and the simplistic positive view, affirming that coldness is a real quality opposed to heat, though its effects may be felt as privation-like.

Key Terms
العدم
الإحساس
البرودة
الحرارة
كيفية
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the argument from sensation decisively proves coldness is a positive quality.
Correction: Razi shows that privation can also be sensed indirectly, so the sensation argument does not prove coldness is a positive quality.

Checkpoint
How does Razi argue that privation can be sensed?
A) By direct perception of absence.
B) By its necessitating an unpleasing effect.
C) By comparing it to hearing.
D) By proving absence is a body.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that privation can be sensed indirectly through its unpleasant effects, and that coldness is a real quality opposite to heat, not merely the absence of heat.

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
4 min
Coldness is a positive quality, not mere absence
Imam Razi refutes the view that coldness is absence of heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that coldness is a real, sensed quality rather than merely the absence of heat.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be sensed, but coldness is sensed, so it must be a positive quality.

A common misconception is that coldness is just the absence of heat. Imam Razi challenges this by appealing to direct sensory experience. In this short passage, he presents a clear argument: we perceive cold as a specific quality, and since non-existence (absence) is not perceptible, coldness cannot be mere absence. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the structure: a claim is stated, then refuted with two reasons.
The author uses the phrase 'otherwise' to present a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
Locate the statement of the opposing view and the author's immediate response ('wrong'). Then find the two arguments: direct perception and the reductio.
3
Analyze the first argument
Imam Razi says we perceive a specific quality from cold things. Why does that disprove absence? Because absence is not a thing that can be sensed.
This is a key principle: non-existence is not perceptible.
4
Analyze the second argument (reductio)
If coldness were absence of heat, then when a body is hot, we would be sensing its absence of coldness—which would be a sensation of coldness. But we don't feel cold when touching something hot.
This shows the contradiction in the opposing view.
5
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect on how this argument establishes coldness as a positive quality. You now understand why it is not simply absence of heat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: منهم من جعل البرودة عدم الحرارة خطأ لأنا نحس من البارد بكيفية مخصوصة فذلك المحسوس ليس عدم الحرارة لأن العدم لا يحس به ولا الجسم وإلا لكان الإحساس بالجسم حال حرارته إحساسا بالبرودة
Issue: Some of them made coldness absence of heat. [This is] wrong because we perceive from the cold a specific quality, so that perceived thing is not absence of heat, for non-existence is not sensed, nor is the body [sensed as cold]; otherwise, the sensation of the body when it is hot would be a sensation of coldness.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a mistaken claim. The author first states the claim that coldness is merely the absence of heat. He then refutes it by arguing that we perceive coldness as a specific, positive quality. Non-existence cannot be sensed, so if coldness were non-existence, it could not be sensed. Furthermore, if coldness were nothing but absence of heat, then when we sense a hot body, we would be sensing its absence of coldness—which is not the case. The argument is straightforward: coldness is a real, sensed quality, not a privation.

Key Terms
البرودة (coldness)
العدم (non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Coldness is merely the absence of heat.
Correction: Coldness is a positive, sensed quality; absence cannot be sensed, so coldness cannot be mere absence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that coldness is absence of heat?
A) Coldness is not sensed at all.
B) Non-existence cannot be sensed, yet coldness is sensed as a specific quality.
C) Heat and cold are the same thing.
D) The body is never cold.

Key Takeaway
Coldness is a real, positive quality that we directly sense, not merely the absence of heat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel cold, do you instinctively reduce it to 'just the absence of heat,' or do you pause to recognize it as a real quality? What does your automatic response reveal about how you perceive the world—are you prone to defining things by what they are not?
?

?
If coldness is a positive quality, then every sensory experience is a direct encounter with a real attribute of creation. How does this reality deepen your gratitude and awareness of the Creator's intricate design in even the most ordinary sensations?

Wetness: Privative or Positive
Imam Razi presents two definitions of wetness and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the classification of wetness as privative or positive depends on its definition, and that different definitions lead to different ontological statuses.

Core Idea
Wetness can be seen as a privation or a positive quality depending on its definition.

In this brief passage, Imam Razi presents two competing definitions of wetness and draws out their implications for its metaphysical status. According to the philosophers, wetness is defined as non-resistance, making it a privation. Alternatively, if defined as ease of adhesion, it becomes a positive quality. This classification affects how we understand dryness as its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the two definitions and their consequences.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the two definitions of wetness mentioned: non-resistance and ease of adhesion. Notice which group is associated with each.
The philosophers are associated with the first definition.
3
Understand the implications
Consider how each definition leads to wetness being either privative (if defined as non-resistance) or positive (if defined as ease of adhesion).
4
Reflect on dryness
Note that dryness is simply the opposite of wetness under either definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: هل الرطوبة عدمية أو وجودية) الرطوبة إن كانت عبارة عن اللاممانعة على ما يقوله الفلاسفة كانت عدمية وإن كانت عبارة عن سهولة الإلصاق كانت وجودية واليبوسة فى مقابلتها .
Issue: Is wetness privative or positive? If wetness is defined as non-resistance, as the philosophers say, then it is privative; if it is defined as ease of adhesion, then it is positive, and dryness is its opposite.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage classifies the nature of wetness based on its definition. The author presents two alternatives: if wetness is defined as 'non-resistance' (al-lā-mumāniʿa), as the philosophers hold, then it is a privation—that is, it is the absence of resistance. If it is defined as 'ease of adhesion' (suhūlat al-ilṣāq), then it is a positive quality. Dryness is the opposite in either case. The author does not choose a side here but simply states the options.

Key Terms
الرطوبة (wetness)
اللاممانعة (non-resistance)
سهولة الإلصاق (ease of adhesion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wetness is always a positive quality.
Correction: Under the definition of non-resistance, wetness is a privation.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, wetness is defined as what?
A) Ease of adhesion
B) Non-resistance
C) A positive quality
D) Dryness

Key Takeaway
The metaphysical status of wetness (privative or positive) depends on its definition: non-resistance (privative) or ease of adhesion (positive).

Heaviness is Additional to Motion
Imam Razi argues that heaviness is a distinct quality from motion using sensory examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi proves that heaviness is a distinct quality from motion through sensory experience.

Core Idea
Heaviness is not just motion; we sense it even when motion is blocked.

Imam al-Razi presents a simple but powerful argument: even when an object's motion is forcibly constrained, we still perceive its heaviness or lightness. This suggests that heaviness and lightness are real qualities, not reducible to motion. In this lesson, we will examine his proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through.
The Arabic text has some corruptions, marked with [?].
2
Identify the two examples
The author gives two examples: a heavy object forcibly kept in the air, and a light object (inflated bladder) forcibly held underwater. Notice how he describes the sensation in each case.
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is that if heaviness were merely a function of motion, then when motion is forced or constrained, the sensation of heaviness or lightness would disappear. But it does not, so heaviness must be additional.
4
Connect to broader kalam discourse
This argument fits into discussions about whether attributes are identical to or distinct from substances. Heaviness is treated as a real attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الثقل أمر زائد على الحركة) لأن الثقيل الممكن في الجوة سرا نحس بثقله والزق المنفوخ المسكن تحت المساء قسرا نحس بخفته مع عدم در كنا فلا يكون وجوديا .
Issue: Heaviness is something additional to motion. Because the heavy [object] possible in [؟] the air forcibly we perceive its heaviness, and the inflated bladder settled under [؟] water forcibly we perceive its lightness with lack [؟] so it is not existential.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage attempts to prove that heaviness and lightness are real qualities distinct from motion. The author gives examples: a heavy object forcibly kept in the air (e.g., suspended) is still sensed as heavy; a light object forcibly kept underwater is sensed as light. The argument is that if heaviness were merely a function of motion, then in forced situations where motion is constrained, the sensation of heaviness or lightness would disappear, but it does not. Thus, heaviness is additional to motion.

Key Terms
الثقل
الخفة
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Heaviness is just the tendency of an object to move downward.
Correction: The author shows that heaviness is sensed even when an object is forcibly prevented from moving downward, so it is a real quality, not merely motion.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what proves that heaviness is additional to motion?
A) Heaviness cannot be sensed.
B) Heavy objects always move downward.
C) Heaviness is sensed even when motion is forced.
D) Motion and heaviness are the same.

Key Takeaway
Heaviness and lightness are distinct realities, not merely functions of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If heaviness is real even when movement is blocked, what hidden burdens have you been ignoring by keeping them still? Will you acknowledge them before they become unbearable?
?
The author proves that qualities exist independent of their actions. What noble quality has Allah placed in you that you have not acted upon because you felt blocked? What would it cost you to let it move?

Definitions of Softness, Smoothness, and Roughness
Imam Razi defines softness, smoothness, and roughness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of softness, smoothness, and roughness as given by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Softness is the absence of resistance; smoothness is uniform part placement.

Imam Razi provides clear, concise definitions for three basic tactile qualities: softness, smoothness, and roughness. These definitions are foundational for discussions about physical properties in kalam. Let's examine each one carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the parallel structure of the two definitions.
The passage begins with 'Issue:' for each term.
2
Identify the three definitions
Locate the definitions for softness, smoothness, and roughness. Note how each is defined in terms of part arrangement or resistance.
Softness is defined negatively (absence of resistance).
3
Understand the privative nature of softness
Softness is not a positive quality but the lack of resistance to pressure. This is a key insight for kalam discussions on qualities.
Compare with smoothness and roughness which are positive descriptions of part arrangement.
4
Contrast smoothness and roughness
Smoothness is equality of part positions; roughness is inequality. This is a binary distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اللين معناه عدم ممانعة الغامر ) مسألة: الملاسة عبارة عن استواء وضع الأجزاء والخشونة عبارة عن كون بعضها أرفع وبعضها أخفض )
Issue: Softness means non-resistance to pressure. Issue: Smoothness is equality of the position of parts, and roughness is that some of them are higher and some lower.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage provides concise definitions for three tactile qualities. Softness is defined as the absence of resistance to a pressing agent (al-ghāmir), making it a privative quality. Smoothness is defined as the uniformity of the arrangement of parts, while roughness is the opposite—some parts are higher and some lower. These definitions are straightforward and focus on physical structure.

Key Terms
اللين (softness)
الملاسة (smoothness)
الخشونة (roughness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Softness is a positive quality or property of an object.
Correction: Imam Razi defines softness as the absence of resistance to pressure, making it a privative (negative) quality, not a positive one.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is softness defined?
A) Equality of parts
B) Non-resistance to pressure
C) Some parts higher than others
D) Ease of adhesion

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi defines softness as absence of resistance, smoothness as uniform part arrangement, and roughness as uneven part arrangement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe something as 'soft', do you actually mean it offers no resistance, or do you mean something else? How does this definition challenge your everyday language?
?
If softness is merely the absence of resistance, then what does that say about your own spiritual state? Are you 'soft' because you offer no resistance to truth, or because you lack conviction?
?
Smoothness is defined as equal arrangement of parts. In your relationships, are your interactions smooth (equal) or rough (uneven)? What would it take to make them truly smooth according to this definition?

Opposites and Colors in the Philosophers' View
Razi reports the philosophers' definition of opposites as white and black with intermediate colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of the two opposites as white and black and their view that intermediate colors are transitions between them.

Core Idea
The two opposites are white and black; other colors are steps between them.

Imam Razi begins by reporting the philosophers' view on sensory opposites. They identify whiteness and blackness as the two primary opposites, and all other colors as intermediate stages in the transition from one extreme to the other. This lesson examines that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence reporting the philosophers' position. Then read the translation. Note the list of intermediate colors.
The passage uses 'baturuq' indicating multiple paths or ways.
2
Identify the two opposites
The philosophers explicitly name whiteness and blackness as the two opposites. Consider why they chose these and not other color pairs.
In classical physics, white and black were often seen as fundamental.
3
Analyze the intermediates
Notice the intermediate colors listed: dust-color, blue, yellow, red. Reflect on how these fit between white and black.
The list suggests a linear spectrum, but modern color theory is more complex.
4
Ask: what is the purpose?
Think about why the philosophers present this classification. Does it help explain sensory perception? Is it complete?
This sets the stage for Razi's critical approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت الحكماء الضدان هما البياض والسواد والانجاه من أحدهما إلى الآخر يكون بطرق كالغبرة والزرقة والصفرة والحمرة وأمثالها ، وكيفيات الأصوات ليست هى الحروف وحدها بل النقل والخفة والجهارة والخفاءة من كيفياتها ، وكذلك كيفيات أخر يميز الإنسان صوت شخص من صوت شخص آخر ، وأما الطعوم التسعة قالوا تتولد من تأثير ثلاثة أشياء هى الحرارة والبرودة والكيفية المتوسطة بينهما فى ثلاثة أشياء الكثافة واللطافة والحالة المتوسطة بينهما والثلاثة فى ثلاثة تسعة ، ويرد عليه أن العفوصة والقبض مختلفان بالشدة والضعف الشدة والضعف موضوعيتهما نوعين لكان كل واحد من هذه الأنواع نوعين وأوصير والحق : أن الطعوم فيها اختلافات كثيرة كما بين حلاوة العسل وحلاوة السكر وحلاوة الدبس وحلاوة البطيخ وغيرها والمركبات منها أيضاً كثيرة لا حد لها ولم يذكر المحسوسة بالشم ، والذين ذكروها قسمرها إلى الملائمة وغير الملائمة وفيها اختلاف لاحد له كما في سائر المحسوسات ، وعدوا الخشونة والملاسة فى المملوسات بدل الصلابة واللين وبالجملة الكلام فى هذا الموضع كثير لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب .
And the philosophers said: The two opposites are whiteness and blackness, and the transition from one to the other occurs through ways like dust-color, blue, yellow, red, and the like. And the qualities of sounds are not only letters, but also pitch, lightness, loudness, and softness are among their qualities, as well as other qualities by which a person distinguishes the voice of one person from another. As for the nine tastes, they said they arise from the influence of three things: heat, cold, and the intermediate quality, upon three things: density, subtlety, and the intermediate state; so three upon three yields nine. It is objected that astringency and constriction differ in intensity and weakness, and intensity and weakness make them two types, so each of these types would be two... And the truth is that tastes have many differences, as between the sweetness of honey, sugar, molasses, and melon, etc., and their compounds are also many without limit. They did not mention the sensible by smell, and those who mentioned it divided it into agreeable and disagreeable, and it has unlimited differences like other sensibles. And they included roughness and smoothness among the tangible qualities instead of hardness and softness. In summary, discussion on this topic is extensive and this book cannot accommodate it.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the philosophers' classification of sensible qualities. It begins with opposites: whiteness and blackness with intermediate colors. Then sounds: not just letters but pitch, loudness, etc. The focus is on the nine tastes derived from three factors (heat, cold, intermediate) interacting with three substrates (density, subtlety, intermediate). The author raises an objection: astringency and constriction differ by intensity, so they should be more than one type. Then the author gives the truth: tastes are far more varied, as seen in different sweet substances. Smell is omitted in the scheme, and when mentioned, it is simply divided into agreeable/disagreeable. The author notes that roughness/smoothness are often listed among tangibles instead of hardness/softness. The passage ends by acknowledging the vastness of the topic, which the book cannot cover fully.

Key Terms
الضدان (the two opposites)
البياض (whiteness)
السواد (blackness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only opposites in colors are white and black.
Correction: The philosophers' view is a specific classification; other opposites exist and colors are more complex.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what are the two opposing qualities in colors?
A) Blue and yellow
B) White and black
C) Red and green
D) Light and dark

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define whiteness and blackness as the fundamental opposites in colors, with other colors as intermediates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers describe white and black as clear opposites with a series of intermediate steps. In your own understanding of Islamic theology, are there any core concepts (like tawhid vs shirk) that you have treated as a gradient rather than a clear binary? Identify one such area and commit to studying its true nature this week.
?
If the philosophers assert that the transition from one opposite to another occurs through defined ways (كالغبرة والزرقة), what 'ways' have you created to transition from obedience to disobedience? Write them down and resolve to remove one such intermediate step starting today.

Qualities of Sounds Beyond Letters
Razi reports the philosophers' view that sound qualities include pitch, loudness, and other features beyond letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers considered sound qualities to include not only letters (phonemes) but also pitch, loudness, and other distinguishing features.

Core Idea
Sound qualities extend beyond spoken letters to include pitch, loudness, and timbre.

After discussing colors, Imam Razi turns to sounds. The philosophers argued that the qualities of sounds are not limited to the letters (phonemes) of speech; they include properties like pitch, lightness, loudness, softness, and other features that allow us to distinguish one person's voice from another. This lesson explores that claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the sound passage
Read the Arabic sentence about sound qualities and its translation. Notice the key terms: النقل (pitch), الخفة (lightness), الجهارة (loudness), الخفاءة (softness).
The passage contrasts 'letters' with these other qualities.
2
Understand 'letters' as baseline
The philosophers note that sound qualities are not only the letters (heard as phonemes). They want to include additional auditory features.
In Arabic, الحروف (al-huruf) means letters or phonemes.
3
Identify the listed qualities
List the qualities mentioned: pitch, lightness, loudness, softness. Think of examples for each.
Pitch is high/low, lightness is softness? Actually الخفة means lightness or softness, الجهارة means loudness, الخفاءة means softness or quietness.
4
Consider the purpose
Why do the philosophers add these? They help distinguish sounds that have the same letters (e.g., same word said by different people).
The passage says 'يميز الإنسان صوت شخص من صوت آخر' (distinguishes one person's voice from another).

The Philosophers' Classification of Tastes and Its Critique
Razi reports the nine-taste derivation, raises an objection, gives the true diversity, and notes omissions in smell and tangibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' derivation of nine tastes from three factors and three substrates, and Imam Razi's critique that this classification is artificial and incomplete.

Core Idea
The nine-taste scheme is neat but fails to capture the real diversity of tastes.

Imam Razi presents the philosophers' theory that nine tastes arise from the interaction of three factors (heat, cold, an intermediate) with three substrates (density, subtlety, an intermediate). He then raises objections: the scheme overlooks intensity differences and the vast variety of real tastes. He also notes omissions of smell and an arbitrary substitution in tangibles. This lesson unpacks the classification and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the tastes passage
Read from 'وأما الطعوم التسعة' to 'لا حد لها'. First, understand the 3x3 derivation.
The Arabic uses a mathematical structure: three factors times three substrates equals nine tastes.
2
Identify the objection
Look for the objection starting with 'ويرد عليه'. Understand why astringency and constriction expose a flaw.
The objection uses logic: if intensity changes produce distinct types, then the nine become many more.
3
Contrast with the truth
Razi states 'والحق: أن الطعوم فيها اختلافات كثيرة'. He gives examples of different sweet tastes. Consider what this shows.
The examples (honey, sugar, molasses, melon) all taste different despite all being sweet.
4
Note the omissions
Read the part about smell and tangibles. Why does Razi mention that they omitted smell and replaced hardness/softness with roughness/smoothness?
This shows the classification is arbitrary and incomplete.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The final sentence says the topic is vast. What does this imply about philosophical schemes?
Razi uses this as a reminder that reality often exceeds neat categories.

وقالت الحكماء الضدان هما البياض والسواد والانجاه من أحدهما إلى الآخر يكون بطرق كالغبرة والزرقة والصفرة والحمرة وأمثالها ، وكيفيات الأصوات ليست هى الحروف وحدها بل النقل والخفة والجهارة والخفاءة من كيفياتها ، وكذلك كيفيات أخر يميز الإنسان صوت شخص من صوت شخص آخر ، وأما الطعوم التسعة قالوا تتولد من تأثير ثلاثة أشياء هى الحرارة والبرودة والكيفية المتوسطة بينهما فى ثلاثة أشياء الكثافة واللطافة والحالة المتوسطة بينهما والثلاثة فى ثلاثة تسعة ، ويرد عليه أن العفوصة والقبض مختلفان بالشدة والضعف الشدة والضعف موضوعيتهما نوعين لكان كل واحد من هذه الأنواع نوعين وأوصير والحق : أن الطعوم فيها اختلافات كثيرة كما بين حلاوة العسل وحلاوة السكر وحلاوة الدبس وحلاوة البطيخ وغيرها والمركبات منها أيضاً كثيرة لا حد لها ولم يذكر المحسوسة بالشم ، والذين ذكروها قسمرها إلى الملائمة وغير الملائمة وفيها اختلاف لاحد له كما في سائر المحسوسات ، وعدوا الخشونة والملاسة فى المملوسات بدل الصلابة واللين وبالجملة الكلام فى هذا الموضع كثير لا يحتمله هذا الكتاب .
And the philosophers said: The two opposites are whiteness and blackness, and the transition from one to the other occurs through ways like dust-color, blue, yellow, red, and the like. And the qualities of sounds are not only letters, but also pitch, lightness, loudness, and softness are among their qualities, as well as other qualities by which a person distinguishes the voice of one person from another. As for the nine tastes, they said they arise from the influence of three things: heat, cold, and the intermediate quality, upon three things: density, subtlety, and the intermediate state; so three upon three yields nine. It is objected that astringency and constriction differ in intensity and weakness, and intensity and weakness make them two types, so each of these types would be two... And the truth is that tastes have many differences, as between the sweetness of honey, sugar, molasses, and melon, etc., and their compounds are also many without limit. They did not mention the sensible by smell, and those who mentioned it divided it into agreeable and disagreeable, and it has unlimited differences like other sensibles. And they included roughness and smoothness among the tangible qualities instead of hardness and softness. In summary, discussion on this topic is extensive and this book cannot accommodate it.

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the philosophers' classification of sensible qualities. It begins with opposites: whiteness and blackness with intermediate colors. Then sounds: not just letters but pitch, loudness, etc. The focus is on the nine tastes derived from three factors (heat, cold, intermediate) interacting with three substrates (density, subtlety, intermediate). The author raises an objection: astringency and constriction differ by intensity, so they should be more than one type. Then the author gives the truth: tastes are far more varied, as seen in different sweet substances. Smell is omitted in the scheme, and when mentioned, it is simply divided into agreeable/disagreeable. The author notes that roughness/smoothness are often listed among tangibles instead of hardness/softness. The passage ends by acknowledging the vastness of the topic, which the book cannot cover fully.

Refutation of Sensible Qualities Surviving Separation
Imam Razi refutes the claim that sensible qualities can exist independent of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Razi refutes the idea that sensible qualities can exist independently of bodies by appealing to the impossibility of accident transference.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot travel; they depend on bodies.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise refutation of an ancient claim that sensible qualities like color, taste, or smell could continue to exist after leaving the body they inhere in. He counters this by invoking the established principle that accidents cannot transfer between substrates. This short passage packs a powerful logical point into a single sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation of the passage to get the basic claim and refutation.
Notice the brevity: the refutation is stated as a simple denial of transfer.
2
Identify the claim and the refutation
The ancients claim sensible things can remain after separation. Razi says it is refuted by refuting the transference of accidents.
3
Understand the key principle
The impossibility of accident transference (intiqāl al-aʿrāḍ) is a foundational kalam principle. Accidents cannot move from one locus to another.
This means qualities like color cannot leave one body and go to another.
4
Reflect on a common misconception
Think about examples like smell or light that appear to transfer. The author insists they do not actually move; they are generated anew.
This clarifies that the refutation is based on philosophical reasoning, not naive observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: من القدماء من زعم أن هذه المحسوسات قد تبقى بعد مفارقة محالها قائمة بأنفسها و إبطاله بإبطال انتقال الأعراض
Issue: Some of the ancients claimed that these sensible things can remain after separation from their loci, subsisting by themselves. Its refutation is by refuting the transference of accidents.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation of a claim that sensible qualities (like color or taste) can continue to exist independently after leaving the body they inhere in. The author responds simply: this is refuted by the impossibility of accidents transferring from one substrate to another. Since accidents cannot migrate, they cannot exist without a body.

Key Terms

محالها

Misconception: Sensible qualities like light or smell can transfer from one object to another.
Correction: In kalam, accidents do not actually move; what appears as transfer is the creation of a new accident by Allah.

Sensible qualities cannot exist independently because accidents cannot transfer between bodily substrates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Paradox of Place: Substance-Location Dilemma
Imam Razi presents the dilemma of place's ontological status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the kalam scholars argued that place cannot be a substance or an accident without leading to paradoxes.

Core Idea
The nature of place leads to a logical dilemma that forces a redefinition of what place is.

Imam Razi presents a classic kalām argument about the nature of place. He begins by noting that the occurrence of a substance in place is a positive attribute. Then he raises a dilemma: if place is non-existent, how can something be in it? If it exists, it must be either a substance or an accident, each leading to absurdities. This passage challenges us to rethink the concept of place in a way that avoids interpenetration or incoherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical dilemma presented by Imam Razi.
Pay attention to the structure: premise, then two horns.
2
Analyze the two horns of the dilemma
Identify why place cannot be non-existent, and why if existent, it leads to problems whether considered a substance or an accident.
Notice the mention of interpenetration (tadākhul) as an impossibility.
3
Consider the escape via contact
Understand that interpreting place as contact (mumāssa) avoids the absurdity of interpenetration, though it may not fully resolve the dilemma.
This shows how kalam scholars refined their definitions to avoid logical pitfalls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأكوان فقد اتفقوا على أن حصول الجوهر فى الحيز أمر ثبوتي فقيل هذا الحيز إن كان معدوما فكيف يعقل حصول الجوهر فى المعدوم وإن كان موجودا فلاشك أنه أمر مشار إليه فهو إما جوهر أو عرض، فإن كان جوهرا كان الجوهر حاصلا في الجوهر وهو قول بالتداخل وهو محال، اللهم إلا أن يفسروا ذلك بالمماسة ولا نزاع فيها، وإن كان عرضا فهو حاصل في الجوهر فكيف يعتمل حصول الجوهر فيه
As for 'akings' (place), they agreed that the occurrence of a substance in a place is a positive matter. So it is said: If this place is non-existent, how can the occurrence of a substance in non-existence be conceived? And if it is existent, then undoubtedly it is something pointed to, so it is either a substance or an accident. If it is a substance, then the substance occurs in a substance, which is the doctrine of interpenetration (tadākhul), and that is impossible, unless they interpret that as contact (mumāssa), and there is no dispute about that. And if it is an accident, then it occurs in the substance, so how can the occurrence of the substance in it be conceived?
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage explores the nature of place (ḥayyiz). It begins by stating that the occurrence of a substance in a place is a positive attribute. Then it raises a dilemma: if place is non-existent, a substance cannot be in it. If place is existent, it must be either a substance or an accident. If it is a substance, then a substance would be inside another substance, implying interpenetration (tadākhul), which is absurd—unless interpreted as mere contact (mumāssa), which is acceptable. If place is an accident, then accidents inhere in substances, so how can a substance be in an accident? The argument exposes the difficulty of conceiving place as a real entity.

Key Terms
الحيز (place)
الجوهر (substance)
التداخل (interpenetration)
المماسة (contact)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Place is an independent entity that can be either a substance or an accident.
Correction: The passage shows that both options lead to absurdities, so place must be understood differently (e.g., as relational or as mere contact).

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the possibility of place being a substance?
A) It is allowed if interpreted as contact.
B) It leads to interpenetration, which is impossible.
C) It is the only plausible option.
D) It is not discussed.

Key Takeaway
The concept of place (ḥayyiz) resists being classified as a substance or accident, revealing the need for a different interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You normally take your physical location for granted—you are 'in' a room, a city. But this passage shows that conceiving of place as a real thing leads to a logical dead end. How does this shake the assumption that you even 'are' anywhere? Can you sense the ground beneath you without clinging to the concept of place?
?
The argument bans interpenetration: one substance cannot occupy the same space as another. Yet your heart is filled with distractions—worries about work, money, status—that seem to occupy the same spiritual space as your devotion to Allah. Are you allowing 'interpenetration' of worldly concerns into the sanctuary of your faith? What would it take to expel them and leave only contact?
?
If place cannot be a substance or accident, then your relationship to Allah cannot be a matter of spatial location. He is not 'in' your heart as a substance is in a place. So what does it mean for you to draw near to Him? Are you content with a connection that has no physical coordinates, or do you secretly wish for a tangible closeness?

Does a substance's location need a cause?
Imam Razi analyzes whether occurrence in place requires a distinct cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Imam Razi, the occurrence of a substance in a place does not require a separate cause, and grasp the two-part reductio argument.

Core Idea
A prior cause cannot explain why a substance is in one place rather than another — unless it pushes.

This lesson examines a nuanced kalām argument about spatial location. Imam Razi presents a dilemma: either the putative cause of a substance's presence in a place exists before it is there, or it does not. Both options lead to problems, showing that no external cause is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure of the argument: a dilemma with two main branches.
The Arabic contains some unclear phrases; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the two branches of the dilemma
Look for the two possibilities: either the cause can exist before the substance's occurrence in that place, or it cannot. Mark these in the text.
3
Follow the first branch (cause exists before)
This branch splits into two sub-branches: either the cause pushes the substance (iʿtimād) or it does not. If it pushes, it's a known concept and not disputed. If not, then it cannot specify one place over another without another cause, leading to infinite regress.
Understand why this branch fails to prove a distinct cause.
4
Follow the second branch (cause exists after)
If the cause can only exist after the substance is already in the place, then its existence depends on that occurrence, making it redundant. It cannot be the cause.
5
Synthesize the conclusion
Since both possibilities are untenable, the original claim that occurrence in place requires a distinct cause is false. The truth is that no such external cause exists.
This conclusion is a key principle in kalām cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في حصول الجوهر بالحيز أن أن ذلك الحصول هل هو معلل بمعنى آخر والحق عدمه لأن المعنى الذى يوجب حصوله في ذلك الحيز ، إما يصح وجوده قبل حصوله في ذلك الحيز أولا يصح ، فإن صح فإما أن يقتضى اندفاع ذلك الجوهر إلى ذلك الحيز أولا يقتضى ، فإن كان الأول كان ذلك هو الاعتماد ولانزاع فيه : وإن كان الثاني لم يكن بأن يحصل بسبب ذلك المعنى في أولى من دين من حصوله فى حيز آخر اللهم إلا بسبب منفصل ثم يعود البحث الأول فيه ، وأما أن لا يصح وجوده إلا بعد حصول الجوهر في ذلك الحيز كان وجوده متوقفا على حصول الجوهر
Issue: They disagreed about the occurrence of the substance in a place: Is that occurrence caused by another meaning? The truth is that it is not. Because the meaning that necessitates its occurrence in that place either can exist before its occurrence in that place or cannot. If it can, then it either requires the repulsion of that substance to that place or it does not. If the first, then that is iʿtimād (pressure/inclination), and there is no dispute about it. If the second, then it cannot be that due to that meaning it becomes more deserving of occurring in one place rather than another, unless by a separate cause, and then the same inquiry returns to that [cause]. And if it cannot exist except after the occurrence of the substance in that place, then its existence is dependent on the occurrence of the substance.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument about whether a substance's presence in a particular place requires a distinct cause (maʿnā). The author's conclusion is that it does not. The argument proceeds by considering a possible cause: either it can exist before the substance's presence in that place, or it cannot. If it can, then either it pushes the substance into that place (in which case it is called iʿtimād, which is a known concept and not disputed), or it does not push, in which case it cannot explain why the substance is in that place rather than another, unless there is an additional cause—leading to an infinite regress. If the cause cannot exist before the substance's presence, then it depends on that presence, making it redundant. The passage appears incomplete but the logical structure is clear.

Key Terms
الحصول (occurrence)
الاعتماد (iʿtimād/inclination)
المعنى (meaning/cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The location of a substance must have a specific prior cause determining it.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that any proposed prior cause either reduces to iʿtimād (which is not the point of dispute) or leads to an infinite regress or circularity, so no separate cause for location is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a cause exists before the substance's occurrence in a place and does not push it, what problem arises?
A) The cause is redundant.
B) The cause cannot prefer one place over another without an additional cause, leading to regress.
C) The cause must be a substance.
D) The cause is iʿtimād.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of a substance in a place does not require a separate cause; any proposed cause either leads to regress or is redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that your being exactly where you are right now has no prior 'meaning' that caused it. Are you truly convinced that every moment of your existence is directly from Allah, with no secondary cause determining your location? How does that change your awareness of His presence this very second?
?
Think of a time you attributed your current situation—your job, your city, your relationships—to a 'cause' like effort or luck. Razi argues such causes cannot explain specificity without infinite regress. Does that make you feel unsettled or liberated? What would it mean to live as if every detail of your placement is from Allah alone?
?
If the cause of your location cannot be a prior 'meaning', then your being here is not deserved or earned—it is a direct gift. Write down one selfish assumption you harbor about your 'right' to be in your current circumstances. How can you turn that into gratitude today?

Origin of the Transfer Illusion
Commentator traces the illusion of accident transfer to light and smell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the mistaken belief in accident transfer came from the apparent movement of light and smell.

Core Idea
Light and smell seem to move, but accidents do not truly transfer.

This short but important note identifies the root of a classical kalam confusion. The commentator points to the everyday observation of light and smell as the source of the illusion that accidents can survive separation from their objects. Understanding this helps us see why the argument against accident transfer is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two examples: light and smell. See how the commentator traces the doubt back to these observable phenomena.
The word 'يتقل' is a scribal variant of 'ينتقل'.
2
Identify the mistaken inference
The ancients saw light and smell appear to move and concluded they remain after leaving their original place. Identify this leap in reasoning.
3
Consider the correction
The commentator does not state the correction directly, but the lesson's context implies that accidents do not actually transfer. Reflect on why the appearance is deceptive.
This connects to the broader kalam principle that accidents are momentary and cannot migrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : إن هذا الشك إنما حصل لهم من الضوء والرائحة وأمثالهما فإنهم لما رأوا الضوء كأنه يتقل من ذي الضوء إلى قابله والرائحة تنتقل من ذي الرائحة إلى الحاسة حسبوا أنها تبقى بعد مفارقة محالها .
(1) I say: This doubt arose for them from light, smell, and their like. For when they saw light as if it transfers from the luminous object to the recipient, and smell transfers from the odorous object to the sense, they thought that they remain after separating from their loci.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This is a marginal note by a commentator (likely the editor or a later scholar) explaining the source of the mistaken belief discussed in the previous lesson. The commentator says that the doubt (shakk) arose from observing light and smell, which appear to move from their sources to other places. Because of this apparent transfer, some ancients mistakenly thought that sensible qualities could survive separation from their original bodies and exist independently.

Key Terms
الضوء (light)
الرائحة (smell)
الشك (doubt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensible qualities like light and smell can actually transfer from one object to another and continue existing.
Correction: The apparent transfer is only an illusion; accidents do not truly move, as they are created anew in each substrate and cannot persist after separation.

Checkpoint

A) Colors changing in the sky.

Key Takeaway
The ancient error about accident transfer originated from a superficial observation of light and smell appearing to move.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Error of Ancients on Sensible Qualities
Imam Razi states the ancients' claim and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some ancients mistakenly believed sensible qualities can persist independently and how this error is refuted by the principle that accidents cannot transfer.

Core Idea
Sensible qualities cannot exist apart from their substrates.

Imam Razi presents a famous mistake: some ancient philosophers thought that qualities like light and smell could leave their source and continue existing on their own. This error, he explains, arose from a superficial observation of how light and smell appear to move. In this lesson, we examine the claim, the refutation, and the underlying confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Razi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the claim and refutation
Find the statement of the ancient claim and the refutation. What principle does Imam Razi use to refute it?
The refutation is brief but fundamental.
3
Understand the source of the error
Imam Razi explains why they made this mistake. Ask yourself: why might light and smell give a false impression?
This connects to the broader principle that accidents do not transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: من القدماء من زعم أن هذه المحسوسات تبقى بعد مفارقة محالها قائمة بأنفسها وإبطاله بإبطال انتقال الأعراض. (1) أقول: إن هذا الشك إنما حصل لهم من الضوء والرائحة وأمثالهما، فإنهم لما رأوا الضوء كأنه ينتقل من ذي الضوء إلى قابله، والرائحة تنتقل من ذي الرائحة إلى الحاسة، حسبوا أنها تبقى بعد مفارقة محالها.
Issue: Some of the ancients claimed that these sensible qualities remain after departing from their substrates, existing in themselves, and its refutation is by refuting the transference of accidents. (1) I say: This doubt only arose for them from light and smell and the like, for when they saw light as if it moves from the illuminated object to its receiver, and smell moves from the odorous object to the sense, they thought that they remain after departing from their substrate.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the first issue: some ancient philosophers claimed that sensible qualities (such as color, smell) persist even after they separate from their physical substrates (e.g., an apple) and exist independently. Imam Razi states that this is refuted by denying the transference of accidents. He then gives his own explanation: the doubt came from observations of light and smell. People see light apparently moving from a source to an illuminated object, and smell seeming to travel from an odorous thing to the sense of smell. They mistakenly thought that these qualities remain after separation. The author implies that this is a confusion about the nature of accidents, which cannot subsist by themselves.

Key Terms
المحسوسات
الأعراض
انتقال الأعراض
الضوء
الرائحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sensible qualities can exist independently of a substrate.
Correction: Accidents cannot subsist by themselves; the apparent movement of light and smell is a sensory illusion.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is the source of the mistaken belief that sensible qualities remain after leaving their substrates?
A) Logical deduction
B) Observation of light and smell
C) Revelation
D) Consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The mistake of the ancients about persistent qualities stems from misinterpreting the apparent movement of light and smell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many times have I accepted a belief based on superficial observation, like the ancients did with light and smell, without testing it against sound principles? What is one such belief in my life that this lesson challenges me to re-examine?
?
If every accident I perceive is a direct creation of Allah and not a transferred quality, how does that reshape my awareness of His constant involvement in every moment? Do I live as if things persist on their own, or do I acknowledge His ongoing creation?
?
Imam Razi points out that the ancients' error came from 'seeing' something that seemed true but was actually false. What 'obvious' thing in my understanding of faith or the world might be a similar illusion that I need to correct through revealed knowledge?
