Unlimited Gradations of Strength
A causal principle linking unlimited strength levels to unlimited mental levels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief levels range across an unlimited continuum, not just a binary certainty/doubt.

Core Idea
Belief strength varies without limit, just like physical strength.

This brief passage establishes a foundational principle for understanding belief: just as physical strength and weakness have unlimited degrees, so do mental states like certainty and illusion. This insight challenges the view that belief is simply 'present or absent', opening the door to a nuanced spectrum of conviction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice the causal structure: 'since... are unlimited, ... are likewise.'
The terms 'fann' (art/skill) and 'wahm' (illusion) here represent species of belief or mental states.
2
Identify the key analogy
Understand that the unlimited gradations of strength/weakness are used as a model for mental gradations. This is an analogy that justifies the infinite variability of belief.
3
Connect to belief levels
Apply this principle: if belief can vary in strength without limit, then there is no single threshold for 'true belief'. This affects how we define certainty and doubt.
This is crucial for epistemology and theology discussions.
4
Reflect on a personal example
Think of a belief you hold. Does it feel stronger or weaker at different times? Why does this support the idea of unlimited gradations?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كانت مراتب القوة أو الضعف غير محدودة كانت مراتب الفن والوهم كذلك
Since the levels of strength or weakness are unlimited, the levels of art (fann) and illusion (wahm) are likewise.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This short sentence provides a general principle: since qualities of strength and weakness can vary without limit, the corresponding levels of mental states like 'art' (fann, likely a term for something like skill or certainty) and 'illusion' (wahm) also have unlimited gradations. This supports the idea that beliefs can vary in strength.

Knowledge as a Relational Attribute
Imam Razi defines knowledge as relational and presents his view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge (ʿilm) is defined as a relational attribute (amr iḍāfī) and how this definition shapes the discourse on divine and human knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not a solitary quality; it is a relation between a knower and a known.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a foundational question in Islamic theology: what is the nature of knowledge? He argues that knowledge is inherently relational, meaning that for something to be 'knowing', there must be a 'known' object. This relational definition sets the stage for later discussions on divine attributes. Let's examine his reasoning and how he distinguishes his view from those of earlier theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Observe how the author presents his claim and the views of others.
Pay attention to the key terms used.
2
Identify the author's claim
Notice that the author states the correct view is that knowledge is relational, and provides a brief justification.
The justification is that we cannot conceive of a knower without a known.
3
Understand the two other positions
The passage summarizes two earlier views: one that adds an entity called taʿlīq, and another that adds a state called ʿālamiyya. Identify what each group affirms.
Both groups affirm more than just the relation.
4
Grasp the author's own position
The author simplifies: he affirms only the relation (taʿalluq) itself, while knowledge and ʿālamiyya are not accepted without proof.
This shows a minimalist approach to attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل إنه أمر إضافى وهو الحق بما أنه لا يمكننا معرفة كون الشىء عالماً إلا إذا وضعنا فى مقابلته معلوما والقائلون به منهم من سمى هذه الإضافة بالتعليق وأثبت أمرا آخر يقتضى هذا التعليق ومنهم من قال العلم عرض يوجب العالمية والعالمية حالة تتعلق بالمعلوم فهؤلاء أنبتوا أمورا ثلاثة ، وأما نحن فلا نقول إلا بهذا التعلق فأما العالمية والعلم فما لم يثبت بالدليل
And it was said that it [knowledge] is a relational attribute, and that is the truth, because we cannot know a thing to be knowing unless we posit against it a known. Among those who hold this, some call this relation 'ta'liq' and affirm another thing that necessitates this relation, and others say knowledge is an accident that necessitates 'worldliness' (al-'alamiyya) and 'worldliness' is a state that relates to the known. So these affirmed three things. As for us, we affirm nothing but this relation. As for 'worldliness' and knowledge, they [are not affirmed unless] established by proof.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of knowledge (ʿilm) as a relational attribute (amr iḍāfī). The author begins by stating that the correct view is that knowledge is relational, because we cannot conceive of something as 'knowing' unless we simultaneously conceive of something 'known' (maʿlūm) opposite it. He then summarizes the positions of earlier thinkers: some call this relation taʿlīq (suspension) and posit an additional entity that necessitates the relation; others hold that knowledge is an accident (ʿaraḍ) that necessitates 'worldliness' (al-ʿālamiyya), and this 'worldliness' is a state (ḥāl) that relates to the known. Thus these two groups affirm three things: the relation, the entity that necessitates it, and the state. The author then states his own position: he affirms only the relation itself (al-taʿalluq), while the existence of 'worldliness' and knowledge as separate entities is not accepted unless proven by evidence.

Key Terms
علم
إضافة
تعلق
تعليق
عالمية
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a simple inherent quality that exists regardless of any object.
Correction: The passage argues that knowledge is necessarily relational; you cannot have a knower without a known.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct view of knowledge (ʿilm)?
A) It is a negative attribute.
B) It is a relational attribute (amr iḍāfī).
C) It is an accident that necessitates worldliness.
D) It is a state unrelated to the known.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is fundamentally a relation between a knower and a known, not a standalone attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your knowledge of Allah is merely a relation, not a settled quality in your soul, then how can you be certain that this relation is real and not just a projection? What proof do you have that your mind is truly connected to the Divine, or are you satisfied with the bare relation itself?
?
You affirm only the relation, as Razi does—but does your daily worship reflect that your knowledge of Allah is nothing but a constant, active connection? Or have you reduced it to a dormant label you carry?
?
If knowledge of Allah is relational, then your ignorance is not a lack of information but a broken link. Where in your life is that link most strained—and what will you do before tonight to repair it?

Does Incapacity Exist as an Attribute?
Razi critiques the view that incapacity is existential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the view of incapacity as an existential attribute is weak due to lack of evidence, and that both definitions (existential vs. absence) remain merely probable without proof.

Core Idea
Without evidence, both defining incapacity as absence or existence are mere guesses.

This lesson examines a brief but sharp critique by Imam Razi regarding whether incapacity ('ajz) should be considered an existential quality. Some Ash'ari theologians argued it is, but Razi finds this unsubstantiated. He also addresses a counter-argument about the definition, showing it too lacks proof, leaving both options as mere possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and the literal English translation. Notice how concise Razi's critique is.
The passage is short but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Identify Razi's two points
First, Razi says the view that incapacity is an existential attribute is weak due to lack of evidence. Second, he rejects the counter-argument that defining it as absence of power is not more likely than the opposite, because both are merely possible without evidence.
Razi's refutation is based on the principle that probability alone is insufficient for theological claims.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Consider the role of evidence in Islamic theology. Razi insists that without a decisive proof (dalil), neither definition can be affirmed. This shows the importance of epistemological rigor in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : عند بعض الأصحاب العجز صفة وجودية وهو ضعيف لعدم الدليل والذي يقال ليس جعل العجز عبارة عن عدم القدرة أولى من العكس ضعيف ، لانا نساعد على أن كليهما محتمل وأنه لولا الدليل لبقى ذلك الاحتمال
Issue: According to some of our companions, incapacity is an existential attribute. It is weak due to lack of evidence. And what is said, that making incapacity an expression of the absence of power is not more likely than the opposite, is weak, because we agree that both are possible, and without evidence that probability would remain.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses whether incapacity ('ajz) is an existential quality (wujūdiyya) or merely the absence of power. Some Ash'ari scholars considered it existential, but Razi says this view is weak due to lack of evidence. He also rejects the counter-argument that defining incapacity as absence of power is not more likely than the opposite, because both are merely probable without evidence.

Key Terms
عجز (incapacity)
صفة وجودية (existential attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Incapacity must be either an existential attribute or the absence of power, and one of these must be true by definition.
Correction: Razi argues that both definitions are merely possible interpretations; without evidence, neither can be affirmed. The truth is not determined by probability alone.

Checkpoint

A) He affirms it.
B) He rejects it as weak.
C) He says it is the same as absence of power.
D) He says it is a matter of definition.

Key Takeaway
Razi rejects the existential view of incapacity as unsubstantiated and shows that without proof, both definitions remain mere possibilities, emphasizing the need for evidence in theological claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you catch yourself leaning toward a view just because it 'makes sense' or is popular, will you pause and ask: 'What is my evidence?' If not, what is your attachment costing your faith?

The Impossibility of Accident Subsisting in Another Accident
Razi states the mutakallimun's agreement on the impossibility of accident on accident and gives the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutakallimun unanimously reject the possibility of an accident subsisting in another accident, and the philosophical argument they refute.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot be hosts; all accidents must ultimately inhere in a substance.

This passage presents a foundational doctrine of kalam: accidents cannot subsist in other accidents. Imam Razi states the consensus of the mutakallimun, then gives their core proof and the philosophers' counterargument, which will be answered later using previously established premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a statement of agreement, a proof, an objection, and a deferred answer.
The OCR has some unclear words, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the two positions
The mutakallimun agree that an accident cannot subsist in another accident. The philosophers disagree. Identify the key claim and the opposing view.
3
Analyse the mutakallimun's proof
The proof is that all accidents must ultimately terminate in a substance. Everything that exists in a locus inheres in the substance, which is the origin.
This prevents infinite regress.
4
Understand the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue that colorness (like a genus) subsists in blackness, an accident. They provide two arguments: the shared attribute and the inherence of accident in substratum.
This appears to show an accident subsisting in an accident.
5
Note the deferral of the answer
Razi says the answer to the philosophers is based on premises already established. This implies the refutation relies on earlier discussions in the text.
Think about what those premises might be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : أتفق المتكلمون على امتناع قيام العرض بالعرض خلافاً للفلاسفة. ومعولنا أنه لابد من الانتهاء بالآخرة إلى الجوهر وحينئذ يكون الكل في حيز الجوهر تبماله وهو الأصل فالكل قائم به. احتجوا بأن السواد يشارك البياض فى اللونية ويخالفه فى السوادية والبياضية، وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز، فاللونية صفة مغايرة للسواد قائمة به، وهما موجودان لانه لا واسطة بين الوجود والعدم فاللونية عرض قائم بالسوادية. وأيضاً فكون العرض حالا في المحل ليس نفس العرض ونفس المحل، لصحة أن يعقلا مع الذهول عن ذلك الحلول، وليس أيضاً أمراً عدمياً لأنه نقيض اللاحلول فهو صفة قائمة بذلك العرض، ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول، فهنا أعراض لا نهاية لها يقوم كل واحد منها بالآخر. والجواب عنها بمقدمات تقدم تقريرها.
Issue: The mutakallimun have agreed on the impossibility of an accident subsisting in an accident, contrary to the philosophers. And our reliance is that it is necessary to ultimately terminate in the substance; then everything is in the locus of the substance with its property [?] and it is the origin, so everything subsists in it. They argued: that blackness shares with whiteness in colorness and differs from it in blackness and whiteness, and that by which there is sharing is other than that by which there is distinction; so colorness is an attribute distinct from blackness subsisting in it, and both exist because there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence; thus colorness is an accident subsisting in blackness. And also: that an accident being in a substratum is not the accident itself nor the substratum itself, because it is valid to conceive them while heedless of that inherence, and it is also not a non-existent thing because it is the opposite of non-inherence; so it is an attribute subsisting in that accident. Then the discussion regarding it is as in the first; so here there are infinite accidents, each one subsisting in the other. And the answer to them is by premises previously established.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal issue (mas'ala) on which the mutakallimun agree: it is impossible for an accident to subsist in another accident. This is contrary to the philosophers. Razi then gives the mutakallimun's core proof: all accidents must ultimately terminate in a substance; everything that exists in a locus exists in the substance. Then he presents the philosophers' counterargument: blackness and whiteness share the genus of colorness, which must be distinct from each specific color; thus colorness subsists in blackness as an accident. This leads to an infinite regress of accidents. The passage concludes by saying the answer to this argument is based on premises already established (referring to a previous discussion). The commentary explains that the mutakallimun reject the idea that accidents can be substrata for other accidents because that would lead to an infinite regress; they hold that all accidents inhere directly in substances.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر
قيام العرض بالعرض
اللونية
السواد
البياض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because colorness is a shared attribute, it must be an accident subsisting in blackness, proving that accidents can host accidents.
Correction: The mutakallimun argue that such a view leads to infinite regress and is therefore impossible; they maintain that all accidents ultimately inhere in substances.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, why is it impossible for an accident to subsist in another accident?
A) Because accidents are not real.
B) Because all accidents must ultimately terminate in a substance.
C) Because accidents cannot have attributes.
D) Because the philosophers proved it.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun maintain that all accidents ultimately inhere in substances, preventing infinite regress; this is a foundational principle in kalam cosmology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that accidents cannot subsist in other accidents—every attribute must eventually rest on a substance. In your own life, are there beliefs or 'accidents'—opinions, preferences, or habits—that you have built upon other shifting foundations, leaving you with an infinite regress of justifications? What is the one unshakable substance upon which your worldview must ultimately rest, and have you honestly examined whether it can bear the weight of all the accidents you hang on it?
?
The philosophers argued that colorness is an accident that must subsist in blackness, but the mutakallimun replied with premises already established. Is there an area in your faith or reasoning where you have accepted a subtle dependency—one thing leaning on another—without tracing it back to its ultimate ground? What would it cost you to follow that chain all the way to its end, and are you willing to accept the answer even if it demands change?
?
Razi says the answer to the philosophers is 'by premises previously established.' When you are confronted with a challenge to your deepest convictions, do you have a foundation of premises so certain that you can reply without panic? What premises are you currently building your life on that you have not reexamined since you first learned them? Commit today to trace one of your core beliefs back to its origin and test whether it truly terminates in a substance or just another accident.

Razi's Proof: Impossibility of Accident Transfer
Razi argues against accident transfer with a proof based on individual existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi holds that an accident cannot transfer from its original substratum, and how this differs from the movement of bodies.

Core Idea
An accident's individual existence is tied to its specific substratum, making transfer impossible.

Razi presents a rigorous argument clarifying that an accident, unlike a body, cannot move from one substratum to another. He first distinguishes between transfer of place (which even the opponent does not claim for accidents) and transfer of substratum. Then he offers a proof based on the accident's dependence on a specific substratum for its very existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Razi begins by clarifying what he means by 'transfer' and then presents his proof.
Pay attention to the distinction between two types of transfer.
2
Identify the two meanings of transfer
Razi distinguishes between transfer of place (for bodies) and transfer of substratum (for accidents). Understand which one is at issue.
The opponent's argument is about the latter, but Razi says it was refuted.
3
Follow the proof against accident transfer
Razi argues that an accident's individual existence requires a specific substratum. Since a vague cause cannot bring about existence, the accident cannot exist with a different substratum.
Key phrase: 'its individual existence is not realized except by a specific substratum'.
4
Compare with the case of a body
Razi contrasts with a body, which only needs location in general, so it can move. This highlights why accidents are different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول فى الانتقال بمعنى الحصول فى حيز بعد الحصول في غيره من الأحياز عن الأعراض لا يحتاج إلى بيان ، فإن العاقل لا يمكن أن يتخيله فضلا أن يدعيه والمطلوب ههنا هو فى الانتقال عنها بمعنى الحصول في محل بعد الحصول فى محل غير ذلك المحل ، وهو لم يتعرض لذلك أصلا وما أورده من الحجة مزيف بما ذكره . والبرهان عليه : أن العرض هو الموجود الذى لا يتحقق وجوده الشخصى إلا بما يحل فيه والشيء المحتاج في وجوده الشخصى إلى علة لا يمكن أن يحتاج إلى علة مبهمة ، لأن المهم لا يكون من حيث هو مبهم موجوداً في الخارج ومالا يكون موجوداً فى الخارج لا يفيد وجوداً فى الخارج بالبديهة ، فالعرض إذا لا يتحقق وجوده إلا بمحل بعينه يتحقق وجوده الشخصى ويبطل بتبدله ذلك الموجود ولذلك يمتنع انتقاله عنه ، وأما الشيء المحتاج في صفة غير الوجود وإلى غيره من حيث طبيعة ذلك الغير كالجسم المحتاج في التحيز لا في الوجود إلى يمتنع أن ينتقل من حيز بعينه إلى حيز آخر يساوى الحيز الأول فى معنى الحيز ، وهكذا إذا تعين مكان الواحد بالنوع كان الواحد بالشخص من جملة ذلك النوع محتاجاً إلى أحد أجزاء حيز ذلك النوع لا بعينه ، ولذلك أمكن انتقاله إلى حيز آخر ، وأيضاً الوجوب الشخصى الحاصل من سبب موجود معه يمكن أن تختلف شرائطه بحسب أزمنة مختلفة كالهيولى المحتاج إلى الصورة لا بعينها ، وذلك غير ما نحن فيه .
I say: Regarding transfer, meaning obtaining in a place after obtaining in another place among the places of accidents, it does not need explanation, for the rational person cannot imagine it, let alone claim it. The required here is regarding transfer from them, meaning obtaining in a place after obtaining in a place other than that place, and he did not address that at all, and what he presented of the argument is refuted by what he mentioned. And the proof for it: that the accident is an entity whose individual existence is not realized except by what it inheres in, and a thing that needs in its individual existence a cause cannot need a vague cause, because the vague, inasmuch as it is vague, does not exist externally, and what does not exist externally cannot cause existence externally by necessity. So the accident's existence is not realized except by a specific substratum by which its individual existence is realized, and it is nullified by the change of that existent; therefore its transfer from it is impossible. As for a thing that needs in a quality other than existence and to another in terms of the nature of that other, such as a body needing in being located but not in existence to transfer from a specific place to another place equal to the first in the meaning of place, and likewise if the place of the one in type is determined, then the one in person from that type needs one of the parts of the place of that type not specifically, and therefore its transfer to another place is possible. Also, the personal necessity arising from a cause existing with it can have its conditions vary according to different times, like matter which needs a form not specifically, and that is not our case.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument (by the author, marked with 'أقول') against the possibility of an accident transferring from one substratum to another. Razi first clarifies that the intended meaning of transfer here is not mere change of position (which is impossible for accidents) but change of substratum. He states that the opponent's argument has been refuted. Then he presents the proof: an accident's individual existence depends entirely on the specific substratum in which it inheres; therefore, it cannot exist with a different substratum. He contrasts this with a body, which only needs location in general, not a specific place, so a body can move. He also gives the example of matter needing a form, but not a specific form. The key distinction is that an accident's existence is tied to a particular substratum, unlike a body's location.

Key Terms
انتقال
عرض
محل
وجود شخصى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident can move from one substance to another, like a body changes location.
Correction: Razi argues an accident's existence is tied to its specific substratum; losing that substratum annihilates the accident, so transfer is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for an accident to transfer from one substratum to another according to Razi?
A) Because accidents have no physical existence.
B) Because its individual existence is tied to its specific substratum.
C) Because bodies prevent transfer.
D) Because transfer is only possible for substances.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that an accident cannot transfer because its individual existence depends on a specific substratum, unlike a body which only needs location in general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that an accident's existence is tied to a specific substratum. In your life, what 'substrata'—like status, relationships, or possessions—do you treat as if they are essential to your identity, forgetting that your true existence depends only on Allah? If those were removed, would your sense of self collapse? Commit to grounding your identity in Allah alone, not in temporary substrata.
?
The proof emphasizes that vague causes have no effect. Are there vague intentions or half-hearted hopes in your spiritual life—like 'I'll pray more' or 'I'll seek knowledge someday'—that you rely on without concrete action? How can you replace vague resolutions with specific, immediate steps tied to real existence? Pick one vague intention today and make it specific.

Razi's Refutation of the Argument for Accident-on-Accident
Razi argues that termination in substance does not rule out intermediate accident-on-accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the argument that accident-on-accident is impossible, and the distinction between termination and intermediation in the chain of accidents.

Core Idea
Termination in substance does not block intermediate accident-on-accident.

Razi here tackles a subtle objection against the possibility of accidents subsisting in other accidents. While both sides agree that the chain must ultimately end in a substance, the real dispute is about whether there can be intermediate steps. Razi points out a confusion between attributes and accidents in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note that the passage ends mid-sentence, but the core argument is complete.
The Arabic text is from page 115 of the Muhassal.
2
Identify Razi's main claim
Find the sentence where Razi states what the necessity of termination does not prove. This is the heart of his refutation.
Look for 'وجوب الانتهاء ... لا يدل على امتناع'.
3
Distinguish termination from intermediation
Razi notes that both parties agree on termination. The disagreement is only about the possibility of intermediate accidents. Understand this distinction.
The opponent's argument conflates these two points.
4
Analyze the criticism
Razi argues that the opponent mistakenly equates attributes with accidents. Note the difference: attributes are conceptually dependent, accidents are existentially dependent.
The passage gives an example that cuts off: 'colorness being the genus of blackness'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : وجوب الانتهاء إلى ما يقوم بالجوهر لا يدل على امتناع قيام البعض بالبعض وقيام البعض الأخير بالجوهر، والقائل بإمكان قيام العرض بالعرض مقر بأن الانتهاء لا يمكن أن يكون إلا إلى الجوهر ، وإنما الخلاف في التوسط هل يمكن أو لا، وهو لم يتعرض لذلك ، وما أورده في احتجاج القائلين بذلك ليس بصحيح ، لأنه أقام الصفات فيها مقام الأعراض والصفة مالا تعقل إلا مع غيره ، والعرض مالا يوجد إلا في غيره ، وقيام بعض الصفات ببعض الأجسام لا يوجب قيام بعض الأعراض ببعض، أما اللونية الجنس للسوادية
I say: The necessity of termination to what subsists in the substance does not prove the impossibility of some [accidents] subsisting in others and the last subsisting in the substance. The proponent of the possibility of accident subsisting in accident acknowledges that termination cannot be except to the substance; the disagreement is only about whether intermediate [accidents] are possible or not, and he did not address that. What he presented in the argument of those who hold that is not correct, because he placed attributes in the position of accidents; but an attribute is not conceived except with another, while an accident does not exist except in another. The subsistence of some attributes in some bodies does not necessitate the subsistence of some accidents in others. As for colorness being the genus of blackness...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is Razi's rebuttal of a specific argument that attempted to prove the impossibility of accident-on-accident based on the necessity of termination in substance. Razi points out that the opponent has misapplied the argument: the proponent of accident-on-accident already agrees that the chain must ultimately end in a substance; the real issue is whether there can be intermediate accidents between the first and the substance. The opponent's argument, which substitutes attributes for accidents, is invalid because attributes are not substances. The passage ends mid-sentence discussing colorness as genus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If accidents must terminate in a substance, then no accident can subsist in another accident at all.
Correction: The necessity of termination does not forbid intermediate accidents; it only requires the chain to end in a substance. The dispute is about intermediation, not termination.

Checkpoint
What is the real point of disagreement according to this passage?
A) Whether accidents exist.
B) Whether intermediate accidents can subsist in other accidents before reaching a substance.
C) Whether substances are eternal.
D) Whether attributes are accidents.

Key Takeaway
Accidents can subsist in other accidents as long as the chain eventually ends in a substance; the opponent's confusion between attributes and accidents invalidates their argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you take time this week to identify one area where you have neglected intermediate means and reconsider your approach?
?
The opponent in this passage made a flawed analogy by conflating attributes with accidents. Think of a time when you might have made a similar mistake—perhaps by assuming that two things are the same because they share a superficial similarity. Could this be affecting how you understand a theological or philosophical issue? Commit to examining one such analogy in your own thinking and testing its validity using the precise definitions from this lesson.

One Accident Cannot Reside in Two Loci
Imam Razi states the consensus and gives proof against exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological consensus that one accident cannot reside in two loci and the rational proof that supports it, as well as the specific exceptions claimed by Abu Hashim and the ancient philosophers.

Core Idea
The impossibility of one accident in two loci is proven by analogy: if so, one body could be in two places.

Imam Razi presents a foundational principle in kalam: no single accident can be inherent in two different substances at the same time. He notes that the consensus is unanimous except for two challenges: the claim of Abu Hashim about composition (al-ta'lif) and the view of some ancient philosophers about relation (al-idafa). Then he provides a logical argument to refute these exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with 'mas'ala' (issue), a common format for theological topics.
2
Identify the consensus and exceptions
The passage states that all theologians agree that one accident cannot reside in two loci, except Abu Hashim who claims composition (ta'lif) can. Also, some philosophers claim relation (idafa) can. Make a mental note of these exceptions.
The exception is not all accidents but specifically composition and relation.
3
Understand the proof by analogy
Imam Razi gives a proof: if one accident could be in two loci, then one body could be in two places, which is absurd. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
The analogy is between accident-locus and body-place relationships.
4
Consider the argument against Abu Hashim
Razi points out that Abu Hashim agrees an accident cannot be in three loci, so why two? Razi demands a distinction. Then he suggests that attributing the difficulty of separating two bodies to the free agent is better than accepting the impossibility.
This shows how theologians use consistency and logical pressure to refute opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اتفقوا على أن العرض الواحد لا يحل في محلين إلا أبا هاشم فإنه قال: التأليف عرض واحد حال في محلين، ووافقنا على أنه يستحيل قيامه بأكثر من محلين، وجمع من قدماء الفلاسفة زعموا أن الإضافة عرض واحد قائم بمحلين كالجوار والقرب. لنا لو جاز في العقل أن يكون الحال في هذا المحل عين الحال في ذلك الجاز أن يكون الحاصل في هذا المكان هو الحاصل في ذلك فيكون الجسم الواحد حاصلا في مكانين، ولأنه وافق على امتناع الحلول في الثلاثة فنطالبه بالفرق وإحالة صعوبة التفكيك على الفاعل المختار أولى من التزام هذا المحال.
Issue: They agreed that one accident does not reside in two loci except Abu Hashim, for he said: composition is one accident existing in two loci, and he agreed with us that it is impossible for it to subsist in more than two loci. And a group of the ancient philosophers claimed that relation is one accident subsisting in two loci, like adjacency and proximity. Our proof: If it were rationally possible for that which is inherent in this locus to be the very same as that inherent in that locus, then it would be possible for what obtains in this place to be what obtains in that place, so one body would obtain in two places. And because he agreed on the impossibility of indwelling in three, we demand from him the distinction. And referring the difficulty of separation to the free agent is more appropriate than committing this impossibility.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal issue (mas'ala) stating that there is consensus among theologians that one accident cannot reside in two different loci, with the exception of Abu Hashim. He claimed that composition (al-ta'lif) is one accident existing in two loci simultaneously, though he agrees it cannot exist in more than two. The passage also notes that some ancient philosophers claimed that relation (al-idafa) is one accident subsisting in two loci, like adjacency and proximity. Imam Razi then provides a proof: if it were rationally possible for an accident to be inherent in two different loci, then by analogy it would be possible for one body to be in two places, which is absurd. Furthermore, since Abu Hashim agrees that an accident cannot be in three loci, we ask him to explain the difference between two and three. Finally, it is more reasonable to attribute the difficulty of separating two bodies to the free agent (who holds them together) than to accept the impossibility of one accident in two loci.

Key Terms
العرض
المحل
التأليف
الإضافة
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the disagreement is about all accidents being able to exist in two loci.
Correction: The disagreement is specifically about composition (al-ta'lif) and relation (al-idafa), not all accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the exception to the consensus that one accident cannot reside in two loci?
A) All accidents according to Abu Hashim
B) Composition (al-ta'lif) according to Abu Hashim
C) Relation (al-idafa) according to the philosophers
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The consensus that one accident cannot reside in two loci is defended by a simple analogy: if it could, then one body could be in two places—an impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that require an 'accident to be in two loci'—logical contradictions you tolerate in your faith? Where are you inconsistent, and will you abandon that inconsistency after seeing Razi's clear proof?
?
If you were to apply the same rational rigor to your daily decisions that Razi applies to this theological issue, what area of your life would demand immediate correction?
?
The consensus of the theologians here is near unanimous. Do you have a pattern of siding with the majority only when convenient, or do you respect established proofs even when they challenge your preferences?

The First Argument for Indivisible Parts: The Point
Imam Razi gives the first argument for indivisible parts using the point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's first argument for the existence of indivisible parts (jawhar fard) based on the nature of the point.

Core Idea
The point's indivisibility proves the existence of atomic substances.

Imam Razi begins his proofs for the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) with an argument built on the point. The point is agreed to be existent and indivisible, and it serves as the contact point between lines or between a sphere and a plane. By tracing the implications of the point's status—whether it is a substance or an accident—Razi establishes the necessity of an indivisible locus. This lesson walks through each step of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the logical connectors and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ثم هذه النقطة' which introduces the final bifurcation.
2
Identify the key premises
Distill the main premises: the point is existent, indivisible, and serves as the extremity of a line and the contact point between a sphere and a plane.
These premises are backed by 'by agreement' and by logical reasoning about touch.
3
Follow the branching reasoning
Trace the two cases: if the point is extended (substance), it is itself the jawhar fard; if it is an accident, consider the divisibility of its locus.
The case of the accident leads to either a contradiction or the desired conclusion.
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that an indivisible substance must exist, either as the point itself or as the indivisible locus of the point.
This is the foundational argument for atomism in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا وجوه. الأول: أن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودي، ولأن الخط يما [؟] بها خطا آخر، وما به تماس الشيء غيره لا يكون عدما محضا، وهي غير منقسمة بالاتفاق، ولأنها طرف الخط فلو كانت منقسمة لكان طرف الخط أحد نصفيها فلا يكون الطرف طرفا، ولأن موضع الملاقات من الكرة الحقيقية المماسة للسطح المستوى الحقيقي غير منقسم وإلا لكان المنطبق منها على المستوى مستويا فكانت الكرة مضلعة، ثم هذه النقطة إن كانت متحيزة ثبت الجوهر الفرد وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو المطلوب.
And we have proofs. The first: that the point is, by agreement, an existential thing. And because the line touches another line by it, and that by which a thing touches another is not pure nothingness. And it is indivisible by agreement. And because it is the extremity of the line; if it were divisible, then the extremity of the line would be one of its two halves, so it would not be an extremity. And because the place of contact of a true sphere with a true plane surface is indivisible; otherwise, the part of the sphere that contacts the plane would itself be plane, so the sphere would be polyhedral. Then this point: if it is extended (muhayyaza), then the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) is established; and if it is an accident, then regarding its indwelling, if its locus is divisible, then its division by the division of its locus is necessary; and if it is not divisible, then that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is the first argument (wajh) for the existence of the indivisible substance (jawhar fard), i.e., the atom. The argument is based on the point (nuqta). The argument proceeds in steps: 1) The point is agreed to be something existent, not nothing. 2) A line touches another line by means of a point; that which does the touching is not nothing. 3) The point is agreed to be indivisible. 4) As the extremity of a line, if it were divisible, the extremity would be one of its halves, not the endpoint itself. 5) The contact place of a true sphere with a true plane surface is a point; if that point were divisible, the contact area would be planar, making the sphere polyhedral. Conclusion: The point is either a substance or an accident. If it is extended (i.e., occupies space), it is itself the indivisible substance. If it is an accident inhering in something, then either its locus is divisible—then the point would be divisible, contradiction—or its locus is indivisible, which proves the required indivisible substance. Thus, the argument concludes that indivisible parts exist.

Key Terms
النقطة
الجوهر الفرد
الخط
الكرة
التماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is assumed to be indivisible by consensus for everyone.
Correction: The passage notes that the point is indivisible by agreement among those who assert its existence, but the argument itself does not rely solely on consensus; it also provides logical reasoning for indivisibility.

Checkpoint
What does the argument conclude about the point if it is an accident inhering in a locus?
A) The locus must be divisible, so the point is also divisible
B) If the locus is divisible, the point becomes divisible; if not, the locus is an indivisible substance
C) The point cannot be an accident
D) The point is a substance by necessity

Key Takeaway
The point's indivisibility and its role in contact force the existence of an indivisible substance, whether directly as an extended point or indirectly as an indivisible locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The proof forces you to accept the existence of an indivisible substance—something that cannot be broken down. Critique of the Agreement on the Point
The commentator critiques the agreement claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator exposes a contradiction in the claim that the point is existent by agreement, using the author's own principle.

Core Idea
An agreement that contradicts itself is no agreement at all.

The commentator begins by scrutinizing the first premise of the point argument: the assertion that 'the point is, by agreement, an existential thing.' He reveals a hidden contradiction when this is compared with the author's own statement that 'the end of a thing is its non-existence.' This lesson unpacks that logical critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the commentator's reasoning.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice that the author says the end of a thing is non-existence, but the point is defined as the end of a line. So the point should be non-existent, not existent.
This is the key flaw.
3
Understand the 'agreement' issue
The commentator argues that the supposed 'agreement' is invalid because the two disputants do not both accept the premise that the point is existent. He suggests a more accurate phrasing.
This shows careful attention to logical consistency.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this critique matters for the overall argument for the atom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: قوله إن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودي، ثم قوله وهي غير منقسمة بالاتفاق مناقض لقوله نهاية الشيء عدمه فلا يكون وجوديا، والنقطة عند من يقول بها نهاية الخط، فإذا هذا اتفاق من غير تراضي الخصمين، ولو قال بدل ذلك باعتراف القائلين به لكان أصوب. قوله: وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها أيضا غير مسلم عند مخالفيه، فإنهم يقسمون الأعراض إلى السارية في محالها وإلى غير السارية، ويعدون النقطة في غير السارية ويقولون إن غير السارية لا يجب انقسامها بانقسام محالها، وملاقاة الكرة الحقيقية للسطح الجعلي المستوى يكون عندهم بنقطة هي طرف قطر تمر بمركز الكرة بموضع التماس، وإلا فإذا ماسة الكرة سطحا آخر مستويا لطرف الآخر من ذلك القطر ومرت دائرة عظيمة بقطر التماس انقسمت تلك الدائرة بسبب التماسين إلى أربع قسى اثنان متماسان للسطح واثنان غير متماسين، ويلزم من ذلك انطباق القوس على السطح المستقيم وذلك محال، وكون التماس بنقطة وانقسام محل النقطة لا يوجب كون النقطة منقسمة على ما مر.
I say: His statement that 'the point is, by agreement, an existential thing,' and then his statement 'it is indivisible by agreement' contradicts his statement 'the end of a thing is its non-existence, so it is not existential.' The point, according to those who affirm it, is the end of the line. So this is an agreement without the consent of the two disputants. If he had instead said 'by the admission of those who affirm it,' it would be more correct. His statement: 'And if it is an accident in its indwelling, if its locus is divisible, then its division by the division of its locus is necessary' is also not accepted by his opponents, for they divide accidents into those that pervade their loci and those that do not, and they count the point among the non-pervading ones. They say that the non-pervading accident does not necessarily divide with the division of its locus. And the contact of a true sphere with a true plane surface, according to them, is at a point that is the endpoint of a diameter passing through the center of the sphere at the point of contact. Otherwise, if the sphere touches another plane surface at the other end of that diameter, and a great circle passes through the diameter of contact, that circle would be divided by the two contacts into four arcs, two touching the surface and two not, necessitating the arc coinciding with the straight surface, which is impossible. And that contact is at a point, and the division of the locus of the point does not necessitate the point being divided, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary criticizing the first argument for the indivisible substance based on the point. The commentator points out a contradiction: the argument claims the point is 'by agreement' existent and indivisible, but the author himself elsewhere says 'the end of a thing is its non-existence', so the point as an end of a line would be non-existent. Thus, the alleged agreement is not valid because the two disputing parties do not both agree to that premise. The commentator suggests that saying 'by the admission of those who affirm it' would be more accurate. Next, the commentator refutes the claim that if the point is an accident in a divisible locus, it must divide. Opponents distinguish between pervasive accidents (which spread throughout their locus) and non-pervasive ones (like points, which do not extend). Non-pervasive accidents do not divide when their locus divides. The commentator then provides a geometric argument: contact between a true sphere and a true plane is at a point (a non-pervasive accident), and even if the locus of that point (the sphere's surface) is divisible, the point itself remains indivisible. The argument uses the example of a great circle and arcs to show that the point of contact cannot be considered extended, thus defending the view that the point does not necessiate divisibility.

Key Terms
النقطة
الاتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is universally agreed to be an existent thing.
Correction: The agreement is not universal; it is only admitted by those who affirm the point, and the author's own principle contradicts this claim.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator identify as the contradiction in the author's argument?
A) The author says the point is both divisible and indivisible
B) The author claims the point is existent by agreement but also says the end of a thing is non-existence, making the point non-existent
C) The author fails to define the point
D) The author uses the term 'agreement' incorrectly

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that the claim of agreement is logically flawed because it contradicts the author's own definition of an end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever claimed to agree with a belief while holding a contradictory position in your heart? How can you ensure your inner convictions align with your outward statements, especially about Allah?

The commentator's critique hinges on a hidden contradiction in the author's own statements. What hidden contradictions might exist in your own understanding of tawhid that you have not yet examined? Will you seek them out?
?
If a point's existence can be questioned by logical inconsistency, what about your own faith—is it built on consistent premises? What step will you take today to strengthen the logical foundations of your aqeedah?

Non-Pervasive Accidents: Defending the Point's Indivisibility
The commentator rebuts the divisibility argument using non-pervasive accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between pervasive and non-pervasive accidents and how it rebuts the argument that the point must divide with its locus.

Core Idea
Non-pervasive accidents, like the point, remain indivisible even when their locus divides.

The commentator now addresses the second part of the point argument: if the point is an accident in a divisible locus, it must divide. Opponents of this view introduce a crucial distinction between pervasive accidents (which spread throughout their locus) and non-pervasive ones (which do not). The point, they argue, is non-pervasive, so its indivisibility is preserved. A geometric example with a sphere and plane illustrates this concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully, focusing on the opponent's classification of accidents.
The geometric example may be challenging; visualize the sphere and plane.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the two types of accidents: pervasive (sāriya) and non-pervasive (ghayr sāriya). Note where the point fits.
This is a key concept in kalam physics.
3
Follow the geometric example
Trace the argument: the contact of a sphere and plane is at a point. If that point were divisible, it would lead to absurdities. Hence, the point remains indivisible.
The example shows that divisibility of the locus does not force division of the accident.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Understand how this defense supports the existence of indivisible particles (atoms).

Four Views on Body Composition
Enumeration of four views on body composition with marginal note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical positions on body composition based on the distinctions of actual/potential and finite/infinite division.

Core Idea
Bodies can be analyzed through two binary distinctions, yielding four possible views.

Imam Razi presents a classification of views on body composition by considering two binary distinctions: whether division is actual or potential, and whether it is finite or infinite. This enumeration covers positions from the mutakallimun, al-Nazzam, al-Shahrastani, and the philosophers. The passage also includes a marginal note clarifying terminology and attribution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure of the enumeration.
The passage uses a logical division (taqsim) to generate four views.
2
Identify the two binary distinctions
The possible division is first either actual or not, and then either finite or infinite. Understand how these combine.
These distinctions are the basis for the four views.
3
Enumerate the four views and their proponents
List each view and match it to the group or individual mentioned: majority of mutakallimun, al-Nazzam, a rebutted view (attributed to al-Shahrastani), and majority of philosophers.
The third view is labeled 'rebutted' (mardud).
4
Examine the marginal note
Read the note beginning with 'I say'. It criticizes the use of 'muqawwim' for parts and clarifies that the rebutted view is from al-Shahrastani.
This note is likely from a commentator, not the main text.
5
Reflect on the logic and significance
Consider why this classification matters for kalam debates on body composition and atomism.
These views are foundational for later discussions on physical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالانقسام الممكن إما أن يكون حاصلا بالفعل أو لا يكون كذلك ، فعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون متناهيا أو غير متناه فخرج من هذا التقسيم أقسام أربعة . أحدها : أن الجسم مركب من أجزاء متناهية كل واحد منها لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وهو قول جمهور المتكلمين. وثانها : أنه مركب من أجزاء غير متناهية بالفعل وهو قول النظام . وثالثها : أنه غير مركب لكنه قابل لانقسامات متناهية وهو قول مردود . ورابعها : أنه غير مركب لكنه لا ينتهى فى الصغر إلى حد إلا وهو بعد ذلك يكون قابلا للتقد[؟] وهو قول جمهور الفلاسفة )) (١) أقول : إطلاق اسم المقوم على الأجزاء مخالف للعرف فإن المقوم يقال للمحمول الذاتي والجزء لا يحمل على كله والذى يصير الشيء المهم بسببه محصلا بالفعل كالفصل للجنس و الجزء لا يكون «كل ، ، القول المردود هو الذي نسبه في سائر كتبه إلى محمد الشهرستاني ، فإنه قال بذلك في كتابه الموسوم بالمناهج والبيانات .
The possible division is either actual or not; in either case, either finite or infinite. From this division four types emerge: one: that the body is composed of finite parts each of which is not divisible at all – this is the view of the majority of the mutakallimun. Second: that it is composed of actually infinite parts – this is the view of al-Nazzam. Third: that it is not composite but is susceptible to finite divisions – this is a rebutted view. Fourth: that it is not composite but does not terminate in smallness to a limit except that it is thereafter susceptible to division – this is the view of the majority of the philosophers. (1) I say: applying the term 'constituent' (muqawwim) to parts is contrary to convention, for muqawwim is applied to an essential predicate, and the part is not predicated of the whole, and that which makes the thing actually realized, like the differentia for the genus, and the part is not 'the whole'. The rebutted view is that which he attributed in his other books to Muhammad al-Shahrastani, for he said that in his book al-Manahij wa al-Bayanat.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible views on the composition of bodies. It lists four positions: 1) body made of finitely many indivisible parts (general mutakallimun); 2) body made of infinitely many actual parts (al-Nazzam); 3) body not composite but finitely divisible (rebutted); 4) body not composite but infinitely divisible (majority of philosophers). A marginal note (by a commentator) criticizes the use of 'muqawwim' for parts and attributes the third (rebutted) view to al-Shahrastani.

Key Terms
انقسام
أجزاء متناهية
أجزاء غير متناهية
جوهر فرد
النظام
المقوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third view (non-composite but finitely divisible) is accepted or its proponent is unknown.
Correction: The text explicitly calls it 'rebutted' (mardud) and attributes it to al-Shahrastani, as clarified in the marginal note.

Checkpoint

A) Body composed of finite indivisible parts
B) Body composed of infinite actual parts
C) Body not composite but finitely divisible
D) Body not composite but infinitely divisible

Key Takeaway
The four views on body composition are derived from two binary distinctions, and each position is associated with specific theological or philosophical schools.

Argument from the Point for the Indivisible Substance
First proof for the indivisible substance using the point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of a point serves as a proof for the existence of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard) in classical kalam.

Core Idea
The point is indivisible, so if it is a substance, it confirms the atom; if an accident, its substratum must also be indivisible.

This passage presents one of the key arguments for the indivisible substance used by mutakallimun. It begins by establishing the point as an existent, indivisible entity, then uses it to support the atomic theory. The argument relies on geometrical reasoning about lines, spheres, and contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to the conclusion. Pay attention to the key claims: the point is existent, indivisible, and then the conditional reasoning.
The text has some OCR uncertainties; focus on the logical structure.
2
Analyze the indivisibility proofs
The passage gives two reasons for indivisibility: because it is the extremity of a line, and because the contact point of a sphere is indivisible. Understand these geometric intuitions.
3
Understand the conditional conclusion
The argument then branches: if the point is a substance, it is the atom; if an accident, either its substratum is divisible (leading to undesirable division) or not divisible (leading to the desired indivisible substratum).
The desired conclusion is that an indivisible entity exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا وجوه [؟] . الأول : أن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودى ، ولأن الخط يو[؟] اس بها خطا آخر وما به نماس الشيء غيره لا يكون عدما محنا[؟] ، وهى غير منقسمة بالاتفاق ولأنها طرف الخط فلو كانت منقسمة لكان طرف الخط أحد نصفيها فلا يكون الطرف طرفا ، ولأن موضع الملاقات من الكرة الحقيقية المماسة للسطح المستوى الحقيقى غير منقسم وإلا لكان المنطبق منها على المستوى مستويا فكانت السكرة مضلعة ، ثم هذه النقطة إن كانت متحيزة ثابت الجوهر الفرد وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان من[؟] ما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو المطلوب (٢) (٢) أقول : قوله إن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودى ، ثم قوله وهى غير منقسمة بالاتفاق مناقض لقوله نهاية الشيء عدمه فلا يكون وجوديا ، والنقطة عند من يقول بها نهاية الخط فإذا هذا اتفاق من غير تراضى الخصمين، ولو قال بدل ذلك باعتراف القائلين به لكان أصوب . قوله : وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها أيضا غير مسلم عند مخالفيه فإنهم يقسمون الأعراض إلى السارية فى محالها وإلى غير السارية ، ويعدون النقطة فى غير السارية ويقولون إن غير السارية لا يجب انقسامها بانقسام محالها وملاقاة الكرة الحقيقية للسطح الجعلى المستوى يكون عندهم بنقطة هي طرف قطر تمر بمركز الكرة بموضع التماس ، وإلا فإذا ماست السكرة سطحا آخر مستويا لطرف الآخر من ذلك القطر ومرت دائرة عظيمة بقطر التماس انقسمت تلك الدائر بسبب التماسين إلى أربع قسى اثنان متماسان للسطح واثنان غير متماسين ، ويلزم من ذلك انطباق القوس على السطح المستقيم وذلك محال ، وكون التماس بنقطة وانقسام محل النقطة لا يوجب كون النقطة منقسمة على ما مر
We have aspects [؟]. First: That the point (nqta) is, by agreement, an existent entity, and because the line is contiguous with it by another line, and that by which something touches another is not pure non-existence. And it is indivisible by agreement, and because it is the extremity of a line, for if it were divisible, the extremity of the line would be one of its halves, so the extremity would not be an extremity. And because the locus of contact of a true sphere touching a true plane surface is indivisible, otherwise the part of it in contact with the plane would be planar, so the sphere would be polygonal. Then this point, if it is extended (mutahayyiz), it confirms the indivisible substance (jawhar fard); if it is an accident (arad), then in its substratum (mahall), if its substratum is divisible, then its division follows from the division of its substratum; if the substratum is not divisible, then that is the desired conclusion. (2) I say: his statement that the point is by agreement an existent entity and then his statement that it is indivisible by agreement contradicts his statement that the extremity of a thing is its non-existence, so it would not be existent. And the point, according to those who affirm it, is the extremity of a line, so this is an 'agreement' without satisfaction of the opponents. If he had said instead 'by acknowledgment of those who affirm it', it would be more correct. His statement: 'if it is an accident in its substratum, if the substratum is divisible, then its division follows from the division of its substratum' is also not granted by his opponents, for they divide accidents into those that permeate their substrata and those that do not, and they count the point among those that do not permeate, and they say that for non-permeating accidents, their division is not necessitated by the division of their substrata. And the contact of a true sphere with a plane artificial surface is, according to them, by a point which is the extremity of a diameter passing through the center of the sphere at the point of contact. Otherwise, if the sphere touched another plane surface at the other extremity of that diameter, and a great circle was drawn on the sphere through the diameter of contact, that circle would be divided by the two contacts into four arcs, two contiguous with the surface and two not contiguous, and from that would follow the correspondence of an arc with a straight line, which is impossible. And the fact that the contact is by a point and the division of the locus of the point does not necessitate the point being divisible, as has been mentioned.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the indivisible atom using the concept of the point. It argues: 1) The point is existent (by agreement) and indivisible because it is the extremity of a line and the contact point of a sphere with a plane. 2) If the point is a substance (mutahayyiz), it is the indivisible atom; if it is an accident, then either its substratum is divisible (so the point divides with it) or not divisible (so the point is indivisible, which is the desired conclusion). A marginal note by an editor critiques the claim of 'agreement', noting that the point as an extremity is considered non-existent by some, and that opponents do not accept that accidents necessarily divide with their substratum if they are non-permeating. The note also provides an alternative geometrical argument about sphere-plane contact.

Key Terms
نقطة
جوهر فرد
عرض
محل
متحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The point is necessarily a substance.
Correction: The argument allows that the point may be an accident; the conclusion still holds that an indivisible substratum exists.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what conclusion follows if the point is an accident and its substratum is indivisible?
A) The point is divisible
B) The point is also indivisible
C) The substratum must be a substance
D) The point does not exist

Key Takeaway
The point argument uses geometrical reasoning to show that an indivisible entity must exist, either as the point itself (if substance) or as its substratum (if accident).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument uses geometry to prove the existence of indivisible atoms. Have you ever considered how rational proofs can strengthen your conviction in unseen realities of the faith? Will you commit to seeking such proofs for other articles of belief, rather than relying solely on blind acceptance?
?
The point is indivisible; no matter how you try, you cannot break it. Your own existence is composed of countless such indivisible atoms. Does this knowledge make you reflect on the power of the Creator who holds together these atoms every moment? How will you acknowledge His control over your very substance today?

Editor’s Critique: The Point and Non-Permeating Accidents
Editor's critique of the point argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's critique of the point argument, focusing on the distinction between permeating and non-permeating accidents.

Core Idea
The point argument fails because opponents treat the point as a non-permeating accident, which does not divide with its substratum.

In this marginal note, the editor (likely the author of the commentary) challenges the validity of the first argument for the indivisible substance. He points out two flaws: the false claim of agreement on the point's existence, and the assumption that all accidents divide with their substrata. He introduces the concept of non-permeating accidents (ghayr al-sariya) and provides a geometrical counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's critique
Read the editor's response to the argument. Notice how he refutes the claim that the point's existence is agreed upon, and then challenges the assumption about accidents.
The critique is thorough and uses both philosophical and geometrical reasoning.
2
Understand the distinction of accidents
The editor distinguishes between permeating (sariya) and non-permeating (ghayr sariya) accidents. Permeating accidents fill their substratum, so they divide with it; non-permeating ones only occupy a part, so they do not necessarily divide.
The point is considered a non-permeating accident by opponents.
3
Follow the geometrical counterexample
The editor describes a sphere touching two parallel planes at opposite points. This setup shows that the contact point's locus (the point of tangency) can be divided into arcs without the point itself dividing, contradicting the original argument.
This is a sophisticated rebuttal using geometry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا وجوه [؟] . الأول : أن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودى ، ولأن الخط يو[؟] اس بها خطا آخر وما به نماس الشيء غيره لا يكون عدما محنا[؟] ، وهى غير منقسمة بالاتفاق ولأنها طرف الخط فلو كانت منقسمة لكان طرف الخط أحد نصفيها فلا يكون الطرف طرفا ، ولأن موضع الملاقات من الكرة الحقيقية المماسة للسطح المستوى الحقيقى غير منقسم وإلا لكان المنطبق منها على المستوى مستويا فكانت السكرة مضلعة ، ثم هذه النقطة إن كانت متحيزة ثابت الجوهر الفرد وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان من[؟] ما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها وإن لم يكن منقسما فهو المطلوب (٢) (٢) أقول : قوله إن النقطة بالاتفاق أمر وجودى ، ثم قوله وهى غير منقسمة بالاتفاق مناقض لقوله نهاية الشيء عدمه فلا يكون وجوديا ، والنقطة عند من يقول بها نهاية الخط فإذا هذا اتفاق من غير تراضى الخصمين، ولو قال بدل ذلك باعتراف القائلين به لكان أصوب . قوله : وإن كانت عرضا في حلها إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامها بانقسام محلها أيضا غير مسلم عند مخالفيه فإنهم يقسمون الأعراض إلى السارية فى محالها وإلى غير السارية ، ويعدون النقطة فى غير السارية ويقولون إن غير السارية لا يجب انقسامها بانقسام محالها وملاقاة الكرة الحقيقية للسطح الجعلى المستوى يكون عندهم بنقطة هي طرف قطر تمر بمركز الكرة بموضع التماس ، وإلا فإذا ماست السكرة سطحا آخر مستويا لطرف الآخر من ذلك القطر ومرت دائرة عظيمة بقطر التماس انقسمت تلك الدائر بسبب التماسين إلى أربع قسى اثنان متماسان للسطح واثنان غير متماسين ، ويلزم من ذلك انطباق القوس على السطح المستقيم وذلك محال ، وكون التماس بنقطة وانقسام محل النقطة لا يوجب كون النقطة منقسمة على ما مر
We have aspects [؟]. First: That the point (nqta) is, by agreement, an existent entity, and because the line is contiguous with it by another line, and that by which something touches another is not pure non-existence. And it is indivisible by agreement, and because it is the extremity of a line, for if it were divisible, the extremity of the line would be one of its halves, so the extremity would not be an extremity. And because the locus of contact of a true sphere touching a true plane surface is indivisible, otherwise the part of it in contact with the plane would be planar, so the sphere would be polygonal. (2) I say: his statement that the point is by agreement an existent entity and then his statement that it is indivisible by agreement contradicts his statement that the extremity of a thing is its non-existence, so it would not be existent. And the fact that the contact is by a point and the division of the locus of the point does not necessitate the point being divisible, as has been mentioned.

This passage presents the first argument for the indivisible atom using the concept of the point. ?

?

In your own journey of learning, where have you resisted using tangible examples to understand complex matters? Are you willing to humble yourself and start with the concrete before leaping to abstract conclusions?

Second Proof from Motion for the Indivisible Substance
Argument from motion for atomic substance with refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion in the present is used to argue for indivisible atoms, and why opponents reject that premise.

Core Idea
Motion’s present existence is the key premise; denying it collapses the proof.

This passage presents the second kalam argument for the indivisible substance, based on motion. It asserts that motion must exist in a present moment, which must be indivisible, leading to atomic parts. However, a marginal note refutes the core premise, claiming that motion cannot exist in a present instant, which is merely a boundary between past and future.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the passage from 'الثاني : أن الحركة لها وجود فى الحاضر' to 'فهو الجوهر الفرد'. Follow the logical steps: existence in present, indivisibility, composition, distance.
Note the use of conditional reasoning.
2
Examine the refutation
Read the marginal note starting from 'أقول: مخلفه يقول'. Understand the opponent's view that the present is not a time and cannot contain motion.
The refutation targets the first premise.
3
Compare both positions
Identify the key point of disagreement: Is the 'present' a time that can contain motion? The commentary will help evaluate the strength of each side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الحركة لها وجود فى الحاضر وإلا لم تكن ماضية ولا مستقبلة ، لأن الماضي هو الذي كان موجودا في زمان حاضر ، والمستقبل هو الذى يتوقع صيرورته كذلك وما يمتنع حضوره لا يصير ماضيا ولا مستقبلا ، ثم ذلك الحاضر عن منقسم وإلا لكان بعض أجزائه قبل البعض ، فعند حضور أحد المصفين لا يكون الصف الآخر موجودا فلا يكون الموجود موجودا هذا خلف فإذا الجزء الحاضر من الحركة غير منقسم وعند قيامه يحصل جزء آخر غير منقسم ، فالحركة مركبة من أمور كل واحد منها قابل للقسمة ، ثم نقول القدر المقطوع من المسافة اكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء التى لا تنجزى إن كان منقسما كانت الحركة إلى نصفه نصف تلك الحركه فتلك الحركة منقسمة هذا خلف ، وإن لم يك منقسما فهو الجوهر (۱) الفرد (۱) أقول: مخلفه يقول الحركة لا وجود لها إلا في الماضى أو في المستقبل، وأما الحال فهو نهاية الماضى وبداية المستقبل وليس بزمان وما ليس بزمان لا يكون فيه حركة لأن كل حركة فى رمان ، وكذلك ستر الفصول المشتركة للقادران جزء ليست بأجزاء لها إذ لو كانت الفصول المشتركة أجزاء المقادير التي هي فصولها لكانت القسمة إلى قسمين إلى ثلاثة أقسام قسمة إلى خمسة أقسام هذا خلف، فإذا الحاضر ليس بحركة ، وهو أدهى أنه هو الحركة وبنى عليه بيانه ، والمخالف لا يسلم أن الماضى من الحركة كان موجوداً إلى أن صار حاضراً إنما يقول هو الذي كان بعضه بالقياس إلى أن الحال مستقبلا وقبل بعضه ماضيا وصار في الحال كله ماضي او هكذا في المستقبل وفي الآن الفاصل بين الماضى والمستقبل لا يمكن أن يتحرك فإن الحركة إنما تقع في زمان وليس شيء من الزمان محاضر لأنه غير قار الذات .
Second: That motion (haraka) has existence in the present (hadir), otherwise it would not be past or future. For the past is that which existed in a present time, and the future is that which is expected to become so. What cannot be present does not become past or future. Then that present is indivisible, otherwise some of its parts would be before others, so when one half is present, the other half would not exist, so the existent would not be existent – a contradiction. Thus the present part of motion is indivisible. When it occurs, another indivisible part occurs, so motion is composed of things each of which is divisible (?) Then we say: the measured distance, each one of those indivisible parts, if it is divisible, then motion to its half would be half that motion, so that motion is divisible – contradiction; if it is not divisible, then it is the indivisible substance (jawhar fard). (1) I say: His opponent says that motion has existence only in the past or future. As for the present, it is the end of the past and beginning of the future and is not a time, and what is not a time has no motion, because every motion is in time. Similarly, the common sections for the ... are not parts thereof, for if the common sections were parts of the quantities which are their sections, then division into two, into three, into five would be a contradiction. Thus the present is not motion. And it is even more problematic that he makes it the motion and builds his demonstration on it. The opponent does not concede that the past of motion existed until it became present; rather he says that it was that some of it relative to the present was future, and some of it before was past, and in the present it all becomes past, and in the future [similar], and in the instant separating past and future, motion cannot occur, for motion only occurs in time, and no part of time is present because it is non-permanent (ghayr qar al-dhat).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage gives the second argument for the indivisible substance from motion. It asserts that motion must have a present existence, and that present must be indivisible; otherwise, parts of the present would precede each other, leading to a contradiction. Thus the present part of motion is indivisible, and motion is composed of such indivisible units. Next, the distance traversed is measured in these indivisible parts: if such a part were divisible, then motion over half of it would be half the motion, making the motion divisible (contradiction); if not divisible, it is the indivisible substance. A marginal note refutes the premise: the opponent denies that motion exists in the present, arguing that the present is not a time (zaman) but a boundary, and motion cannot occur in a non-time. The note further explains that the opponent views motion as spread over past and future, with the present instant being motionless.

Key Terms
حركة
حاضر
زمان
جوهر فرد
الآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes motion exists in a present instant, but opponents deny any motion in the present, which is only a boundary.
Correction: The present instant (al-ān) is not a duration of time (zaman) but a boundary between past and future; motion requires a timespan, so no motion occurs in an instant.

Checkpoint
According to the marginal note, why is the motion argument invalid?
A) Motion is continuous, not atomic
B) The present is not a time and cannot contain motion
C) The distance is infinite
D) Motion exists only in the future

Key Takeaway
The second argument for atomic substance hinges on motion's presence; the opponent's denial of motion in the present instant renders the argument unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the present moment contains no motion, as the opponent argues, then you are never actually ‘moving’ through life—only looking back at what has passed or anticipating what is to come. How often do you find yourself living in regret of the past or anxiety about the future instead of acting with presence? What is one decision you can make today that truly commits you to a future you desire, rather than being paralyzed by the instant?
?
The argument assumes that motion exists in the present, yet the opponent insists it does not. Your own sense of being ‘active’ right now—reading, thinking, existing—may be an illusion if the present is just a boundary. Does this make you question the reality of your everyday actions? How can you ensure that you are truly engaged in the present rather than merely existing between past and future?
?
The indivisible substance argument tries to break down motion into discrete units. Consider a habitual sin or a good deed you repeated: each instance was a discrete unit. Do you see your life as a series of indivisible choices? Which single choice today could be the indivisible unit that changes your trajectory toward Allah? Make that choice now.
