Proof by Traversal Against Infinite Parts
Proof against infinite parts via traversal and objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that if a body had infinite actual parts, traversing it would require infinite time, and the objection that parts are only potential.

Core Idea
Infinite parts make motion impossible because you can never reach the end.

Before debating whether the universe has infinite parts, theologians must first show that infinite parts lead to absurdity. This lesson presents a classic kalam proof: if a body had infinitely many actual parts, then to get from one end to the other, you would have to traverse an infinite number of halves, which is impossible. The objector responds that the parts are only potential, not actual. This sets the stage for the refutation of the claim that the divisible is one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the flow of the argument and objection.
2
Identify the proof structure
Notice how the argument uses a regress of halves: to reach the end you need to reach half, then quarter, etc., leading to infinite time.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the objection
The objector claims the argument only applies if parts are actual; they hold parts are only potential.
4
Recognize the author's response
The author counters that the claim of unity for what is divisible is false, which will be proven in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية لامتنع الوصول من أوله إلى آخره بالحركة إلا بعد الوصول إلى نصفه ولا متنع الوصول إلى نصفه إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعه ، فإذا كانت المفاصل غير متناهية وجب أن لا يصل المتحرك إلى آخر المسافة إلا فى زمان غير متناه وفساد اللازم فساد الملزوم . لا يقال : هذا إنما يلزم على من يقول الأجزاء التى لا نهاية لها حاصلة بالفعل، وخن لا نقول به بل الجسم عندنا واحد قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية . لانا نقول القول بوحدة ما يقبل القسمة باطل لوجوه . أحدها: أن وحدته إن كانت نفس الذات أو من لوازمها امتنعت إزالتها إلا عند عدم الذات وإن كان من العوارض الزائلة فهو محال ، لأن القائم بما يقبل الانقسام قابل الانقسام فالوحدة في نفسها قابلة الانقسام فإن قامت بها وحدة أخرى لوم التسلسل وإن لم تقم بها وحدة أخرى كانت تلك الوحده منقسمة بالفعل فالموصوف بها كذلك ، فالجسم منقسم بالفعل . وثانيهما: أنا إذا جعلنا الماء الواحد مائين فالماء ان الحاصلان إن قلنا إنهما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن أحدهما ما كان عين الذات فكان مغايرا له فالجزءان كانا موجودين بالفعل وإن قلنا إنهما ما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك كان ذلك إحداثا لهذين المنثين وإعداما للماء الأول وهو باطل بالبديهة . وثالثها : أن كل مره يمكن فرضه فى الجسم فهو موصوف بخاصية غير حاصلة في الجزء الآخر ، لأن مقطع موصوف بالنصفية ولا يتصف بها إلا مورد القسمة وكذا مقطع الثلث والربع ، وإذا كان لكل واحد من المقاطع الممكنة خاصة بالفعل وعندهم أن الاختصاص بالخواص المختلفة يوجب حصول الانقسام بالفعل لام
Third: If the body were composed of infinite parts, it would be impossible to reach from its beginning to its end by motion except after reaching its halfway, and impossible to reach its halfway except after reaching its quarter. So if the joints are infinite, the mover would not reach the end of the distance except in infinite time, and the corruption of the implication entails the corruption of the implied. It is not said: this only applies to those who say the infinite parts exist actually, and we do not say that; rather, the body according to us is one thing susceptible to infinite divisions. We say: the statement of the unity of what is divisible is false for several reasons. One of them: that its unity, if it is the essence itself or its necessary concomitants, its removal is impossible except with the non-existence of the essence; if it is among accidental attributes, it is impossible because what subsists in what is divisible is divisible, so unity itself is divisible. If it is subsisted by another unity, that leads to infinite regress; if it is not subsisted by another unity, then that unity is actually divided, so the described subject is likewise, so the body is actually divided. Second: if we make one water into two waters, the two resulting waters: if we say they existed before, then it is known necessarily that one of them was not the same essence, so it was other than it, so the two parts existed actually; if we say they did not exist before, that would be creating these two waters and annihilating the first water, which is self-evidently false. Third: every section (marra?) that can be assumed in the body is described by a property not present in the other part, because the cut (muqta') is described by half-ness (nisfiyya) and it is not so described except by the occurrence of division, similarly the cut of the third and quarter. And if each of the possible cuts has an actual property, and according to them, specification by different properties necessitates the actual occurrence of division... (incomplete)
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against infinite parts: traversing an infinite series of halves would require infinite time, which is impossible. Thus the implication (infinite parts) is false. The objector says this applies only to those who posit actual infinite parts; they hold the body is one and potentially infinitely divisible. The author refutes the claim that the divisible is one with three proofs: (1) Unity itself is either essential (immutable) or accidental. If accidental, it must be divisible as what subsists in a divisible thing; if it is divisible, then either it is supported by another unity (infinite regress) or not (actual division). (2) The example of dividing water: one water becomes two; if the two existed before, then parts existed actually; if not, then creation and annihilation occur, which is absurd. (3) Different cuts of the body have distinct properties (e.g., half-ness) that only exist after actual division, so those properties are actual, implying the parts are also actual. The text ends abruptly on page 119.

Three Proofs That the Divisible Cannot Be One
Three proofs refuting the unity of the divisible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three kalam arguments showing that anything divisible cannot be truly one, because unity itself would be divisible, the example of water implies actual parts, and different cuts have distinct properties.

Core Idea
Divisibility destroys unity: if something can be divided, it cannot be one in reality.

Having shown that infinite parts are impossible, the author now refutes the claim that the body is one and only potentially divisible. He offers three arguments: first, unity itself would be either essential (unchanging) or accidental; if accidental, it must be divisible since it inheres in a divisible subject, leading to regress or actual division. Second, the example of dividing water shows that the two resulting waters either pre-existed (so parts are actual) or were created from nothing (absurd). Third, different cuts (half, third, quarter) have distinct properties that can only exist after actual division, so parts are actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read each proof carefully in both Arabic and English to grasp the logic.
2
Analyze the first proof
Understand distinction between essential and accidental unity, and why accidental unity leads to regress or actual division.
3
Analyze the second proof
Consider the water example: if one water becomes two, the two either pre-existed (so parts are actual) or were created ex nihilo (absurd).
4
Analyze the third proof
Note that different cuts have unique properties like half-ness, which require actual division to exist; thus parts must be actual.
The text ends abruptly but the argument is clear.
5
Synthesize the refutation
See how all three proofs collectively show that the claim of unity for a divisible thing is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو تركب الجسم من أجزاء غير متناهية لامتنع الوصول من أوله إلى آخره بالحركة إلا بعد الوصول إلى نصفه ولا متنع الوصول إلى نصفه إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعه ، فإذا كانت المفاصل غير متناهية وجب أن لا يصل المتحرك إلى آخر المسافة إلا فى زمان غير متناه وفساد اللازم فساد الملزوم . لا يقال : هذا إنما يلزم على من يقول الأجزاء التى لا نهاية لها حاصلة بالفعل، وخن لا نقول به بل الجسم عندنا واحد قابل لانقسامات غير متناهية . لانا نقول القول بوحدة ما يقبل القسمة باطل لوجوه . أحدها: أن وحدته إن كانت نفس الذات أو من لوازمها امتنعت إزالتها إلا عند عدم الذات وإن كان من العوارض الزائلة فهو محال ، لأن القائم بما يقبل الانقسام قابل الانقسام فالوحدة في نفسها قابلة الانقسام فإن قامت بها وحدة أخرى لوم التسلسل وإن لم تقم بها وحدة أخرى كانت تلك الوحده منقسمة بالفعل فالموصوف بها كذلك ، فالجسم منقسم بالفعل . وثانيهما: أنا إذا جعلنا الماء الواحد مائين فالماء ان الحاصلان إن قلنا إنهما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك فمن المعلوم بالضرورة أن أحدهما ما كان عين الذات فكان مغايرا له فالجزءان كانا موجودين بالفعل وإن قلنا إنهما ما كانا موجودين قبل ذلك كان ذلك إحداثا لهذين المنثين وإعداما للماء الأول وهو باطل بالبديهة . وثالثها : أن كل مره يمكن فرضه فى الجسم فهو موصوف بخاصية غير حاصلة في الجزء الآخر ، لأن مقطع موصوف بالنصفية ولا يتصف بها إلا مورد القسمة وكذا مقطع الثلث والربع ، وإذا كان لكل واحد من المقاطع الممكنة خاصة بالفعل وعندهم أن الاختصاص بالخواص المختلفة يوجب حصول الانقسام بالفعل لام
Third: If the body were composed of infinite parts, it would be impossible to reach from its beginning to its end by motion except after reaching its halfway, and impossible to reach its halfway except after reaching its quarter. So if the joints are infinite, the mover would not reach the end of the distance except in infinite time, and the corruption of the implication entails the corruption of the implied. It is not said: this only applies to those who say the infinite parts exist actually, and we do not say that; rather, the body according to us is one thing susceptible to infinite divisions. We say: the statement of the unity of what is divisible is false for several reasons. One of them: that its unity, if it is the essence itself or its necessary concomitants, its removal is impossible except with the non-existence of the essence; if it is among accidental attributes, it is impossible because what subsists in what is divisible is divisible, so unity itself is divisible. If it is subsisted by another unity, that leads to infinite regress; if it is not subsisted by another unity, then that unity is actually divided, so the described subject is likewise, so the body is actually divided. Second: if we make one water into two waters, the two resulting waters: if we say they existed before, then it is known necessarily that one of them was not the same essence, so it was other than it, so the two parts existed actually; if we say they did not exist before, that would be creating these two waters and annihilating the first water, which is self-evidently false. Third: every section (marra?) that can be assumed in the body is described by a property not present in the other part, because the cut (muqta') is described by half-ness (nisfiyya) and it is not so described except by the occurrence of division, similarly the cut of the third and quarter. And if each of the possible cuts has an actual property, and according to them, specification by different properties necessitates the actual occurrence of division... (incomplete)

This passage presents the third argument against infinite parts: traversing an infinite series of halves would require infinite time, which is impossible. Thus the implication (infinite parts) is false. The objector says this applies only to those who posit actual infinite parts; they hold the body is one and potentially infinitely divisible. (2) The example of dividing water: one water becomes two; if the two existed before, then parts existed actually; if not, then creation and annihilation occur, which is absurd. (3) Different cuts of the body have distinct properties (e.g., half-ness) that only exist after actual division, so those properties are actual, implying the parts are also actual. The text ends abruptly on page 119.

Correction: These proofs show that potential divisibility leads to actual divisibility: unity itself would be divisible, the water example demonstrates actual parts, and properties of cuts require actual division.

Three Arguments for Atom Divisibility and Their Refutation
Razi presents three arguments for atom divisibility and gives a unified answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments Razi presents for the divisibility of the atom and his unified refutation based on the distinction between directional relations and essential division.

Core Idea
Directional differences do not imply essential division.

In this passage, Razi presents three sophisticated arguments that seem to show the atom must be divisible. After laying them out, he offers a single, elegant answer that distinguishes between having different directions (jihāt) and actually being divided in essence. This lesson walks through each argument and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp Razi's enumeration of the three arguments and the answer. Then read the translation to understand the logic.
Notice that each argument uses a different approach: faces, superimposed surfaces, and motion alignment.
2
Understand each argument's logic
Identify the key premise in each argument that seems to force divisibility. Ask yourself: what assumption is being made about the atom?
3
Examine the unified answer
Razi responds that all arguments only show diversity of directions, not division in the essence. Study the example of the circle's center.
The example is crucial: the center is a point, yet it aligns with all parts of the circumference.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation preserves the indivisibility of the atom. Why does directional diversity not require parts?
This is a key move in kalam atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أحدها : أن كل متحيز يفرض فإن الوجه الذى منه يلاقى ما على عينه غير الذى منه يلاقي بوجوه ما على يساره فيكون منقسما . وثانيها : أنا إذا ركبنا سطحا فوق آخر لا يتجزى ثم نظرنا رأينا أحد وجهيه دون الثاني والوجه المرئى غير الذي ليس بمرئى فيكون منقسما . وثالثها : أنا لو ركبنا خطا من ستة أجزاء ووضعنا فوق طرفه الأيمن جزءا وتحت طرفه الأيسر جزءا ثم تحركا إلى أن يصل كل واحد منهما إلى آخر المسافة فلابد أن يمر كل واحد منهما بالآخر ولا يمكن ذلك إلا بعد أن يتحاذيا وموضع التحاذى متصل الثالث والرابع ، وإذا وقع الجزء على ذلك الموضع فقد ماس لكل واحد من نصفيه نصف كل واحد منهما فيلزم التجزئة . الجواب أن ما ذكرتموه يدل على تغاير جهات الجزء وذلك لا يوجب القسمة في الذات، فإن مركز الدائرة يحاذى جملة أجزاء الدائرة مع أن المركز نقطة غير منقسمة
They argued: First: every supposed atomic individual (mutahayyiz) has a face from which it meets what is on its right, different from the face from which it meets what is on its left, so it is divisible. Second: if we superimpose one surface on another indivisible [atom], then look, we see one of its two faces without the second; the visible face is other than the non-visible, so it is divisible. Third: if we construct a line of six parts and place a part above its right end and a part below its left end, then they move until each reaches the end of the distance, necessarily each passes by the other, and that is only possible after they align; the place of alignment is connected to the third and fourth; when the part falls on that place, it has touched each of its halves to each half of each [of the other], entailing division. The answer: what you have mentioned indicates the diversity of the directions of the part (jihat al-juz'), but that does not necessitate division in the essence; for the center of a circle is aligned with all parts of the circle while the center is an indivisible point.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments for the divisibility of the atom (al-jawhar al-fard) and their refutation. The first argument: any atom that is supposed to have faces—the face that meets what is on its right is different from the face that meets what is on its left—thus it has distinct parts and is divisible. The second argument: if we place one surface (sath) on top of another indivisible atom, we see one face but not the other, so the atom has two distinct faces and is divisible. The third argument: a thought experiment with a line of six parts and two moving parts that must align—showing that at the moment of alignment, each part touches the other in two halves, implying division. The unified answer: these arguments only show that the atom has different directions (jihāt), not that its essence is divided; the center of a circle aligns with all parts of the circle yet is itself an indivisible point. Thus, relational differences do not necessitate intrinsic division.

Key Terms
متحيز
جسم
جزء
وجه
مركز
نقطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that different faces imply actual division.
Correction: Razi clarifies they indicate relational diversity (taghayyur jihāt), not essential partition (qisma fī al-dhāt).

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer, what do the three arguments actually prove about the atom?
A) That it is divisible into parts
B) That it has different directions (jihāt)
C) That it is composed of matter and form
D) That it is infinite

Key Takeaway
The three arguments only reveal that the atom has different directions, not that its essence is divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi’s answer shows that having different directions does not mean your essence is divided. In your own life, you may feel pulled in many directions—work, family, worship. Do you see these as signs that your heart is fragmented, or can you maintain a unified core intention (niyya) for Allah? Write one specific change you will make this week to align your varied tasks toward a single goal.
?
The opponents were convinced by external appearances (faces, alignment) that the atom must be divisible. Are there judgments you have made about others based only on their outward actions, assuming they are internally divided? Recall one person you have misjudged and ask Allah for forgiveness, then commit to seeing beyond surface differences.

Ibn Sina's Argument for Hylè and Form Composition
Ibn Sina argues that body is composed of hylè and form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's philosophical argument that the body is composed of hylè and form based on the denial of the indivisible atom.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina argues that the body's receptivity to separation proves it has a substrate (hylè) distinct from its form.

Ibn Sina, the great Islamic philosopher, proposed that every body is composed of two principles: hylè (prime matter) and form (determining attribute). This passage presents his reasoning, which draws on the idea that the body is one yet receptive to separation, implying a substrate that remains constant while form changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the passage and find where Ibn Sina states that the body is composed of hylè and form. Note his definition of taḥayyuz as the form's attribute and hylè as its locus.
2
Follow the argument
Notice that Ibn Sina argues based on the denial of the indivisible atom. He says the body is one in itself but receptive to separation, and whatever is receptive to something must exist with hylè. Then he argues that connection does not remain with separation, so what is receptive to separation is different from connection.
This step-by-step reasoning builds the case for two principles.
3
Understand the conclusion
Recognize that Ibn Sina concludes that the body must have two distinct principles: hylè (the substrate) and form (the connection). This sets up the basis for his hylè-form composition theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: زعم ابن سينا أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة ومعناه أن التحيز صفة حالة فى شىء. فالتحيز هو الصورة ومحله الهيولى ، واحتج عليه بناء على نفى الجوهر الفرد بأن الجسم فى نفسه واحد وهو قابل للانفصال ، والقابل للشىء موجود مع الهيولى لا محالة والاتصال لا يبقى مع الانفصال فالقابل للانفصال شيء مغاير للاتصال . جوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يقال الانفصال هو التعدد والاتصال هو الوحدة فالجسم إذا انفصل بعد اتصاله كان معناه أنه صار متعددا بعد أن كان واحدة ، فالطارى والزائل هو الوحدة والتعدد ، وهما عرضان والمورد هو الجسم
Issue: Ibn Sina claimed that the body is composed of hylè (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). Its meaning is that occupation of space (taḥayyuz) is an attribute subsisting in something. So occupation of space is the form, and its locus is hylè. He argued for this based on the denial of the indivisible atom: the body is in itself one and is receptive to separation (inqisām), and that which is receptive to something exists with hylè necessarily, and connection (ittiṣāl) does not remain with separation, so the receptive of separation is something other than connection. His answer: Why may it not be said that separation is multiplicity (ta‘addud) and connection is unity (waḥda)? So when the body separates after its connection, its meaning is that it became multiple after having been one. So the supervening and the ceasing are unity and multiplicity, and they are two accidents, and the substrate is the body.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's view that the body is composed of hylè (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra). Ibn Sina defines taḥayyuz (occupation of space) as an attribute inhering in something: the taḥayyuz is the form, and its locus is hylè. He argues based on the denial of the indivisible atom: the body is one in itself yet receptive to separation (inqisām). Whatever is receptive to something must exist with hylè (since hylè is the substrate of change). Also, connection does not remain with separation, so that which is receptive to separation must be distinct from connection (i.e., the form of connection). Therefore, the body has two principles: hylè (substrate) and form (determining attribute). Razi's answer: He challenges the presupposition. Why not say that separation is simply multiplicity (ta‘addud) and connection is unity (waḥda)? When a body separates after being connected, it becomes multiple after being one. Unity and multiplicity are accidents (a‘rāḍ) that come and go, while the underlying substrate (mawrid) is the body itself. Thus, there is no need to posit hylè and form as distinct principles; the body itself is the substrate that successively bears the accidents of unity and multiplicity.

Key Terms
الهيولى
الصورة
التحيز
الجوهر الفرد
الانفصال
الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hylè
C) Occupation of space (taḥayyuz)
D) Separation

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's argument for hylè and form rests on the idea that the body's receptivity to separation requires a substrate (hylè) distinct from its form (connection).

Razi's Refutation of Hylè-Form Composition
Razi refutes Ibn Sina's hylè-form composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes Ibn Sina's hylè-form theory by reinterpreting separation and connection as accidental unity and multiplicity inhering in the body itself.

Core Idea
Razi denies the need for hylè by arguing that separation and connection are mere accidents of unity and multiplicity applied to the body.

Razi, the Ash'ari theologian, responds to Ibn Sina's argument by questioning its fundamental premise. Instead of positing two distinct principles, he offers a simpler explanation: the body itself is the substrate that bears the accidents of unity and multiplicity. This answer preserves the Kalam view that bodies are simple substances created directly by Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer
Carefully read the passage where Razi begins with 'Why may it not be said...' and notice how he challenges the necessity of hylè.
2
Redefine the key terms
Razi reinterprets separation (inqisām) as multiplicity (ta‘addud) and connection (ittiṣāl) as unity (waḥda). Understand that these are not distinct principles but descriptions of the body's state.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Recognize that Razi concludes by calling unity and multiplicity accidents that come and go, with the body itself as the substrate. This eliminates the need for hylè and form as real components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina's argument for hylè and form is irrefutable.
Correction: Razi refutes it by reinterpreting separation and connection as accidents of unity and multiplicity, eliminating the need for hylè.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what are unity (waḥda) and multiplicity (ta‘addud)?
A) Two substances
B) The hylè and form
C) Two accidents (ʻaraḍān)
D) The body itself

Key Takeaway
Razi reduces Ibn Sina's hylè-form dichotomy to accidental change, affirming the body as a simple substance that directly bears accidents of unity and multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that what seems essential (connection/separation) is really accidental. When a relationship changes, do you react as if the person themselves have changed, or can you separate the accidental from the substance? This week, identify one change in a relationship and ask yourself: is this a change in accident or in substance?
?
The body remains constant while unity and multiplicity come and go. What is the constant in you that remains through your changing moods, failures, or successes? Are you more attached to your changing states or to your unchanging self that stands before Allah?
?
Razi challenges you to simplify your understanding: two principles reduced to one. Is there a habit or belief you have that you could simplify by redefining it? What excessive complexity might you be carrying that distracts you from the straight path?

Refutation of Body Composition from Accidents
Razi refutes the view that body is composed of color, taste, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi’s argument that the body is not composed of accidents, because the shared quiddity of taḥayyuz is distinct from differentiating qualities.

Core Idea
The body's essence is spatial occupation, not a bundle of accidents.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses a claim by Ḍirār and al-Najjār that the body’s quiddity is a composition of accidents like color, taste, and smell. Razi presents a simple but powerful argument: all bodies share the property of occupying space (taḥayyuz), yet they differ in their accidental qualities. From this he concludes that the body’s essence is distinct from those accidents. Let’s examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Read the first sentence to see what view Ḍirār and al-Najjār held about the body’s quiddity.
They claimed it is composed of color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness.
2
Understand the key observation
Notice that Razi points out all bodies share taḥayyuz but differ in their accidental qualities. This is the logical pivot of the argument.
If the body were composed of accidents, all bodies would share the same set, but they don’t.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Razi concludes that taḥayyuz is a distinct quiddity, not reducible to the accidents. Therefore, the body cannot be composed of them.
The shared element is the essence; the differing elements are accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: زعم ضرار والنجار أن ماهية الجسم مركبة من لون وطعم ورائحة وحرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة وهو باطل لأن المتحيزات متساوية فى ماهية التحيز ومتباينة بألوانها وروائحها وطعومها وما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز ، فالتحيز ماهية مغايرة لهذه الصفات
Issue: Ḍirār and al-Najjār claimed that the quiddity of the body is composed of color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. It is false because bodies that occupy space are equal in the quiddity of occupation of space (taḥayyuz) and differ by their colors, smells, and tastes; that by which they share is not that by which they differ. So occupation of space is a quiddity distinct from these attributes.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes the view of Ḍirār and al-Najjār that the body's essence (māhiyya) is composed of accidents like color, taste, smell, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Razi's argument: all bodies that occupy space (al-mutaḥayyizāt) are equal in the quiddity of taḥayyuz (occupation of space), but they differ in their colors, smells, etc. That by which they are common (i.e., taḥayyuz) is not that by which they are distinct (the accidents). Therefore, taḥayyuz is a quiddity separate from these attributes. Consequently, the body cannot be composed of those accidents, because the common element (taḥayyuz) is not reducible to them.

Key Terms
ماهية
تحيز
متحيز
لون
طعم
رائحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body’s essence is made up of accidents like color and taste.
Correction: Razi shows that taḥayyuz is common to all bodies, while accidents differ; thus the body’s quiddity is separate from these accidents.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main reason for rejecting that the body is composed of accidents like color and taste?
A) Bodies have no colors
B) Occupation of space is common while accidents differ
C) Accidents are indivisible
D) The body is a single substance

Key Takeaway
The quiddity of the body is occupation of space, not a collection of accidental qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the body’s quiddity is simply taḥayyuz, what does that imply about how we should treat others? Is there a specific person you have misjudged based on appearance? What action will you take today to correct that?
?
If all bodies share the same quiddity of spatial occupation, then at the level of essence we are no different from a rock or an animal. Yet we know humans have a rational soul. How does this lesson help you appreciate that your real worth lies in your soul and actions, not your physical accidents? What habit can you start to focus more on your inner reality than outer form?

Four Possibilities on Body Origination
Razi classifies the four possible views on body origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities regarding the origination or eternity of bodies as classified by Imam al-Razi, and recognize which view is held by the majority of religious communities.

Core Idea
Only four logical positions exist on whether bodies are originated or eternal.

Razi begins a new section on accidents by addressing a fundamental disagreement among theologians: are bodies originated or eternal? He shows that regardless of one's position, the possibilities are logically limited to just four combinations of essence and attributes. This classification frames the entire debate on creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Razi introduces the issue and then enumerates four possibilities.
The passage begins with 'The second consideration concerning accidents.' This sets the context.
2
Identify the four possibilities
Find the four statements that follow 'either it is...'. Each one pairs a status of essence and attributes.
The possibilities are: (1) both originated, (2) both eternal, (3) eternal essence/originated attributes, (4) originated essence/eternal attributes.
3
Understand why only four
Recognize that there are exactly four combinations because two binary categories (essence and attributes) each have two states (eternal or originated). This exhausts the logical possibilities.
4
Note the historical supporters
Razi points out that the first view (both originated) is held by the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Zoroastrians. This indicates the wide acceptance of this position across religious traditions.
Consider why these groups might adopt this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال النظر الثانى فى العوارض مسألة: اختلف أهل العالم في حدوث الأجسام والوجوه الممكنة فيه لا تزيد على أربعة . فإنه إما أن يكون محدث الذات والصفات . أو قديم الذات والصفات . أو قديم الذات محدث الصفات . أو بالعكس . أما الأول : فهو قول الجمهور من المسلمين والنصارى واليهود والمجوس
He said: The second consideration concerning accidents. Issue: The people of the world disagreed on the origination (ḥudūth) of bodies. The possible aspects therein do not exceed four: either it is originated in essence and attributes, or eternal in essence and attributes, or eternal in essence and originated in attributes, or the opposite. As for the first, it is the view of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Zoroastrians.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage begins a new section: 'The second consideration concerning accidents' (al-naẓar al-thānī fī al-ʿawāriḍ). It then states that people have disagreed about whether bodies are originated (ḥādith) or eternal (qadīm). Razi logically divides the possible positions into exactly four: (1) body is originated in both essence (dhāt) and attributes (ṣifāt); (2) eternal in both; (3) eternal in essence but originated in attributes; (4) the reverse (originated in essence but eternal in attributes). He then notes that the first position (both originated) is the view of the majority of Muslims, Christians, Jews, and Zoroastrians. This classification sets up the subsequent discussion of arguments for each view.

Key Terms

عوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there are many possible positions on whether bodies are originated or eternal, such as entirely different categories.
Correction: Razi shows that only four logical combinations are possible when considering essence and attributes, because each must be either eternal or originated. This exhausts all possibilities.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that all possible positions on the origination of bodies reduce to four logical combinations of essence and attributes, with the view that both are originated being the most widely held.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Razi's Answer: Disconnection and Connection as Accidents
Razi answers an objection about disconnection as multiplicity and connection as unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's answer to the objection that disconnection and connection are real attributes by showing they are accidents of unity and multiplicity, not essential properties.

Core Idea
Disconnection and connection are accidents of multiplicity and unity, not the body itself.

This passage presents Razi's concise answer to a kalām objection regarding the nature of disconnection and connection. Some had argued that if these are real attributes, they would have to be accidents, but the body is the substrate. Razi responds by affirming that they are indeed accidents—specifically, disconnection is multiplicity and connection is unity—and these are what come and go, while the body remains the substrate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translated version to grasp the flow.
Notice the rhetorical question format used by Razi.
2
Identify the objection being answered
The passage starts with 'His answer' implying a prior objection. Consider what that objection might be: that disconnection and connection are real attributes but need a substrate.
This objection likely comes from atomists or proponents of indivisible atoms.
3
Understand Razi's core claim
Razi states that disconnection is multiplicity and connection is unity. This redefines the attributes in terms of accidental states.
The body itself does not become a different substance; only its accidental qualities change.
4
Recognize that unity and multiplicity are accidents
The passage concludes that 'unity and multiplicity' are what come and go (al-ṭārī wa al-zā'il), and they are two accidents (ʿaraḍān), while the substrate (al-mawrid) is the body.
This preserves the body as a single substance throughout change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يقال الانفصال هو التعدد والانصال هو الوحدة فالجسم إذا انفصل بعد اتصاله كان معناه أنه صار متعددا بعد أن كان واحدة ، فالطارى والزائل هو الوحدة والتعـــــدد ، وهما عرضان والمورد هو الجسم
His answer: Why is it not permissible to say that disconnection is multiplicity and connection is unity? So the body when it becomes separated after its connection means that it became multiple after it was one. So what is incidental and passing is unity and multiplicity, and they are two accidents, and the substrate is the body.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection, likely from an atomist or a proponent of the indivisible atom theory. The objection may have argued that if disconnection and connection are real attributes, they would have to be accidents, but the body itself is the substrate. Razi responds by agreeing that disconnection and connection are indeed accidents of unity and multiplicity. He states that when a body becomes disconnected after being connected, it means it has become multiple after being one. The terms 'unity' and 'multiplicity' are what come and go (الطارى والزائل), so they are accidents (عرضان) while the body itself is the substrate (المورد). Thus, the answer clarifies that the change is in the accidental attributes, not in the substance of the body.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that disconnection and connection are essential properties of the body rather than accidents.
Correction: Razi shows they are accidents of unity and multiplicity, not essential to the body's substance.

Checkpoint

A) The essence of the body
B) Unity and multiplicity
C) The substrate only
D) The soul

Key Takeaway
Razi clarifies that disconnection and connection are accidents of multiplicity and unity, while the body remains the unchanging substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you define yourself by your changing states—like feeling connected or disconnected in your faith—rather than by your constant essence as a servant of Allah? What would change if you saw those states as mere accidents that do not alter your core identity before Him?

Razi on Hyle and Form Composition of Body
Razi comments on body composition from hyle and form, contrasting with kalam views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical claim that body is composed of hyle and form, and how Razi contextualizes this view against kalam atomism and spatial occupancy.

Core Idea
Body composition from hyle and form is a common philosophical doctrine, not exclusive to Ibn Sina.

Razi introduces a philosophical theory of body composition that contrasts with the mutakallimun's emphasis on spatial occupancy (tahayyuz). He explains that the recipient of qualities like connection and unity cannot itself be a body, leading philosophers to posit hyle and form. This lesson unpacks that argument and relates it to the atomist alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the corruptions, but focus on the main argument.
The text has OCR errors, so treat with caution.
2
Identify the two theories
The passage contrasts the philosophical hyle-form theory with the kalam atomist theory. Identify how each defines the substrate of body.
3
Analyze the argument about the recipient
Razi argues that if connection and disconnection are unity and multiplicity, then the recipient cannot be connected or disconnected. What does this imply for the nature of the substrate?
4
Connect to atomism
The passage ends by noting that on the atomist view, the recipient is the atom itself rather than hyle. How does this shift the ontology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول القول بأن الجسم مركب من الهبولى والصورة ليس مما ابتدعه ابن سينا ولا ما اختص به بل به جميع الفلاسفة والتحيز لا يقول به إلا بعض المتكلمين وموضعه المساهية بشرط الوجود وليس له إلى الصورة نسبة ولو كان الاتصال والانفصال هما الوحدة والتعدد لكان القابل لهما ليس متصل ولا ينفصل ولا بواحد ولا بمتعدد ، وكل ماهو جسم فإما متصل أو منفصل وإما واحد أو متعدد فإذا لاشي. ما هو قابل طما مجسم ، فقد سموا القابل بالهيولى ، والاتصال والوحدة هو الصوة ، هذا على تقدير في الجوهر الفرد أما على تقدير ثبوته فالقابل هو الجوهر ويعرض له التأليف فيصير جسما
(1) I say: The statement that the body is composed of hyle (الهبولى) and form (الصورة) is not something that Ibn Sina originated nor is it exclusive to him, but all philosophers (hold it). And spatial occupancy (التحيز) is not held except by some mutakallimun, and its locus is the 'مساهية' [?] under the condition of existence, and it has no relation to form. And if connection and disconnection are unity and multiplicity, then the recipient of them is neither connected nor disconnected, neither one nor multiple. And everything that is a body is either connected or disconnected, and either one or multiple. So [if that is the case] nothing that is a recipient of that is a body. So they named the recipient 'hyle', and connection and unity are the form. This is upon the assumption of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد). But upon the assumption of its [the atom's] existence, the recipient is the atom, and composition (التأليف) occurs to it, so it becomes a body.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a parenthetical comment (apparently a gloss or editorial note) that discusses the philosophical theory of body composition from hyle and form. The author (likely Razi or an editor) notes that this theory is not original to Ibn Sina but is held by all philosophers. He then contrasts it with the mutakallimun's concept of 'تحيز' (spatial occupancy), which is only held by some theologians. He argues that if connection and disconnection are interpreted as unity and multiplicity, then the recipient (القابل) of these attributes cannot itself be described as connected or disconnected, one or multiple; hence it cannot be a body. The philosophers call this recipient 'hyle', and they call connection and unity 'form'. The author then relates this to the theory of the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد): on that theory, the recipient is the atom, and composition (التأليف) occurs to it to form a body.

Correction: The passage states that it is held by all philosophers, not exclusive to Ibn Sina.

Checkpoint

A) The atom
B) The body
C) Hyle
D) Spatial occupancy

Key Takeaway
The hyle-form theory of body composition was a common philosophical doctrine, and its argument centers on the need for a non-corporeal recipient of the properties of connection and unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Refuting Touch and Separation as Existential Attributes
Imam Razi critiques the claim that touching or separating from another body is an existential attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three objections to the claim that a body's touching or separation from another body is an existential attribute.

Core Idea
Touching and separation are relational — and relations cannot be existent attributes.

Imam Razi encounters an argument that tries to classify the physical relation of touching as an existential attribute because it is the opposite of non-touching. He quickly dismantles this line of reasoning by pointing out that touching and separation are relations (iḍāfāt), and according to the opponent's own principles, relations have no external existence. In this passage, he also addresses a subtle point about the term 'maskan' to further undermine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Imam Razi's argumentation come through.
Notice the three separate 'Also' points.
2
Identify the three refutations
The passage presents three distinct arguments against the opponent. List them in your own words.
3
Understand the relational objection
Focus on the second argument: why does saying touching is a relation negate its existential status?
This is the core move.
4
Reflect on the 'maskan' point
Consider why the third argument about 'maskan' not being relational strengthens the refutation.
It shows the opponent's framework is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في الجواب: إن مماسة الجسم أو مباينته لجسم آخر وصف وجودي لأنه نقيض اللامماسة فنقول عليه قد مر الكلام على هذا التقرير وأيضا المماسة والمباينة إضافيتان وعندك لا شيء من الإضافات موجود وأيضا المسكون ليس إضافيا فلا يصح تفسيره بالإضافات.
And as for his statement in the answer: 'Indeed, the touching of a body or its separation from another body is an existential attribute because it is the opposite of non-touching,' we say against it: The discussion on this presentation has passed. Also, touching and separation are relations, and according to you, none of the relations exist. Also, the 'maskan' (the inhabited) is not relational, so it is not correct to interpret it by relations.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection where someone claimed that a body touching or separating from another body is an existential attribute because it is the opposite of non-touching (which would be non-existential). Imam Razi responds that this point has already been discussed. Moreover, touching and separation are relations (i.e., relative attributes). According to the opponent's own view, relations do not exist as real attributes. Therefore, they cannot be considered existential. Additionally, the term 'maskan' (perhaps meaning 'the place occupied' or 'the inhabited') is not relational, so interpreting it through relations is incorrect.

Correction: Imam Razi shows that the opposite of a non-existential (non-touching) need not be existential; in this case, touching is a relation, which is non-existent according to the opponent.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the claim that touching/separation is an existential attribute by showing they are relations, and relations are non-existent in the opponent's view.

First Proof for Similarity of Bodies and Its Objection
First proof for similarity of bodies with objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the similarity of bodies based on intermixing and the objection that it relies on incomplete observation.

Core Idea
Bodies must be similar because they intermix; an objection questions the scope of observation.

Imam al-Razi opens the discussion on the similarity of bodies by presenting the first of three proofs from his school, followed by an objection. This passage lays out a logical argument based on intermixing and then challenges its empirical basis. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping the broader debate on bodily similarity in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument and objection.
Note the structure: issue, proof, objection.
2
Analyze the proof's logic
Identify the premise: bodies, if equal in something, intermix. The inference: this intermixing only happens if bodies are similar. Conclusion: bodies are similar.
Consider why intermixing implies similarity.
3
Examine the objection's force
The objection says the proof requires observing all bodies intermixing, which is impossible. So the argument is mere conjecture. Ask yourself: Is this a strong objection? Why would the author present it without answer?
This objection is left unanswered here; see the next lesson for the reply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الأجسام بأسرها متماثلة خلافا للنظام. واحتج أصحابنا بثلاثة أوجه. أحدها: أن الأجسام بتقدير استوائها في الأمر [؟] تلبس بعضها ببعض، ولو لا تماثلها لما كان كذلك. الاعتراض: أن هذه الدلالة إنما تصح في حق من تصفح جميع الأجسام وشاهد التباس كل واحد منها بكل ماعداها، وأما قبل ذلك فليس إلا الرجم بالظن.
Issue: Bodies in their entirety are similar, contrary to al-Nazzam. And our companions adduced three proofs. The first: that bodies, by virtue of their equality in [something], intermix with each other, and were it not for their similarity, it would not be so. Objection: This indication is only valid for one who surveys all bodies and witnesses the intermingling of each one with all others; otherwise, it is nothing but conjecture.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs for the similarity (tamathul) of all bodies, attributed to the author's school (probably Ash'ari), against al-Nazzam who held that bodies are dissimilar. The proof argues that bodies, if they were equal in a certain respect, would intermix, and intermixing occurs only if they are similar. The objection counters that this reasoning presupposes a complete observation of all bodies and their mutual intermingling, which is impossible; thus the argument is based on guesswork. The author does not provide a reply here, but moves to the second proof.

Key Terms
تماثل
تماثل الأجسام
النظام
الاعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is immediately answered by the author.
Correction: The author presents the objection without a reply in this passage; the answer comes later in the text.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument of the first proof for the similarity of bodies?
A) Bodies are equal in their acceptance of accidents.
B) Bodies intermix due to their similarity, and if they were not similar they would not intermix.
C) All bodies are composed of the same substance.
D) Bodies are known to be similar through direct perception.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for similarity relies on intermixing, but the objection shows its empirical limitation: it requires observing all bodies, which is impossible.

?

?
The objection warns against conjecture (الظن). In your own life, where are you currently content with conjecture about matters of faith or practice? Will you commit to seeking out a surer basis, even if it requires effort?

Second Proof: Objection to Equal Accident Acceptance
Imam al-Razi presents the second proof for the similarity of bodies based on equal reception of all accidents, with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the similarity of bodies based on equal acceptance of accidents and the key objection that undermines its conclusion.

Core Idea
Equal accident-acceptance does not prove essential similarity.

Imam al-Razi presents a second argument for the similarity of all bodies: they all equally accept all accidents, so they share the same essence. However, he immediately subjects this argument to a critical objection. This lesson walks through the proof and the powerful counterargument that shows why equality in accidents does not imply equality in essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the proof, then the objection.
The objection is introduced with 'الاعتراض'.
2
Understand the proof
The proof claims that all bodies are equal in accepting all accidents, therefore they are equal in essence. Ask yourself: Is it true that all bodies accept all accidents?
3
Analyze the objection's points
The objection has three main points: (1) Not all bodies accept all accidents (fire, celestial sphere); (2) The story of Abraham is a particular case; (3) Even if equal in acceptance, that only shows commonality in properties, not essence.
Notice the distinction between lawazim (concomitants) and malzumat (substrata).
4
Reflect on the logical conclusion
The objection successfully shows that equal accident-acceptance does not prove essential similarity. Understand why this is a powerful critique.
This connects to broader debates about essence and accidents in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها بأسرها متساوية في قبول جميع الأعراض فتكون متساوية في الماهية. الاعتراض أنه لم يصح عندنا أن جرم النار قابل للكثافة الأرضية وأن جرم الفلك قابل للصفات المزاجية وقصة إبراهيم عليه السلام جزئية فلا تدل على الحكم الكلى، وأيضاً فلم لا يجوز أن يقال إن الله تعالى خلق في بدن إبراهيم كيفية عندها يستلذ مماسة النار كما فى النعامة، ثم بتقدير استواء الكل في قبول الأعراض فلا يلزم منه استوائها فى تمام الماهية لأن الاشتراك فى اللوازم لايدل على الاشتراك في الملزومات.
And the second: they are entirely equal in accepting all accidents, so they are equal in essence. Objection: It is not established for us that the fiery heavenly sphere is receptive to earthly density, nor that the celestial sphere is receptive to temperamental qualities, and the story of Abraham, peace be upon him, is particular so it does not indicate a universal judgment. Also, why may it not be said that God created in Abraham's body a quality by which he derives pleasure from touching fire, as in the ostrich? Then, given the equality of all in accepting accidents, it does not follow that they are equal in the complete essence, because commonality in concomitants does not indicate commonality in the substrata.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the similarity of bodies: all bodies equally accept all accidents, so they share the same essence (mahiyya). The objection denies the premise, citing that fire is not receptive to earth's density, and the celestial spheres are not receptive to mixed qualities. The story of Abraham (who was not burned) is a particular case, not a general rule. Furthermore, even if all bodies were equal in accident-acceptance, this only shows commonality in concomitants (lawazim), not in the substrata (malzumat) themselves. Thus the proof fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equal accident-acceptance implies essential similarity.
Correction: The objection shows that equal accident-acceptance only indicates similarity in properties (concomitants), not in essence (substrata).

Checkpoint
What is the main objection against the second proof for similarity of bodies?
A) Bodies are not equal in accepting all accidents.
B) The story of Abraham is a universal proof.
C) Equality in accidents proves equality in essence.
D) Accidents are not real properties.

Key Takeaway
Equality in accepting accidents does not prove equality in essence, as commonality in concomitants does not indicate commonality in substrata.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the logical flaw in the proof—that shared properties do not prove shared essence—remind you of the need to purify your inner intention (niyya) rather than rely on external actions alone?

Third Proof for Similarity: Bodies as Occupying Place
al-Razi presents the third proof and an objection distinguishing essence from attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for similarity of bodies based on being-in-place and the objection that distinguishes essence from attribute.

Core Idea
Being-in-place is a property, not the essence of body.

Imam al-Razi presents a third argument for the similarity of all bodies: they all share the property of occupying place. However, a critical objection follows: this property is not the essence of body but merely an attribute. This lesson examines the proof and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the argument and objection stand as presented.
2
Identify the proof and the objection
Identify the two parts: the proof (body defined by occupying place, so all bodies equal) and the objection (occupying place is a ruling, not essence).
Notice that the objection mirrors the one from the previous proof.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself why the distinction between essence and attribute is critical here. How does it invalidate the proof?
The objection relies on the principle that equality in concomitants does not prove equality in substrata.

?

?

In your spiritual life, are you mistaking outward actions (like prayer, charity, or religious routine) for the essence of faith? What might the true essence be, and how can you move beyond mere appearances to cultivate genuine inner conviction? Impossibility of Interpenetration and the Unity of Body's Definition
Al-Razi examines the impossibility of interpenetration and ties it to the unitary definition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the single definition of body to argue for its essential similarity and against interpenetration.

Core Idea
A unified definition of body proves its similarity and prevents interpenetration.

Imam al-Razi tackles two interrelated issues: whether bodies can interpenetrate and what defines the essence of body. He argues that the impossibility of interpenetration follows from the unity of the definition of body, which all groups accept in a single form, leading to the conclusion that bodies are essentially similar. This lesson unpacks that argument and the disagreements with al-Nazzam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get a feel for al-Razi's concise style.
Notice the abrupt shift from interpenetration to definition.
2
Identify the main claims
Find the two key claims: (1) interpenetration is impossible, (2) the definition of body is one and indicates similarity.
These are connected in al-Razi's mind.
3
Understand the role of definition
See how al-Razi uses the unity of definition to argue for similarity (tamathul) and against interpenetration.
The example of natural vs. mathematical body shows two aspects but one concept.
4
Examine al-Nazzam's objection
Al-Nazzam rejects similarity, claiming bodies differ in species based on properties. Understand his position.
He distinguishes species difference from conceptual difference.
5
Consider the additional reference
Taqi al-Din is mentioned as holding a similar view; note al-Razi's skepticism about the report.
This reinforces the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: التداخل محال في الاجسام خلافا للنظام. القسمة. أقول: الحد الدال على ماهية الجسم، على اختلاف الأقوال فيه واحد عند كل قوم بلا وقوع فيه، ولذلك اتفق الكل على تماثله، فإن المختلفات إذا اجتمعت في حد واحد وقع فيه التقسيم ضرورة كقولنا الجسم إما القابل للأبعاد أو المشتمل عليها ويراد بهما الطبيعى والتعليمى. والنظام يقول بتخالف هما التخالف خواصها يوجب تخالف الأنواع لا تخالف المفهوم فى الحدود، وذكروا أن تقى الدين العجال أيضاً ذهب إلى تخالف الاجسام وأنا ما رأيت في كلامه إلا ماقاله الجمهور.
Issue: Interpenetration is impossible in bodies, contrary to al-Nazzam. Division. I say: The definition indicating the essence of body, despite differences of opinion, is one for each group without discrepancy therein, therefore all agreed on its similarity. For when differing things meet in one definition, division necessarily occurs, as we say: body is either receptive to dimensions or encompassing them, meaning the natural and the mathematical. Al-Nazzam holds that they are different [types], that the difference of their properties necessitates difference of species, not difference of the concept in definitions. And they mentioned that Taqi al-Din al-'Ijal also held to the difference of bodies, but I have not seen in his words except what the majority said.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage tackles the issue of interpenetration (tadakhul) of bodies, which al-Nazzam supposedly allowed. Al-Razi first states that interpenetration is impossible. Then he moves to the definition of the essence of body, noting that despite varying opinions, each group uses a single definition without internal inconsistency, so all agree on the similarity (tamathul) of bodies. He gives an example: body is defined as 'receptive to dimensions' (physical body) or 'encompassing dimensions' (mathematical body). Al-Nazzam, however, holds that bodies are different in species due to differing properties, not in the conceptual definition. Al-Razi also mentions that Taqi al-Din al-'Ijal is said to hold the same view, but al-Razi claims he only saw the majority view from him. Thus the passage both refutes interpenetration and supports the similarity of bodies through definitional unity.

حد الجسم
ماهية الجسم
التماثل
الطبيعي
التعليمي
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of interpenetration is a standalone empirical claim.
Correction: Al-Razi connects it to the logical unity of definition and similarity of bodies.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi use the definition of body to argue for similarity?
A) He shows that different groups share one definition, implying a shared essence.
B) He argues that bodies have no definition.
C) He claims al-Nazzam's definition is contradictory.
D) He relies on sensory evidence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that the impossibility of interpenetration is grounded in the unified definition of body, which reveals the essential similarity of all bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat different objects as fundamentally different in essence, or do you recognize a shared reality of 'body'? How might al-Razi's argument challenge your perspective on the physical world?
?
Al-Nazzam was misled by differences in properties into thinking bodies belong to different species. Where in your own thinking do you confuse accidental differences with essential ones—perhaps in how you view people or objects around you?
?
If interpenetration were possible, the world would be chaotic and unpredictable. How does the impossibility of interpenetration reflect the order and wisdom of Allah's creation, and does this realization deepen your trust in His design?

Argument for Sameness and Its Refutation
Razi presents the second argument for the sameness of bodies and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sharing in accidents does not prove sameness of essence, and the logical principle that concomitants do not imply the essence.

Core Idea
Shared accidents do not prove shared essence.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a second argument for the sameness of all bodies, claiming that since all bodies equally receive accidents, they must be equal in essence. He then refutes this argument by showing that the premise is not accepted and that even if it were, shared concomitants do not imply shared essences. This introduces a key logical principle in kalam: the distinction between necessary concomitants and the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the first sentence: 'all bodies are equal in receiving all accidents, so they are equal in essence.' This is the claim to be refuted.
2
Understand the first objection
Razi objects that we do not accept that fire can receive earth density or that celestial spheres can receive temperamental qualities. This challenges the premise.
The story of Abraham is a particular case, not a universal rule.
3
Identify the logical principle
Even if equality in receiving accidents were granted, Razi argues that sharing in concomitants (lawāzim) does not prove sharing in essences (malzumāt). This is the core refutation.
Concomitants are necessary properties that follow from the essence but are not the essence itself.
4
Reflect on the ostrich analogy
Razi suggests that God could have created a special quality in Abraham's body to make him enjoy the fire, just as an ostrich enjoys hot sand. This shows that the accident of being unaffected by fire does not reveal the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها بأسرها متساوية في قبول جميع الأعراض فتكون متساوية في الماهية . الاعتراض أنه لم يصح عندنا أن جرم النار قابل للكثافة الأرضية وأن جرم الفلك قابل للصفات المزاجية وقصة إبراهيم عليه السلام جزئية فلا تدل على الحكم الكلى ، وأيضاً فلم لا يجوز أن يقال إن الله تعالى خلق في بدن إبراهيم كيفية عندها يستلذ مماسة النار كما فى النعامة ، ثم بتقدير استواء الكل في قبول الأعراض فلا يلزم منه استوائها فى تمام الماهية لأن الاشتراك فى اللوازم لا يدل على الاشتراك في الملزومات
And the second: all of them are equal in receiving all accidents, so they are equal in essence. The objection: we do not accept that the fire substance is receptive to earth density and that the celestial sphere substance is receptive to temperamental qualities, and the story of Abraham (peace be upon him) is particular so it does not indicate a universal judgment. Also, why is it not permissible to say that God created in Abraham's body a quality whereby he derives pleasure from touching fire, as in the ostrich? Then even if we grant the equality of all in receiving accidents, it does not necessitate their equality in complete essence, because sharing in concomitants does not indicate sharing in the things that have those concomitants.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the second argument for the sameness of bodies) and its refutation. The argument claims that all bodies are equal in their capacity to receive all accidents, therefore they are equal in essence (māhiyya). The refutation raises several points: first, the premise is not accepted—we do not agree that fire can receive earth density or that celestial spheres can receive temperamental qualities. Second, the story of Abraham is a particular case and cannot be generalized. Third, even if we grant the premise, shared concomitants (lawāzim) do not necessitate shared essences (malzumāt). This is a fundamental logical principle in kalam: accidental properties do not determine essential nature.

Key Terms
الأعراض
الماهية
اللوازم
الملزومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share an accident, they must share the same essence.
Correction: Sharing in accidents (which are concomitants) does not prove sharing in essence; essence is more fundamental than its accidental properties.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical flaw the objection points out in the second argument?
A) The argument assumes all bodies are equal in accidents.
B) The story of Abraham is not relevant.
C) Sharing in concomitants does not prove sharing in essences.
D) Fire cannot receive earth density.

Key Takeaway
Shared concomitants do not necessitate shared essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, are there areas where you confuse what a person does (accidents) with who they truly are (essence)? Identify one relationship where you may have made this mistake and commit to changing your view.

?
When you see someone who does not share your outward practices, do you still assume they share the same faith if they have the same core beliefs? Or conversely, do you judge their faith based on outward actions? How does the principle of this lesson challenge your tendency?

The Third Argument for the Sameness of Bodies and Its Refutation
Razi presents the third argument for the sameness of bodies and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equality in a common attribute (like occupying space) does not prove equality in essence, and how the distinction between essence and attribute is crucial in kalam.

Core Idea
Equality in a shared property does not imply equality in essence.

In this passage, Razi presents a third argument that attempts to prove all bodies are essentially the same because they all occupy space. He then refutes it by distinguishing between an essence and its attributes. Understanding this distinction is foundational to the kalam approach to analyzing objects and their properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the first half of the Arabic passage: the claim that body means 'that which occupies space' and that all bodies share this, so they are equal in essence.
Notice the logical form: if all bodies share a defining property, they are essentially the same.
2
Read the refutation
Read the objection: occupying space is not the essence but an attribute. Equality in attributes does not prove equality in essences.
This refutation relies on a prior principle about concomitants (lawāzim) and essences (malzumāt).
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this principle is important: it prevents false inferences from shared properties to shared identities.
This is a recurring theme in kalam: attributes do not define essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أن الجسم لا معنى له إلا الحاصل في الحيز والأجسام بأسرها متساوية فيه فتكون متساوية في الماهية والاعتراض أن الحصول فى الحيز ليس ذات الجسم بل حكما من أحكامها وقد ذكرنا أن التساوى في اللوازم لا يدل على التَساوي في الملزومات
And the third: that body has no meaning except as that which occupies space, and all bodies are equal in this, so they are equal in essence. The objection: that occupying space is not the essence of body but rather one of its attributes, and we have already stated that equality in concomitants does not indicate equality in the things that have those concomitants.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the sameness of bodies and its refutation. The argument defines body solely as that which occupies space (al-ḥāṣil fī al-ḥayyiz) and then claims that since all bodies share this property, they are equal in essence. The refutation counters that occupying space is not the essence (dhāt) of body but merely one of its attributes (ḥukm min aḥkāmihā). The earlier principle is reiterated: equality in concomitants (lawāzim) does not entail equality in the underlying essences (malzumāt). Thus, the argument fails to establish essential sameness.

اللوازم
الملزومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share a property, they must be essentially the same.
Correction: Sharing a property (like occupying space) does not mean they share the same essence. The property is merely a concomitant, not the essence itself.

Checkpoint

C) The argument does not mention accidents.
D) There is no refutation given.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essence and attribute is critical: equality in a shared property does not imply equality in essence.

The Impossibility of Interpenetration of Bodies
Razi presents the proof against interpenetration and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpenetration of bodies is impossible in Islamic theology, including the argument from similarity and the intuitive proof.

Core Idea
Two bodies cannot occupy one space; it's a rational necessity.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi addresses the controversial view of al-Nazzam, who claimed that bodies can interpenetrate. The argument from similarity is presented, but al-Razi notes its limitation against al-Nazzam. The decisive proof is the immediate judgment of the intellect that two bodies cannot share the same location. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the two-part structure: the claim/proof and the commentary.
2
Understand the similarity argument
The first part argues that interpenetration would eliminate distinctions among similar bodies, leading to unity. Grasp why this is supposed to be impossible.
This argument assumes bodies are similar, which al-Nazzam rejects.
3
Analyze the critique and intuitive proof
The commentator shows that the similarity argument fails against al-Nazzam because he does not accept similarity. The real proof is the intuitive intellect's judgment that two bodies cannot occupy one space. Also note the nuance about accidents.
The discussion on accidents shows that some interpenetration (points, lines) is controversially allowed, but not for bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : التداخل محال في الاجسام خلافا للنظام : لأنها متماثلة فلو تداخلت لارتفع الامتياز بالذات واللوازم والعوارض فيفضي إلى اتحاد الاثنين (1) أقول : لما التزم النظام القول بوجود الجواهر الفردة غير المتناهية في الجسم المتناهى لزمه القول بتداخل الجواهر والدليل الذي ذكره المصنف عام في الأجسام والأعراض ، والنظام لا يقول بتماثل الأجسام فلا يكون ذلك حجة عليه والمعتمد هو حكم بديهة العقل بأن الجسمين لا يجتمعان في حيز واحد، وأما في الأعراض في وضع نظر لأن القائلين بوجود الفصول المشتركة للكميات جوزوا اجتماع النقط في محل واحد مطلقا، واجتماع الخطوط لا في جهة الطول واجتماع السطوح لا في جهة الطول والعرض .
Issue: Interpenetration is impossible in bodies, contrary to al-Nazzam: because they are similar, so if they interpenetrated, distinction by essence, concomitants, and accidents would be removed, leading to the union of the two. (1) I say: Since al-Nazzam committed himself to the existence of infinite indivisible atoms in a finite body, he was forced to accept the interpenetration of atoms. The proof mentioned by the author is general [applicable] to bodies and accidents, but al-Nazzam does not affirm the similarity of bodies, so it is not a proof against him. The reliable [argument] is the judgment of the intuitive intellect that two bodies cannot occupy one space. As for accidents, there is room for consideration because those who affirm common differentiate for quantities allowed the meeting of points in one place absolutely, and the meeting of lines except in the dimension of length, and the meeting of surfaces except in the dimensions of length and breadth.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage addresses the mas'ala of interpenetration (al-tadākhul) of bodies, arguing it is impossible against al-Nazzam. The proof: since bodies are similar (mutamāthila), interpenetration would eliminate all distinctions (essence, concomitants, accidents), resulting in the unity of the two. The marginal note explains that al-Nazzam was forced to accept interpenetration because he held that finite bodies contain infinite atoms. However, the proof based on similarity does not apply to al-Nazzam because he does not accept the similarity of bodies. The commentator states that the real proof is the intuitive rational judgment that two bodies cannot occupy the same space. As for accidents (like points, lines, surfaces), some philosophers allowed certain types of interpenetration, but that is a separate discussion.

Key Terms

الامتياز بالذات

بديهة العقل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that if points can interpenetrate, then bodies can too.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between bodies and accidents; interpenetration of bodies is impossible by intuitive reason, while some philosophers allow interpenetration of points, lines, and surfaces in specific ways.

The impossibility of interpenetration of bodies is ultimately grounded in the immediate judgment of the intellect, not in a similarity-based proof.
