Proof That Bodies Are Visible: Length as Substance
Razi argues that length is a substance, proving bodies are visible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that length cannot be an accident because it would make the indivisible atom divisible, thereby establishing that bodies are inherently visible.

Core Idea
Length is a substance, not an accident — so visibility belongs to the body itself.

In this passage, Razi presents a key argument for the visibility of bodies that relies on the atomic structure of matter. He contends that if length were an accident, it would force the indivisible atom to become divisible. Therefore length must be a substance, and since we see length, we see the substance itself. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Start by reading the Arabic and translation. The claim is that bodies are visible, contrary to philosophers. Notice the proof begins with 'لنا أنا زى الطويل'.
The phrase 'أنا زى الطويل' is unclear; it may mean 'we see the long'.
2
Understand the atomic premise
Razi assumes 'body is composed of parts that are indivisible' (الأجزاء التي لا تجزأ). This is the atomic theory of the Mutakallimun. Grasp this premise before continuing.
3
Follow the conditional argument
If length were an accident, it would inhere in a single atom. That atom would then be longer than others, making it divisible — a contradiction. Therefore length is not an accident.
The conclusion 'وهو ال [؟]' may be 'and that is impossible'.
4
Conclude that length is a substance
Since length is not an accident, it must be a substance. And since we see length (long things), we see the substance itself, so bodies are visible intrinsically.
This step completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الأجسام مرئية خلافا للفلاسفة : لنا أنا زى الطويل والعريض والطول لا يكون عرضا لأنه ثبت كون الجسم مركبا من الأجزاء التي لا تجزأ ، فلو كان الطول عرضا لكان محله الجزء الواحد لاستحالة قيام العرض الواحد بأكثر من محلين فالجزء الموصوف بالطول يكون أكثر مقدارا مما ليس موصوفا فيكون الطويل قابلا للقسمة وهو ال [؟] . وإذا كان الطول الجوهر وطويل مرئى فيكون الجوهر مرتيا الاعتراض أنا تباعدنا على إثبات الجوهر الفرد ولكن لا نسلم أن الطول نفس الجوهر وإلا لمكان الجوهر الفرد طويلا فيعود الانتسام ، بل هو عبارة عن تألف الجواهر في سمت مخصوص والتأليف عرض ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المرئى هو التأليف. وأجيب عنه بأنا نوى الطويل حاصلا في الحيز وذلك يعقل في العرض فعلنا أن المربي هو الجوهر فشبه أن يكون ذلك كلاما غير الأول (۳) أقول : الفلاسفة لا ينكرون كون الأجسام مرئية بل إنما يقولون الأجسام مرئية بتوسط الألوان
Issue: Bodies are visible, contrary to the philosophers. Our evidence: we [see?] the long and wide, and length is not an accident, because it has been established that body is composed of indivisible parts. So if length were an accident, its substrate would be a single part, because it is impossible for one accident to inhere in more than two loci. Then the part described by length would be greater in magnitude than that which is not described, so the long thing would be divisible, and that is the [؟]. If length is a substance and the long thing is visible, then the substance is visible. Objection: We agree on the existence of the individual atom, but we do not accept that length is the same as the substance; otherwise the individual atom would be long and division would return. Rather, it is the composition of atoms in a specific direction, and composition is an accident, so why is it not permissible that the visible thing is the composition? Answer: We [see?] the long thing occupying space, and that is conceivable in an accident; so we say that the [؟] is the substance, and it is as if that is a different statement from the first. (3) I say: The philosophers do not deny that bodies are visible; rather, they say that bodies are visible through the mediation of colors.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala arguing that bodies are visible in themselves, contrary to philosophers who claim visibility is through colors. The author's proof: length cannot be an accident because body is composed of indivisible parts (atoms). If length were an accident inhering in a single atom, that atom would become divisible, which is impossible. Therefore length is a substance, and since the long body is visible, the substance itself is visible. The objection: the opponent agrees on atoms but denies that length is the substance itself; rather, length is the composition of atoms and composition is an accident. The visible could then be the composition. The answer: we perceive the long thing occupying space, and that is conceivable as an accident? (unclear). The marginal note clarifies that the philosophers do not deny visibility of bodies but attribute it to colors.

Key Terms
الطول
الجوهر الفرد
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Length is obviously an accident like heat or color.
Correction: Razi argues that length cannot be an accident because it would compromise the indivisibility of atoms, making it a substance.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, why cannot length be an accident?
A) Accidents cannot inhere in substances.
B) Length is essential to body.
C) It would make an indivisible atom divisible, a contradiction.
D) The philosophers argued against it.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that bodies are visible by demonstrating that length must be a substance rather than an accident, given the atomic structure of matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's proof depends on accepting that bodies are made of indivisible atoms. Do you hold any belief about Allah or the universe that rests on a premise you haven't examined? Are you willing to trace your faith back to its foundations, or do you prefer comfortable conclusions?
?
When faced with a contradiction (like an atom becoming divisible), Razi rejects the premise that caused it. Is there a contradiction in your life—a belief that leads to an impossible outcome—that you have ignored rather than corrected? Will you confront it today?
?
The argument makes the invisible substance known through visible length. In your spiritual life, are you ignoring the visible signs of Allah—in creation, in your circumstances, in your conscience—that point to what you cannot see? What will you do to start seeing them?

Objection and Answer: Composition as Accident and Philosophers' True Position
Razi presents an objection and answer, with a marginal note clarifying philosophers' stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that length is merely composition (an accident) and the answer that we perceive the long thing as occupying space, plus the clarification that philosophers attribute visibility to color mediation.

Core Idea
The objection reduces length to an accidental composition, but Razi maintains substance visibility; philosophers actually accept visibility through colors.

Having argued that length is a substance, Razi now addresses an objection: perhaps length is not the substance itself but the arrangement of atoms, which is an accident. The opponent suggests that what we see is the composition, not the body. Razi's answer is brief, but a marginal note clarifies the philosophers' actual stance—they do not deny visibility of bodies but say it requires color. Let's unpack this dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation for the objection and answer. Notice the objection agrees on atoms but reinterprets length as composition (accident).
The answer phrase 'أنا نوى الطويل' is unclear; perhaps 'we see the long'.
2
Understand the objection's core
The opponent says length is not identical to the substance; it is the composition (ta'lif) of atoms in a specific direction. Composition is an accident, so why cannot the visible thing be the composition?
3
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi replies: 'We [see?] the long thing occupying space, and that is conceivable in an accident; so we say that the [؟] is the substance.' The argument seems to claim that the perception of length as occupying space indicates it is not merely an accidental composition.
The text 'المربي' likely means 'the substance' or 'the visible thing'. The answer is cryptic.
4
Consider the marginal note
The note (3) says: 'The philosophers do not deny that bodies are visible; rather, they say that bodies are visible through the mediation of colors.' This shifts the debate: the disagreement is about the medium, not visibility itself.
This clarifies the true position of the philosophers.

The Permissibility of the Void (Al-Khala')
Razi defines the void and gives proofs for its permissibility against Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the void in Islamic kalam, the two proofs Razi gives for its permissibility, and his response to the Aristotelian objection.

Core Idea
The void is possible, and denying it leads to absurdities like overlap and circularity.

Razi begins by stating his position on the void, which is permissible according to him and many philosophers, contrary to Aristotle. He then clarifies the meaning of the void and provides arguments from physical reasoning. This passage demonstrates the kalam method of establishing a claim through rational proof, anticipating objections, and replying with analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
Read the passage and identify its main components: claim, definition, two proofs, objection, and answer.
This will help you follow the argumentative flow.
2
Analyze the first proof
Focus on the lifting page argument. Understand why the middle state must be empty at the first moment of separation.
Consider the logical steps: if the page rises at once, the middle cannot be occupied.
3
Analyze the second proof
Study the moving body argument. Follow the implications of rejecting the void: overlapping and circularity arise.
Notice the dependence of motion on place and the regress argument.
4
Examine the objection and reply
Read the Aristotelian objection about magnitude and Razi's answer using hypothetical estimation.
Contrast real vs. hypothetical estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الخلاء جائز عندنا وعند كثير من الفلاسفة خلافا لأرسطاط ليس وأنباعه. والمراد من الخلاء كون الجسمين بحيث لا يتماسان ولا يكون بينهما ما يماسانه. لنا إذا رفعنا صفحة عن مثلها ارتفع جميع جوانبها دفعة واحدة وإلا وقع الفكك فيها، وفي أول زمان الارتفاع حال وسطها، لأن حصول الجسم هناك لا يكون بعد مروره في الطرف فحال كونه في الطرف لم يك في الوسط فيكون الوسط خاليا، ولأن الجسم إذا انتقل من مكانه إلى مكان فالمكان المنتقل إليه إن كان خاليا قبل ذلك فقد حصل الغرض، وإن كان عملو ما فالذى كان فيه إن لم ينتقل عنه لزم التداخل، وإن انتقل عنه فإما أن ينتقل إلى مكان الجسم المنتقل إليه فيلزم منه الدور، ولأنه يتوقف حركة كل واحد منهما على مكانه على حركة الآخر عن مكانه أو إلى مكان آخر، والكلام فيه كمانى الأول فيلزم أن البقعة إذا تحركت أن يتدافع جملة كرة العالم وهو بالعمل. احتجوا: بأن الخلاء يحتمل التقدير فيكون مقدارا. جوابه: لا نسلم أنه يحتمل التقدير على سبيل التحقيق بل على سبيل التقدير، كما أنا نقول لو كان نصف قطر العالم ضعف ماهر الآن لكان ذلك المحيط واقعا سارج العالم، لكن لما كان ذلك على سبيل التقدير لم يلام ثروت مقدار خارج العالم، كذا ههنا.
Question: The void is permissible according to us and according to many of the philosophers, contrary to Aristotle and his followers. And what is meant by the void is that two bodies be such that they do not touch each other and there is nothing between them that touches them. For us: if we lift a page from its like, all its sides rise at once, otherwise there would be a breaking in it. And at the first moment of rising, there is a middle state, because the body's presence there is not after its passing through the side; so while it is at the side, it is not in the middle, so the middle is void. And because if a body moves from its place to another place, then if the place to which it moves was void before that, the purpose is achieved; and if it was occupied, then what was in it if it does not move from it, overlapping follows; and if it moves from it, then either it moves to the place of the body that moved to it, resulting in circularity; and because the motion of each one depends on its place moving from its place or to another place, and the discussion about it is like the first, so it would follow that if the spot moved, the entire sphere of the world would push against it, and that is absurd. They objected: that the void admits of estimation, so it is a magnitude. Its answer: We do not grant that it admits of estimation in reality, but only hypothetically, just as we say: if half the radius of the world were twice what it is now, then that circumference would be outside the world. But since that is hypothetical, it does not necessitate a magnitude outside the world. Likewise here.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a complete argument (mas'ala) defining the void and proving its possibility against Aristotle. Razi first defines the void as a state between two bodies where they do not touch and no other body touches them. Then he gives two proofs: the first involves lifting a page from another, arguing that at the instant of separation, the middle must be empty; the second involves a body moving from one place to another, leading to contradictions like overlap or circularity if void is denied. Then he presents an objection from the Aristotelians: the void would be a magnitude because it can be measured. Razi replies that estimation does not imply real magnitude, using the analogy of a hypothetical larger world radius that does not actually exist outside the world. The argument follows a standard kalam format: claim, proof, objection, answer.

Key Terms
الخلاء
مقدار
تقدير
تداخل
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The void is absolutely denied by all philosophers.
Correction: Razi shows that the void is accepted by many philosophers and only rejected by Aristotle and his followers.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'al-khala' (the void) according to Razi?
A) A place with no body at all.
B) Two bodies such that they do not touch and nothing touches them between them.
C) A body that is infinitely small.
D) The absence of any moving object.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the void must be permissible because otherwise physical motions would involve absurdities like overlap and circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that even though the void is nothing, it is necessary for motion and must be admitted. In your spiritual life, what 'voids' or empty spaces are you avoiding? Is there a silence, a solitude, or a lack of distraction that you fill with noise because it feels empty? This lesson shows that emptiness can be essential. Will you allow a void in your heart for God to fill, or will you keep it occupied with false comforts?
?
The philosophers denied the void because they thought it led to absurdities, but Razi turned their argument around. Are you willing to follow the logic wherever it leads, even if it means abandoning comfortable assumptions?
?
Razi uses a simple physical example—lifting a page—to prove a metaphysical truth. What small, tangible signs in your daily life might be pointing you to a deeper reality about God's existence or attributes? Will you look for those signs and let them change how you think and live?

Razi's Proof That Bodies Are Finite
Razi proves that bodies are finite using a geometric contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's geometric argument for the finitude of bodies and how it refutes the position of the Indians.

Core Idea
An infinite body would lead to logical contradiction, proving finitude.

This lesson examines Razi's concise proof that all bodies are finite. He uses a thought experiment involving infinite and finite parallel lines to show that an infinite body is impossible. This argument is a key tool in kalam to establish that the universe must have been created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the setup of the argument.
The text is truncated but the core idea is clear.
2
Understand the geometric setup
Razi imagines an infinite line and a finite line parallel to it. Consider what happens when the finite line deviates.
3
Identify the contradiction
The finite line would have to align with a point on the infinite line, but because the infinite line has no end, it leads to an infinite number of alignments—impossible.
This shows the impossibility of an actual infinite.
4
Apply to bodies
If bodies could be infinite, similar contradictions would arise. Therefore, bodies must be finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الاجسام متناهية خلافا للهند. لنا أنا إذا فرضنا خطا غير متناه وفرضنا خطا آخر متناهيا موازيا الأول، فأما إذا مال المنهي عن المرازاة أول نقطة المسامتة، لكن ذلك محال، إذ لا نقطة إلا وفوقها أخرى فتكون نقطة إلى المسامتة فلابد من هي
Question: Bodies are finite, contrary to the Indians. For us: if we assume an infinite line and assume another finite line parallel to the first, then when the finite line deviates from being opposite the first point of alignment, but that is impossible, for there is no point except that there is another above it, so there would be a point to alignment, so it must be that...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a brief mas'ala arguing that bodies are finite, against the Indian mathematicians (or philosophers) who held that the universe is infinite. Razi sets up a geometric proof: take an infinite line and a finite line parallel to it. The finite line must align with some point on the infinite line, but because the infinite line has no end, the finite line would have to meet an infinite number of points, leading to a contradiction. The proof is incomplete in the text, but the idea is that finitude follows from the impossibility of an infinite regress in distances. The reasoning anticipates classical kalam proofs for the finitude of the world.

Misconception: Some might think Indians were a religious sect; they were a philosophical school.
Correction: In Razi's context, 'Indians' refers to a group of thinkers who held that the universe is eternal and infinite.

D) No contradiction arises.

Key Takeaway
Razi's geometric argument shows that the concept of an actual infinite body leads to logical contradiction, thereby proving that all bodies are finite.

?

?
The contradiction in an infinite body shows that only Allah is without limit. Do you secretly wish for something infinite in your life—control, pleasure, or time? How can you redirect that desire toward the only One who is truly infinite?

The Debate on the Finitude of Bodies
Razi presents opponent's argument for infinite bodies and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument about whether bodies are finite or infinite, including the opponent's reasoning, the mutakallimun's answer, and the philosophers' stronger solution.

Core Idea
The debate on whether bodies are finite hinges on what lies outside the world.

This passage from Razi's Muhassal presents a classic kalam debate. The opponent argues that if bodies are finite, the space outside must be either distinguished or not, both leading to absurdity. Razi then presents two responses: the mutakallimun's weak answer of 'estimative spaces' and the philosophers' dismissal based on the unreliability of the estimative faculty. Let us follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the three main sections: the opponent's argument, the mutakallimun's answer, and the philosophers' answer.
The passage uses technical terms like musamata and ahiyaz.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent claims that if bodies are finite, what is outside all bodies must either have distinct sides or not. If distinct, it must be a body or magnitude, contradicting the assumption. If not distinct, it contradicts clear reason (north vs south). So bodies must be infinite.
3
Examine the mutakallimun's answer
The mutakallimun posit infinite 'estimative' spaces (ahiyaz taqdiriya) that exist only in the mind. Razi critiques this: if they don't correspond to reality, they are false assumptions; if they do, real spaces exist, reviving the problem.
This shows the weakness of relying on mental constructs without realist grounding.
4
Learn the philosophers' solution
The philosophers (hukama') deny any real distinction outside the world; the apparent distinction is a judgment of the estimative faculty (wahm), not reason, and is thus unacceptable in philosophical proof.
This is presented as the stronger answer because it avoids affirming infinite real spaces.
5
Summarize the debate
Review the three positions: opponent's argument for infinity, mutakallimun's weak infinite estimative spaces, philosophers' denial of distinction. Understand why Razi finds the philosophers' answer superior.
This debate illustrates key differences between mutakallimun and philosophers on ontology and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسامتة . مع الفوقانية قبل المسامنة مع التحتانية ، فلان فرض خط غير متناعية يفضى إلى هذا المحال احتج الخصم بأن الأجسام لو كانت متناعية لكان الخارج عنها بأسرها إما أن يتميز فيه جانب من جانب فيكون محالا وإما أن لا يتميز فإن تميز لم يكن ذلك عدما محضا لأن النفى المحض لا خصوصية فيه ولا تحقق فكيف يحصل الامتياز ، بل لابد وأن يكون أمرا وجوديا ولاشك فى أنه إما أن يكون مشاراً إليه فيكون مقدارا أو يكون جسمها فالخارج عن كل الأجسام جسم هذا خلف ، وإن لم يتميز جانب فيه عن جانب فهذا محال عن بداهة العقل ، لأن العقل الصريح يشهد بأن الطرف الذى يلى القطب اليمين غير الذي يلى القطب الشمالي ، ذلك مكابرة في البديريات . فإنكار الجواب أما المتكلمون فقد سلوا أحيارا متميزة خارج العالم غير متناهية ، وزعموا أنها أمور تقديرية غير موجودة وهذا ضعيف ، لأن المقدر هو الذي لا وجود له إلا في الذهن والذى لا وجود له إلا في الذهن إن لم يكن مطابقا للخارج كان ذلك فرضا كاذبا وإن كان مطابقا لزم منه وجود الأحياز في نفس الأمر وحينئذ يعود الالزام وأما الحكماء فإنهم أصروا على أن خارج العالم لا يتميز فيه جانب من جانب وأن الحاكم بهذا التميز هو الوهم لا العقل وحكم الوهم غير مقبول .
The parallelism (al-musamatah) [occurs] with the vertical direction before the opposition (al-musamana) with the horizontal direction. For if a line is assumed infinite, it leads to this absurdity. The opponent argued that if bodies were finite, then what is external to all of them would either have a distinction of one side from another, which is impossible, or have no distinction. If it is distinguished, then it is not pure nothingness, because pure negation has no particularity nor reality, so how can distinction occur? Rather, it must be something existent, and undoubtedly it is either something pointed to, so it is a magnitude, or it is a body. Thus what is external to all bodies is a body, which is a contradiction. And if it is not distinguished side from side, then this is impossible by the necessity of reason, because clear reason testifies that the side that faces the right pole is other than the side that faces the left pole; that [denial] is an obstinacy in obvious matters. The answer: as for the mutakallimun, they conceded distinct spaces (ahiyaz) outside the world that are infinite, and claimed that they are estimative matters (umur taqdiriya) that do not exist. But this is weak, because the estimative (al-muqaddar) is that which has no existence except in the mind, and that which has no existence except in the mind — if it does not correspond to the external, it is a false assumption; and if it does correspond, then it entails the existence of spaces in reality, and then the objection returns. As for the philosophers (al-hukama'), they insisted that outside the world there is no distinction of side from side, and that the judge of this distinction is the estimative faculty (al-wahm), not reason (al-'aql), and the judgment of the estimative faculty is unacceptable.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the finitude of bodies. It begins by mentioning a technical point about 'parallelism' (musamata) and 'opposition' (musamana), likely referring to geometric relations. The opponent argues that if bodies are finite, the space outside them must be either distinguished (which would make it a body or magnitude, contradicting the claim that there is nothing beyond bodies) or undifferentiated (which contradicts the clear intuition that the side facing the north pole differs from the side facing the south pole). The mutakallimun respond by positing infinite but 'estimative' (taqdiri) spaces — mental constructs with no real existence. Razi criticizes this: if estimative, they either misrepresent reality (false assumption) or correspond to reality (implying real spaces, which reinvokes the problem). The philosophers (hukama') solve the issue by denying any real distinction outside the world; they say the perceived distinction is a product of the estimative faculty (wahm), not reason, and such judgments are not acceptable in philosophical proof. Thus the passage shows three positions: the opponent's argument, the mutakallimun's weak answer, and the philosophers' strong dismissal.

Key Terms
musamata
ahiyaz
taqdiri
wahm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun's answer that spaces are merely estimative successfully avoids affirming infinite bodies.
Correction: Razi shows that if the estimative does not correspond to reality, it's a false assumption; if it does correspond, it implies real spaces, so the problem remains.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's main criticism of the mutakallimun's answer that the spaces outside the world are 'estimative' (taqdiri)?
A) Estimative spaces are impossible because they are infinite.
B) If estimative, they either contradict reason or entail real spaces, reviving the problem.
C) The philosophers have a better answer using the estimative faculty.
D) The mutakallimun forgot to consider the north and south poles.

Key Takeaway
Razi presents the debate on the finitude of bodies, showing that the mutakallimun's answer of estimative spaces is weak, while the philosophers' denial of real distinction outside the world avoids the problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine the space beyond the universe, do you naturally distinguish directions like up, down, left, and right? This passage says such distinctions are a product of your estimative faculty (wahm), not reason. In what other areas of your faith or worldview might you be relying on imagination rather than sound reason? What concrete step can you take this week to discipline your mind to follow clear rational argument over untutored intuition?
?
The mutakallimun tried to escape the opponent's argument by positing 'estimative spaces'—a weak answer that Razi criticizes. Are there beliefs you hold simply because they are comfortable or convenient, even though they don't stand up to scrutiny? Write down one such belief and the evidence against it. Then commit to seeking the truth, even if it is harder.
?
The philosophers here insist that reason ('aql) alone should judge, not wahm. Yet your daily life is full of quick judgments based on habit and emotion. Choose one area where you tend to follow your gut feeling over logical reasoning (e.g., about a person, a religious matter, a life decision). How can you intentionally train yourself to let reason lead, especially in matters of ultimate concern?

Classification of Bodies into Simple and Composite
Imam Razi classifies bodies into simple and composite, and simple into celestial and elemental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how bodies are classified in kalam into simple and composite, and the further division of simple bodies into celestial and elemental.

Core Idea
Bodies are either simple (homogenous) or composite (heterogeneous).

In kalam, understanding the nature of bodies is essential for later discussions about creation and the universe. Imam Razi begins by presenting a basic classification that distinguishes bodies based on the relationship between their parts and the whole. This foundational division prepares for deeper analysis of celestial and elemental bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the concise definitions and the logical flow from general to specific.
2
Identify the two key definitions
The passage defines 'simple' and 'composite' bodies. Understand the criterion: whether every part resembles the whole in complete essence.
3
Explore the subdivision
Simple bodies are further divided into celestial and elemental. Reflect on what these categories might entail.
This sets up the next topics in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الأجساد
الجسم إما أن يكون بسيطا وهو الذى يشابه كل واحد من أجزائه كله في تمام الماهية ، وإما مركب وهو الذي لا يكون كذالك ، أما البسيط فإما فلكي وإما عنصرى
Division of bodies. The body is either simple, which is that every one of its parts resembles the whole in complete essence, or composite, which is not like that. As for the simple, it is either celestial or elemental.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of bodies. It defines a simple body (بسيط) as one in which every part shares the complete essence of the whole, meaning the parts are homogenous in nature. A composite body (مركب) is one that is not like that, i.e., its parts differ in essence. Then it divides simple bodies into two types: celestial (فلكي) and elemental (عنصرى). This sets the stage for subsequent detailed discussion of each type.

The Logical Contradiction in Mixture Theory
Mutakallimun object to the philosophers' theory of mixture of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's logical objection to the philosophers' theory of mixture and how they refute a counter-argument.

Core Idea
A quality cannot be both broken and unbroken at the same time.

This passage presents a classic kalam critique of the philosopher's theory of mixture. The mutakallimun argue that if mixing involves each element's quality breaking the other, then simultaneous breaking results in a logical contradiction. They also consider and refute a possible counter-argument involving the form and quality distinction. This lesson will unpack their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Notice the dialectical structure: claim, objection, counter-objection, rebuttal.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical steps.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers claim that when elements mix, each element's overpowering quality (sūra) breaks the other, resulting in an intermediate quality called temperament (mizāj).
3
Analyze the mutakallimun's objection
The mutakallimun object using the principle that the cause must coexist with the effect. They pose two scenarios: if both breakings occur simultaneously, each quality is both broken and not broken (contradiction); if they occur separately, the effect exists without its cause (impossible).
The principle of causality is key.
4
Understand the rebuttal to the counter-objection
A potential objector says the breaker is the constitutive form, which remains unbroken, while the quality is broken. The mutakallimun respond that the form is broken via the supervening quality, so the problem recurs.
This reinforces their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المركبات فزعموا أن هذه العناصر إذا اختلطت انكسرت سورة كل واحد منها بكيفية الآخر فحصل كيفية متوسطة هي المزاج والمتكلمون قالوا العلة مقارنة للمعلول فإذا كان الكاسر سورة كل واحد منهما يكسره الآخر فإن حصل الانكساران دفعة واحدة لزم حصول الكاسرين في ذلك الزمان فيكون كل واحد من تلك الكيفيتين في ذلك الآن منكسرا وغير منكسر هذا خلف وإن لم يوجد معا فهو محال لأن المعلول يعود غالبا لا يقال الكاسر هو الصورة المقومة وهي باقية من غير انكسار والمنكسر هي الكيفية وهي قابلة للأشد والأضعف لأنا نقول الصورة إما تكسر بواسطة الكيفية الفائضة فيعود المحذور فهذا تمام القول في الجواهر الجسمانية
As for compounds: they claimed that when these elements mix, the overpowering force of each one is broken by the quality of the other, resulting in an intermediate quality which is the temperament (mizāj). The mutakallimun said: The cause is concomitant with the effect. So if the breaker is the overpowering force of each one and each breaks the other, then if the two breakings occur simultaneously, the two breakers must exist in that time, so each of those two qualities would be at that moment both broken and not broken—this is a contradiction. And if they do not exist together, it is impossible because the effect would return [without cause]—usually it does not return. One might say: The breaker is the constitutive form, which remains unbroken, and the broken is the quality, which is susceptible to intensity and weakness. But we say: The form is broken by means of the supervening quality, so the same problem returns. This completes the discussion on corporeal substances.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's objection to the philosophers' theory of mixture (mizāj). The philosophers claim that when elements mix, each element's overpowering quality breaks the other's, resulting in an intermediate quality. The mutakallimun object using the principle that the cause (al-ʻillah) must be concomitant with the effect (al-maʻlūl). If each quality both breaks and is broken, then if both breakings occur simultaneously, each quality would be both broken and not broken, a contradiction. If they occur at different times, then the effect (the breaking) would exist without its cause, which is impossible. A possible objection: the breaker is the constitutive form (ṣūrah), which remains unbroken, while the quality (kayfiyyah) is broken. But the mutakallimun respond that the form is broken via the supervening quality, so the same problem recurs. The passage ends by concluding the discussion on corporeal substances.

Key Terms
المركبات
المزاج
العلة
المعلول
الكيفية
الصورة
الجواهر الجسمانية
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mutakallimun deny any mixing of elements at all.
Correction: They do not deny mixing itself; they object to the specific mechanism of one quality breaking another, as it leads to logical contradiction.

Checkpoint

B) Because it leads to a contradiction in time
C) Because they believe only God creates qualities
D) Because they deny the existence of qualities

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's objection shows that the mechanism of simultaneous breaking leads to logical impossibility, and the distinction between form and quality does not resolve it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Arguments Against T'ayyun as a Positive Attribute
Razi enumerates three arguments against t'ayyun being a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments theologians use to deny that determination (t'ayyun) is a positive attribute and the logical problems (infinite regress, circularity, multiplication) they raise.

Core Idea
If t'ayyun were positive, it would create infinite regress, circles, or multiplication—so it cannot be real.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a key theological debate about whether the identity (t'ayyun) of a thing is a real positive attribute or merely a conceptual distinction. Theologians argue that if t'ayyun were a real attribute, it would lead to absurd consequences. They give three arguments, each showing a different logical problem: infinite regress, circularity, and the impossibility of one attribute subsisting in two loci. Let's examine these arguments closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and the first argument
Start by reading the opening question and the first argument: if t'ayyun were positive, each instance would require a further determination, leading to infinite regress.
Notice the phrase 'إلى غير النهاية' indicating infinite regress.
2
Identify the second argument about circularity
The second argument claims that combining t'ayyun with the essence after the essence exists is impossible, but the essence only exists after t'ayyun, creating a circle or double determination.
The term 'دور' means circular reasoning.
3
Understand the third argument about unity of existence
The third argument states that if t'ayyun is other than the essence, the existence of one cannot be the existence of the other, so a single thing would become two, leading to infinite multiplication.
The conclusion 'بل أمور غير متناهية' stresses the absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكونا متباينين بنفسيهما ثم المتكلمون أنكروا كون العين أمرا ثبوتيا واحتجوا بأمور الأول الله لو كان التعين أمر ثبوئيا لمكان مساويا اسائر التعينات فى الماهية المسماة بالتعين ويمتاز كل واحد منهما عن صاحبه بخصوصية فيلزم أن يكون للمنتمين تدين آخر إلى غير النهاية الثانى وهو أن التعين لو كان أمرا ثبوتيا لاستحال انضمامه إلى المساهية بعد وجود الماهية لكن المساهية إلا توجد إلا بعد التعين فإن كان هذا العين هو الأول لزم الدور وإن كان غيره كان الشيء الواحد معينا مرتين وهو محال الثالث: وهو أن التعين إذا كان أمرا مغايرا للماهية استحال أن يكون الوجود القائم بأحدهما هو الوجود القائم بالآخر لاستحالة قيام الصفة الواحدة بتحلين بل يكون وجود أحدهما غير وجود الآخر فيكون الشيء الواحد ليس بواحد بل اثنين ، ثم الكلام فيهما كما في الأول ولا شيء من الواحد ليس بواحد بل أمور غير متناهية . واحتج القائلون بكون التعين أمرا ثبوتيا زائدا بأن هذا الإنسان يشارك الإنسان الآخر فى كونه إنسانا ويخالفه في هويته ، فهويته مغايرة للإنسانية وتلك الهوية صفة ثبوتية ، لأن هذا الإنسان موجود والمفهوم من هذا جزء المفهوم من هذا الإنسان وجزء الموجود موجود فالمفهوم هذا موجود (۱) أقول الحجة الأولى أوردها للمتكلمين إنما يتوجه على تقدير ثبوت تعين كل يشترك فيه التعينات ولو كان كذلك لسكان ماهية التعين مشتركا فيها فلم يكن تعينا ، والمراد هنا من التعين ما به المغايرة بين الميلين وهو لا يكون مشتركا فيها ، وإنما يقال على أفراد النمينات النمين أو ما به لمغايرة قولا عرضيا وصار كل واحد منهما منها غير الآخر بنفسه بتعين آخر ، فلا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون للمنعين تعين . والحجة الثانية الفائلة بأن النعين لو كان ثبوتيا لاستحال انضمامه إلى الماهية إلا بعد وجود الماهية فليس بوارد لأن التعين هو الذى يوجد الماهية بسبب انضمامه إليها ، ولا يلزم انضمامه إليها ، ولا يلزم من ذلك دور ولا ثبوت التعين مرتين والحجة الثالثة القائلة بأن وجود الماهية غير وجود التعين فهما اثنان بل أمور غير متناهية ليس بصحيح لأن الماهية توصف بالوجود بسبب اتصافها بالتعين ، وكما أن الماهية المعينة لموجود واحد المائل فيكون ذلك تعريفا للشيء بنفسه ، والحق أن هذه الماهيات متصورة تصورا أوليا لأن كل واحد يعلم بالضرورة أن السواد يماثل السواد ويخالف البياض ، وتصور المماثلة والمخالفة جزء البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهيا
Question: Every two existents must necessarily be distinct in themselves. Then the theologians denied that 'ayn (identity) is a positive attribute and argued with matters: First, if t'ayyun (determination) were a positive attribute, it would be equal to the other determinations in the essence called determination, and each would be distinguished from its companion by a particularity, necessitating for the two determinations another determination ad infinitum. Second, if t'ayyun were positive, its combination with the essence after the existence of the essence would be impossible, but the essence only exists after t'ayyun. If this t'ayyun is the first, there is a circle; if another, then one thing is determined twice, which is impossible. Third, if t'ayyun is other than the essence, it is impossible for the existence subsisting in one to be the existence subsisting in the other, because one attribute cannot subsist in two [loci]; rather, the existence of one is other than the existence of the other, so the one thing would not be one but two. Then the discussion concerning them is as in the first, and nothing from the one is not one but infinite matters. And those who hold that t'ayyun is an additional positive attribute argued that this human shares with the other human in being human and differs in his identity, so his identity is other than humanity, and that identity is a positive attribute, because this human exists, and the concept of 'this' is part of the concept of 'this human', and part of the existent is existent, so the concept 'this' is existent. (1) I say: The first argument presented by the theologians is only directed on the assumption of the existence of a determination in which determinations share, and if it were so, the essence of determination would be shared in it, so it would not be determination. The intended meaning here of t'ayyun is that by which there is otherness between the two things, and it is not shared in. Rather, it is said of individuals of determinations or that by which there is otherness, as a secondary predication, and each one of them becomes other than the other by itself with another determination, so that does not necessitate for the two determinations a [further] determination. And the second argument saying that if t'ayyun were positive, its combination with the essence except after the existence of the essence would be impossible—it is not valid, because t'ayyun is that by which the essence exists through its combination to it, and it does not necessitate its combination to it, nor does that necessitate a circle or the existence of t'ayyun twice. And the third argument saying that the existence of the essence is other than the existence of t'ayyun, so they are two, indeed infinite matters—it is not correct, because the essence is described with existence due to its being described with t'ayyun, and just as the determined essence is for one existent, that would be a definition of the thing by itself. And the truth is that these essences are primarily conceived, because everyone knows necessarily that blackness is similar to blackness and differs from whiteness, and the conception of similarity and difference is part of the primary [knowledge]; it is more appropriate to be primary.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents a theological question (mas'ala) about the necessary distinction between any two existents. It then reports the arguments of the theologians who deny that 'determination' (t'ayyun) is a positive attribute, and the counter-arguments of those who affirm it. The structure is: first, three arguments against t'ayyun being positive (infinite regress, circularity, multiplication of existences). Then, the affirmative argument from identity (huwiyya) as a positive attribute distinct from common humanity. Finally, a commentator (likely the editor) refutes each of the three negative arguments: the first is only valid if t'ayyun were shared, but it is not; the second fails because t'ayyun is what brings the essence into existence; the third fails because the essence acquires existence through t'ayyun, and the resulting definition is not circular. The commentator concludes that such essences are known primarily, as with similarity and difference.

Key Terms
تعين (t'ayyun)
ماهية (mahiyya)
وجود (wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the first argument against t'ayyun being positive?
A) Infinite regress
B) Circularity
C) Multiplication of existences
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The theologians argue that if t'ayyun were a real attribute, it would lead to logical contradictions, so it must be merely conceptual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own individuality (your sense of being uniquely 'you') is merely conceptual and not a real attribute of your soul, how would that change the way you view your worth before Allah? Are you prepared to see your identity as something that does not fundamentally define you?
?
The arguments show that treating determination as a real attribute leads to logical absurdities. In your daily life, do you act as if certain roles or labels (e.g., 'successful', 'failure', 'scholar', 'layperson') are real, essential features of who you are? What would it cost you to see them as merely conceptual determinations?
?
The third argument warns that one attribute cannot inhere in two subjects, so if t'ayyun were real, it would multiply oneness. Reflect: Is there a 'label' or 'identity' you hold so tightly that it divides your heart from single-minded devotion to Allah? How might releasing that attachment bring you closer to tawhid?

Affirming T'ayyun as Positive and Refuting the Arguments
Razi presents the affirmative argument and refutations of the three negative arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument for t'ayyun as a positive attribute and how the commentator refutes the three negative arguments, concluding that these essences are primary concepts.

Core Idea
T'ayyun is what makes an essence exist; the theologians' objections fail because they misunderstand its role.

After presenting the theologians' arguments against t'ayyun being a real attribute, al-Razi now turns to the position of those who affirm it. They argue that the identity something has beyond its common essence is a positive attribute. The commentator then systematically refutes each of the three previous arguments, showing that they are based on incorrect assumptions. Finally, he notes that such essences are known by primary intuition, just like similarity and difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmative argument from identity
The affirmative side argues that a human shares humanity with others but has a unique identity (huwiyya), which is a positive attribute because 'this' is part of the concept of 'this human' and part of the existent is existent.
Note the reasoning: 'جزء الموجود موجود' (part of the existent is existent).
2
Follow the refutation of the first argument
The commentator says the first argument only works if t'ayyun were shared, but the intended meaning of t'ayyun is that by which things are other—not shared. So no infinite regress follows.
The phrase 'وهو لا يكون مشتركا فيها' emphasizes the non-shared nature.
3
Understand the refutation of the second argument
The second argument fails because t'ayyun is what brings the essence into existence, not something added later. There is no circle or double determination.
Key phrase: 'لأن التعين هو الذى يوجد الماهية بسبب انضمامه إليها'.
4
See the refutation of the third argument and the conclusion
The third argument is invalid because the essence is described as existing due to its being described with t'ayyun; there is no multiplication. The commentator then states that these essences are primarily conceived, as everyone knows blackness is similar to blackness and differs from whiteness.
The final statement 'أولى أن يكون بديهيا' asserts the primacy of such concepts.

Source Text

فيها فلم يكن تعينا ، والمراد هنا من التعين ما به المغايرة بين الميلين وهو لا يكون مشتركا فيها ، وإنما يقال على أفراد النمينات النمين أو ما به لمغايرة قولا عرضيا وصار كل واحد منهما منها غير الآخر بنفسه بتعين آخر ، فلا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون للمنعين تعين . والحجة الثانية الفائلة بأن النعين لو كان ثبوتيا لاستحال انضمامه إلى الماهية إلا بعد وجود الماهية فليس بوارد لأن التعين هو الذى يوجد الماهية بسبب ?

?

?

How does this realization change the way you view the mundane events of your day? Will you consciously remember that your existence is sustained by this attribute, or will you live as if you are self-sufficient?

Classification of Two Different Things: Opposites and Non-Opposites
Razi classifies two different things into opposites and non-opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies two different things into opposites and non-opposites, and the defining feature of opposites.

Core Idea
Two different things are either opposites that cannot coexist or non-opposites that can.

Razi begins by establishing the fundamental classification of any two different things. This distinction is crucial for later arguments about the nature of attributes and substances. Let's see how he defines opposites and what makes them distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style.
This is a definitional passage from Razi's Muhassal.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two types of two different things: opposites (diddan) and non-opposites. What is the key condition for opposites?
Opposites are defined by the impossibility of combining in essence.
3
Understand the example
Razi gives blackness and whiteness as opposites, and blackness and motion as non-opposites. Reflect on why these examples fit.
Blackness and whiteness cannot coexist in the same spot; blackness and motion can.
4
Apply the concept
Think of your own examples of opposites and non-opposites to solidify the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الغيران إما أن يكونا مثلين أو مختلفين - والمختلفان إما أن يكونا ضدين وهما الوصفان الوجوديان اللذان يمتنع اجتماعهما لذاتيهما كالسواد والبياض : وإما أن لا يكونا كذلك كالسواد والحركة ، واختلف المتكلمون في الغيرين فالمنزلة قالوا الشيئان وأصحابنا قالوا هما اللذان يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر إما بمكان أو بزمان أو وجود وعدم، والخلاف لفظى محض ، أما المثلان محدوهما بأنهما للذان يشتركان فى الصفات الدانية أو أنهما اللذان يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر أو يسد مسده وهذه العبارات مختلفة لأن الاشتراك مرادف للتماثل، والقيام مقام الآخر افظة مستعارة حقيقتها (1) أقول : الشيئان إما أن يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر بوجه من الوجوه أو لا يمكن ، والأول ينقسم إلى المنلين والمختلفين ، فإذا جعل الخيران شيئين فقط سهل الأقسام الثلاثة ، وإن جعلا ممكن المفارقة خرج منهما قسم واحد وهو الشيئان اللذان لا يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر أولا يمكن ، والقسم الأول على رأى من يقول إن صفات الله لاهى هو ولا غيره يصبح الخروج الموصوف والعفة عن كونهما غيرين على التفسير الثانى وهل هما شيئان أم لا فيه خلاف ، وقد جوز إطلاق الشيئين عليهما أبو الحسن وأبى عن ذلك أصحاب
Question: Two different things are either similars or different. And two different things are either opposites—which are two positive attributes that are impossible to combine in their essences, like blackness and whiteness—or they are not so, like blackness and motion. The theologians differed concerning 'two things': Al-Munzila said they are [simply] two things, and our companions said they are two that can be separated from each other either by place, time, or existence and non-existence, and the dispute is purely verbal. As for similars, they defined them as two that share in essential attributes, or two each of which takes the place of the other or fills its role. These expressions differ because sharing is synonymous with similarity, and taking the place of the other is a borrowed expression whose reality [...] (1) I say: Two things are either such that one can be separated from the other in some respect or cannot. The first is divided into similars and different. If one makes 'two different things' [just] two things, the three divisions become easy. If one makes them capable of separation, one division emerges: two things that cannot be separated from each other either [or cannot]. The first division, according to those who say that God's attributes are neither He nor other than Him, becomes an exception regarding the described and the attribute from being 'two things' on the second interpretation, and whether they are two things or not is disputed. Abu al-Hasan allowed applying the term 'two things' to them, while his companions refused.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines key relational categories in kalam: two things (ghayran), similars (mithlan), opposites (diddan), and different (mukhtalifan). It first classifies two different things into opposites (which cannot coexist in one subject) and non-opposites. Then it notes a verbal dispute among theologians about the definition of 'two things': some say simply two things, while others require possible separation in place, time, or existence. The commentator clarifies that the dispute is purely verbal. For similars, several definitions are given, all considered different expressions of the same concept. The commentator then expands on the classification: two things are either separable or not, and opposites and similars fit into the separable category. He also addresses a special case: God's attributes, which according to some are neither identical to Him nor other, lead to a complication in the definition of 'two things'.

Key Terms
غيران (ghayran)
مثلان (mithlan)
مختلفان (mukhtalifan)
ضدان (diddan)
وصفان وجوديان (positive attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any two different things that cannot coexist are opposites.
Correction: Opposites must be positive attributes that cannot combine in essence. For example, existence and non-existence are not opposites under this definition because non-existence is not a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes opposites from non-opposites?
A) Opposites are always colors
B) Opposites cannot combine in their essences
C) Opposites are separated by place
D) Opposites are two things that can take each other's place

Key Takeaway
Opposites are two positive attributes that cannot coexist in the same subject due to their essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a personal conflict you have where you assume two things cannot both be true or present (e.g., mercy and justice). According to Razi's classification, are they truly opposites? How does this insight change how you should approach that conflict? What is one specific step you will take to avoid treating non-opposites as opposites?
?
When you judge others, do you sometimes categorize their traits as mutually exclusive (e.g., 'they are either humble or confident')? Razi says opposites only exist between two positive attributes by essence. Is your judgment based on essence or on assumption? Commit to examining one such assumption today.
?
If opposites are only positive attributes that cannot coexist by essence, then what does that say about how you view your own shortcomings? Are you treating a sin and a virtue as opposites when they might be non-opposites? What would it mean to allow both to exist in your self-assessment without contradiction?

Definitions of 'Two Things' and Similars
Razi discusses the verbal dispute over 'two things' and defines similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the verbal dispute among theologians over the definition of 'two things' and the various definitions of similars.

Core Idea
The dispute over defining 'two things' is purely verbal, while similars are defined by shared essential attributes.

Razi presents a dispute among theologians about what exactly 'two things' means — is it simply two entities, or must they be separable? He then moves to the definition of similars, offering multiple phrasings that all point to the same idea. This lesson unpacks these discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the second part of the source carefully. Note the different positions on defining 'two things'.
The passage includes a comment by Razi himself ('I say...') which expands the classification.
2
Analyze the dispute
Identify the two views on 'two things' given by Al-Munzila and 'our companions'. Understand why Razi calls it verbal.
3
Examine the definitions of similars
The passage offers multiple definitions of similars. See how they are essentially synonymous.
Razi notes that 'sharing' is the core idea.
4
Understand the commentator's analysis
Razi then proposes his own classification: two things are either separable or not. This leads to a discussion about God's attributes.
This part is more advanced; grasp the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الغيران إما أن يكونا مثلين أو مختلفين - والمختلفان إما أن يكونا ضدين وهما الوصفان الوجوديان اللذان يمتنع اجتماعهما لذاتيهما كالسواد والبياض : وإما أن لا يكونا كذلك كالسواد والحركة ، واختلف المتكلمون في الغيرين فالمنزلة قالوا الشيئان وأصحابنا قالوا هما اللذان يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر إما بمكان أو بزمان أو وجود وعدم، والخلاف لفظى محض ، أما المثلان محدوهما بأنهما للذان يشتركان فى الصفات الدانية أو أنهما اللذان يقوم كل واحد منهما مقام الآخر أو يسد مسده وهذه العبارات مختلفة لأن الاشتراك مرادف للتماثل، والقيام مقام الآخر افظة مستعارة حقيقتها (1) أقول : الشيئان إما أن يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر بوجه من الوجوه أو لا يمكن ، والأول ينقسم إلى المنلين والمختلفين ، فإذا جعل الخيران شيئين فقط سهل الأقسام الثلاثة ، وإن جعلا ممكن المفارقة خرج منهما قسم واحد وهو الشيئان اللذان لا يمكن أن يفارق أحدهما الآخر أولا يمكن ، والقسم الأول على رأى من يقول إن صفات الله لاهى هو ولا غيره يصبح الخروج الموصوف والعفة عن كونهما غيرين على التفسير الثانى وهل هما شيئان أم لا فيه خلاف ، وقد جوز إطلاق الشيئين عليهما أبو الحسن وأبى عن ذلك أصحاب
Question: Two different things are either similars or different. And two different things are either opposites—which are two positive attributes that are impossible to combine in their essences, like blackness and whiteness—or they are not so, like blackness and motion. The theologians differed concerning 'two things': Al-Munzila said they are [simply] two things, and our companions said they are two that can be separated from each other either by place, time, or existence and non-existence, and the dispute is purely verbal. As for similars, they defined them as two that share in essential attributes, or two each of which takes the place of the other or fills its role. These expressions differ because sharing is synonymous with similarity, and taking the place of the other is a borrowed expression whose reality [...] (1) I say: Two things are either such that one can be separated from the other in some respect or cannot. The first is divided into similars and different. If one makes 'two different things' [just] two things, the three divisions become easy. If one makes them capable of separation, one division emerges: two things that cannot be separated from each other either [or cannot]. The first division, according to those who say that God's attributes are neither He nor other than Him, becomes an exception regarding the described and the attribute from being 'two things' on the second interpretation, and whether they are two things or not is disputed. Abu al-Hasan allowed applying the term 'two things' to them, while his companions refused.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines key relational categories in kalam: two things (ghayran), similars (mithlan), opposites (diddan), and different (mukhtalifan). It first classifies two different things into opposites (which cannot coexist in one subject) and non-opposites. Then it notes a verbal dispute among theologians about the definition of 'two things': some say simply two things, while others require possible separation in place, time, or existence. The commentator clarifies that the dispute is purely verbal. For similars, several definitions are given, all considered different expressions of the same concept. The commentator then expands on the classification: two things are either separable or not, and opposites and similars fit into the separable category. He also addresses a special case: God's attributes, which according to some are neither identical to Him nor other, lead to a complication in the definition of 'two things'.

Key Terms
الغيران (al-ghayran)
المثلان (al-mithlan)
الشيئان (al-shay'an)
المنزلة (Al-Munzila)
أصحابنا (our companions)
خلاف لفظى (verbal dispute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the dispute over the definition of 'two things'?
A) It is a substantial disagreement
B) It is purely verbal
C) The correct view is that of Al-Munzila
D) The dispute cannot be resolved

Key Takeaway
The dispute over defining 'two things' is purely verbal, and similars are defined by sharing essential attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent disagreement you had about religious matters. Was it truly about substance or merely about how terms were defined? How can you avoid wasting time on verbal disputes in the future?
?
In your understanding of God's attributes, do you sometimes blur essential and accidental attributes? This lesson reminds us that similars are defined by essential attributes. How does this affect your tawhid?
?
Razi's classification shows that some theological debates hinge on definitions. Are there positions you hold that might be based on mere verbal distinctions rather than core truths? Will you re-examine them for clarity?

The Infinite Regress Argument Against Positive Otherness
Razi presents a refutation of the claim that otherness is a positive attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that 'difference' is a positive attribute leads to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
Treating 'difference' as positive forces an infinite chain of meanings.

This passage presents an argument from some theologians that the property of being 'other' (taghayur) is a real, positive attribute. Razi shows that this assumption inevitably leads to an infinite regress of meanings, a consequence that is rationally impossible. Understanding this refutation reveals how Islamic theologians carefully analyze relational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the opening: 'Some claimed that two different things are different by a meaning.' This is the claim to be refuted.
2
Trace the reasoning from positive otherness to infinite regress
Follow the argument: otherness exists, is not negative → it is positive → this positive meaning must itself differ from others, leading to an infinite chain.
Pay attention to how each step forces the next.
3
Understand why infinite regress is impossible
Reflect on why infinite regress is considered invalid in kalam: it violates the principle that a chain must have a starting point. This shows the flaw in treating relational concepts as positive attributes.
This is a standard critique in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : زعم بعضهم أن الغيرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان والضدان والمختلفان احتجوا بأن المفهوم من كون السواد والبياض سوادا وبياضا مغاير للمفهوم من كونهما غيرين ومختلفين وضدين ، وكذلك بأن التغاير والاختلاف والتضاد ، حاصلة في غير السواد والبياض وظاهره أنه ليس أمراً سلبيا فهو أمر ثبوتى ، فثبت أن المتغايرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان مقابلان لمعنى ، ثم قالوا وذلك المعنى لا بد وأن يغاير غيره فغايرته لغيره معنى قائم به ، وهو أنه لا بد وأن يكون إما مثلا لغيره أو مغايراً له ومخالفا و مسائلته مع غيره أو مخالفته له معنى قائم به ، ثم الكلام فيه كما فى الأول وهو يوجب القول بمعان لا نهاية لها .
Question: Some claimed that two different things are different by a meaning, and likewise similars, opposites, and different. They argued that the concept of blackness and whiteness being black and white is different from the concept of them being other, different, and opposites; and similarly that otherness, difference, and opposition exist in other than blackness and whiteness, and it appears not to be a negative matter, so it is a positive matter. Thus it is established that two different things are different by a meaning, and likewise similars correspond to a meaning. Then they said: that meaning must differ from something else, so its otherness from something else is a meaning subsisting in it, and it is that it must be either similar to something else or other than it and different, and its opposition to something else or its difference from it is a meaning subsisting in it. Then the discussion concerning it is as in the first, and it necessitates the assertion of infinite meanings.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage reports the argument of those who claim that being 'different' (ghayriyya) is a positive attribute. Their reasoning: the concept of being black or white is distinct from the concept of being other or opposite, and such relational concepts are found in many cases and are not merely negative, so they must be positive. They then argue that this positive meaning itself must differ from others, leading to a chain of further meanings ad infinitum. The passage ends by noting that this necessitates infinite regress, which is impossible. The author (Razi) seems to present this as a refutation or at least a problematic consequence, as the commentator also suggests in the previous lesson that such regress is invalid.

Key Terms
غير (ghayr)
تغاير (taghayur)
معنى (ma'na)
ثبوتي (thubuti)
سلسلة غير متناهية (infinite chain)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Relational properties like difference are always positive attributes.
Correction: Treating them as positive leads to infinite regress, so they are considered negative or relative in Sunni kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the argument presented, what follows if 'otherness' is taken as a positive meaning?
A) The world is eternal
B) Infinite regress of meanings
C) Two things become one
D) Definition is impossible

Key Takeaway
Treating 'otherness' as a positive attribute forces an infinite chain of meanings, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you defined yourself by being 'different' from someone else—whether in belief, status, or identity. How did that difference become a 'positive' thing in your mind, and did it lead to an endless cycle of comparisons and divisions? Can you now see that difference is not a real attribute but a relation, and what would it take to let go of that self-definition?
?
The argument shows that treating otherness as a positive attribute leads to infinite regress. In your spiritual life, where have you sought 'meaning' in what makes you distinct from others—perhaps in piety, knowledge, or practice? Could this be a subtle form of pride that separates you from the unity of submission? What concrete step can you take today to see yourself not as 'other' but as a servant among servants?
?
If otherness is not a real positive thing, then all distinctions are merely relational. How does this truth affect the way you interact with someone you consider deeply different from yourself—in religion, worldview, or life circumstances? Are you willing to see that otherness as a relational accident rather than a fundamental reality, and what would that mean for your next encounter with that person?

The Claim that Diversity Requires a Positive Attribute
Al-Razi presents a claim and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that different things must be different by a positive meaning, and how the proponents justified it.

Core Idea
Diversity is not negative; it must be a real attribute.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents the view of some scholars who claim that the fact that two things are different or similar is not merely a lack of identity but a positive quality. They argue by comparing the concept of being black and white with being different, concluding that difference must be caused by a positive meaning. Let's examine their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and proof
Read the Arabic passage first, then the translation. Notice that the argument uses the example of black and white to show that the concept of being different is distinct from the concept of being black or white.
The term 'معنى' (meaning) here refers to a positive attribute.
2
Understand the logic
The proof has two parts: (1) The concept of diversity is different from the concept of the things themselves. (2) Diversity is found in many things and is not a negation. Therefore, diversity must be a positive attribute. Ask yourself: Is this convincing?
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that two different things are different by a meaning, and likewise similars. This sets up the next step where they apply the same reasoning to that meaning.
Consider what might happen if we apply this reasoning to the meaning itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: زعم بعضهم أن الغيرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان والضدان والمختلفان. احتجوا بأن المفهوم من كون السواد والبياض سوادا وبياضا مغاير للمفهوم من كونهما غيرين ومختلفين وضدين، وكذلك بأن التغاير والاختلاف والتضاد، حاصلة في غير السواد والبياض، وظاهره أنه ليس أمرا سلبيا فهو أمر ثبوتي، فثبت أن المتغايرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان مقابلان لمعنى. ثم قالوا وذلك المعنى لا بد وأن يغاير غيره فغايرته لغيره معنى قائم به، وهو أنه لا بد وأن يكون إما مثلا لغيره أو مغايرا له ومخالفا ومسائلته مع غيره أو مخالفته له معنى قائم به، ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول وهو يوجب القول بمعان لا نهاية لها. فالتزموا ذلك وكلامنا في هذا الباب قد تقدم.
Question: Some claimed that two different things are different by a meaning, and similarly two similars, two opposites, and two different things. They argued: The concept of black and white being black and white is different from the concept of them being two different things, two diverse things, and two opposites. Likewise, because diversity, difference, and opposition are found in other than black and white, and it is apparent that it is not a negative thing, so it is a positive thing. Thus it is established that two different things are different by a meaning, and similarly two similars are [related] to a meaning. Then they said: That meaning must be different from other things; so its difference from other things is a meaning subsisting in it, and it is that it must either be similar to other things or different from it and contrary to it, and its being with other things or its opposition to other things is a meaning subsisting in it. Then the discussion continues as before, and it leads to postulating infinite meanings. So they committed to that, and our discussion on this topic has already preceded.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a position held by some scholars that the distinction between two things is due to a positive attribute (meaning) inherent in them. They argue by example: the concepts of being black and white are different from the concepts of being different or opposite. Since diversity (تغاير) is found in things beyond just black and white, and it is not a negative concept (سلب), it must be a positive attribute. Thus, each pair of things that are different, similar, or opposite have a meaning that makes them so. But then they apply the same reasoning to that meaning: it must also be different from others, and that difference requires another meaning, leading to an infinite regress of meanings. Al-Razi points out that this leads to an infinite chain, which is problematic. The phrase 'فالتزموا ذلك وكلامنا في هذا الباب قد تقدم' indicates that the proponents accepted this consequence, and al-Razi says his argument on this topic has already been presented.

Key Terms
غيرين
مثلان
ضدان
معنى
تغاير
سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Diversity is purely negative (absence of identity).
Correction: The passage argues diversity is positive because it is a distinct concept found in many things.

Checkpoint
What example is used to argue that the concept of being different is distinct?
A) Black and white
B) Hot and cold
C) Good and evil
D) Square and circle

Key Takeaway
The proponents argue that diversity requires a positive meaning because it is not negative and is a distinct concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difference of opinion among Muslims—on fiqh, theology, or practice—do you subconsciously dismiss it as a mere lack of correctness, or do you treat it as a real, meaningful attribute of the other person's faith? How would your conversations change if you genuinely accepted that diversity is a positive reality created by Allah? This week, choose one person you disagree with and deliberately seek to understand their perspective as a 'positive meaning' rather than a deficiency.
?
Consider the differences between you and your spouse, parents, or close friend. Do you see them primarily as obstacles or nuisances (negatives) or as real, substantive attributes that shape your relationship? The passage argues that difference itself is a positive thing. What would it mean for you to thank Allah for the differences in your relationships, and how would that transform your interactions? Commit to expressing appreciation for one specific difference today.
?
The proof in this lesson relies on the idea that 'difference' is not just the absence of sameness but a real quality. Reflect on your own relationship with Allah: Are your sins merely absences of obedience, or are they real, positive acts of rebellion? How does viewing them as 'positive attributes' (in a negative sense) affect your repentance and need for forgiveness? This Ramadan, resolve to treat each sin as a real act that requires real turning back, not just a passive omission.

The Infinite Regress of Meanings
Al-Razi extends the argument to show infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how applying the same reasoning to the meaning itself leads to an infinite regress, and why this is problematic.

Core Idea
One meaning leads to another, ad infinitum.

After establishing that difference is by a meaning, the proponents then apply the same logic to that meaning itself. They argue that the meaning must also be similar or different to other things, requiring yet another meaning. This leads to an infinite chain, which they accept but which Al-Razi considers a flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the infinite regress argument
Read the Arabic and translation. The proponents now say: the meaning that makes two things different must itself be different from other things. That difference is another meaning. Then that meaning must also be different, leading to an infinite chain.
The word 'مسائلته' is unclear, but the overall meaning is that each meaning requires another meaning for its difference.
2
Plot the regress
Try to diagram the regress: Meaning1 makes two things different -> Meaning1 must be different from other things -> Meaning2 makes Meaning1 different -> Meaning2 must be different from other things -> ... infinite.
This shows that if you grant the initial premise, you are forced to accept an infinite number of meanings.
3
Consider Al-Razi's response
Al-Razi notes that the proponents accepted this infinite regress, but he says his earlier discussion (elsewhere) has already refuted this position. Reflect on why infinite regress is considered a problem in argumentation.
In logic, an infinite regress is often a sign that the premise is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: زعم بعضهم أن الغيرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان والضدان والمختلفان. احتجوا بأن المفهوم من كون السواد والبياض سوادا وبياضا مغاير للمفهوم من كونهما غيرين ومختلفين وضدين، وكذلك بأن التغاير والاختلاف والتضاد، حاصلة في غير السواد والبياض، وظاهره أنه ليس أمرا سلبيا فهو أمر ثبوتي، فثبت أن المتغايرين متغايران بمعنى وكذا المثلان مقابلان لمعنى. ثم قالوا وذلك المعنى لا بد وأن يغاير غيره فغايرته لغيره معنى قائم به، وهو أنه لا بد وأن يكون إما مثلا لغيره أو مغايرا له ومخالفا ومسائلته مع غيره أو مخالفته له معنى قائم به، ثم الكلام فيه كما في الأول وهو يوجب القول بمعان لا نهاية لها. فالتزموا ذلك وكلامنا في هذا الباب قد تقدم.
Question: Some claimed that two different things are different by a meaning, and similarly two similars, two opposites, and two different things. They argued: The concept of black and white being black and white is different from the concept of them being two different things, two diverse things, and two opposites. Then they said: That meaning must be different from other things; so its difference from other things is a meaning subsisting in it, and it is that it must either be similar to other things or different from it and contrary to it, and its being with other things or its opposition to other things is a meaning subsisting in it. Then the discussion continues as before, and it leads to postulating infinite meanings. So they committed to that, and our discussion on this topic has already preceded.

They argue by example: the concepts of being black and white are different from the concepts of being different or opposite. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is harmless and does not invalidate an argument.
Correction: Infinite regress is problematic because it never terminates and indicates a flaw in the reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
