Refuting the Objection: Can a Thing Change from Impossible to Possible?
Imam Razi refutes an objection about the change of a thing's essential status from impossible to possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of the objection that a thing's essential status (impossible, possible, necessary) can change over time, and you will grasp the precise meaning of 'possible' (ممكن) as intellectual non-impossibility.

Core Idea
A thing's essence does not change; possibility is about what the intellect does not deem impossible, not about actual states.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection and answer from Imam Razi regarding the definition of the 'possible' (ممكن). An opponent argues that since originated things pass from non-existence to existence, they must be susceptible to both states, and possibly even change from being essentially impossible to essentially necessary. Imam Razi responds by clarifying the true meaning of 'susceptibility' and showing that the objection rests on a misunderstanding of possibility. This lesson unpacks the dialectical steps and reinforces the correct kalam definition of the possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the entire passage once. Notice how the opponent builds from the premise that originated things have a beginning to argue for susceptibility to both existence and non-existence.
Pay attention to the opponent's claim that a thing could be impossible by itself before existence.
2
Identify the opponent's core argument
The opponent suggests that if a thing has a beginning, then before that beginning it was impossible by itself, and after that beginning it became possible by itself. He then asks why this change cannot extend to becoming necessary by itself.
This challenges the stability of the three rational categories (impossible, possible, necessary).
3
Learn Imam Razi's two-fold response
First, Razi questions whether the non-existent can be a subject of judgment at all, given the opponent's own doctrine. Second, he clarifies the definition of 'possible': it does not mean the essence actually possesses both states, but rather that the intellect does not deem it impossible for the essence to remain or cease.
The key is shifting from actual states to intellectual permissibility.
4
Reflect on the time argument
Razi denies that the possible has a fixed 'first moment' of validity, because supposing it entered existence a moment earlier would entail being eternal, which is absurd. This shows that the opponent's premise about a first moment of possibility is flawed.
Consider why this argument safeguards the concept of creation from eternity.
5
Connect to the broader kalam framework
Understand that this definition of possibility is crucial for proving the existence of a creator and distinguishing contingent beings from the Necessary Being. It prevents confusion between essential and temporal attributes.
This passage is foundational for kalam proofs of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل الكلام على هذه المقدمات قد تقدم إلا على قولنا إن كل محدث ممكن .
قوله : المحدث كان معدوماً ثم صار موجوداً فيكون قابلا للعدم والوجود لا محالة .
قلنا من مذهبكم أن المعدوم ليس بشيء لاعين ولاذات بل كان نفيا محضا ، وإذا كان كذلك استحال الحكم عليه بالقبول والاقبول ، سلمنا صحة الحكم عليه ، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها حين كانت معدومة كانت واجبة العدم لعينها ثم في زمان وجودها صارت واجبة الوجود لعينها ، تقريره أن الشيء بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم لا يجوز في العقل فرض تقدمه لا إلى الأول وإلا لزم صحة كون الشىء مع كونه مسبوقا بالعدم أزليا وذلك محال ، فإذا لصحة وجوده بداية فقبل تلك الصحة كان ممتنعا لذاته ثم انقلب ممكنا لذاته، وإذا جاز ذلك فلم لا يجوز أن يقال كان متما لذاته ثم انقلب واجبا لذاته .
والجواب : أن قولنا الممكن قابل للوجود والعدم لا نعنى به أن تلك الماهية متقررة حالة الوجود والعدم يل نعنى به أن الماهية لا يمتنع فى العقل بقاؤها كما كانت ولا يمتنع في العقل بطلانها . قوله : لم لا يجوز أن تكون الماهية ممتعة لذاتها في وقت ثم تنقلب واجبة لذاتها في وقت آخر .
قلنا : هب أن الأمر كذلك لكن حصول الامتناع يتوقف على حضور وقته المخصوص وحصول الوجوب يتوقف على حضور الوقت الآخر ، والماهية من حيث هى هى مع قطع النظر عن الوقتين لا يبقى لها إلا القبول. قوله : الممكن المأخوذ بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم لصحة وجوده أول .
قلنا : لا نسلم وإلا لزم أن يكون فرض دخوله في الوجود قبل ذلك الوقت بلحظة يوجب صيرورته أزليا
وذلك محال بالبديهة
If it is said: the discussion on these premises has already been presented except regarding our statement that every originated thing is possible. His statement: The originated thing was non-existent then became existent, so it is necessarily susceptible to non-existence and existence. We say: according to your doctrine, the non-existent is not a thing, neither essence nor entity, but rather was pure negation. And if that is the case, it is impossible to judge it as susceptible or non-susceptible. We grant the validity of the judgment upon it, but why is it not permissible to say that when it was non-existent it was necessary of non-existence by its essence, then at the time of its existence it became necessary of existence by its essence? Its exposition: a thing with the condition of being preceded by non-existence, it is not permissible in the intellect to suppose its priority without beginning; otherwise it would entail the validity of the thing, while being preceded by non-existence, being eternal, and that is impossible. Thus, if its existence has a beginning, then before that beginning it was impossible by itself, then it turned into possible by itself. And if that is permissible, then why is it not permissible to say it was impossible by itself then turned into necessary by itself? The answer: our statement that the possible is susceptible to existence and non-existence does not mean that that essence is established in the state of existence and non-existence; rather we mean that the essence is not impossible in the intellect for it to remain as it was, and not impossible in the intellect for it to cease. His statement: why is it not permissible for the essence to be impossible by itself at one time then turn into necessary by itself at another time? We say: grant that it is so, but the occurrence of impossibility depends on the presence of its specific time, and the occurrence of necessity depends on the presence of the other time, and the essence in itself, disregarding the two times, has nothing left for it except susceptibility. His statement: the possible taken with the condition of being preceded by non-existence has the validity of its existence first. We say: we do not accept that; otherwise it would entail that supposing its entry into existence a moment before that time would necessitate its becoming eternal, and that is intuitively impossible.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and answer) regarding the definition of 'possible' (ممكن) and whether a thing can change from being essentially impossible to essentially possible over time. The objection begins by noting that the earlier discussion covered most premises except the claim that every originated thing is possible. The objector then argues: since an originated thing passes from non-existence to existence, it must be susceptible to both non-existence and existence. The response refutes this by first pointing out that the objector's own doctrine (of the non-existent being pure negation) makes it impossible to speak of 'susceptibility' in non-existence. Even if we grant that judgment, the objector asks: why could it not be that the thing, when non-existent, was necessary of non-existence by its essence, then later becomes necessary of existence by its essence? He argues that a thing preceded by non-existence cannot be supposed to have no beginning (otherwise it would be eternal while preceded by non-existence, impossible). So it has a beginning before which it was impossible, then became possible. If such change is allowed, why not allow change from impossible to necessary? Imam Razi answers by clarifying the definition of 'possible': it does not mean the essence actually has both states, but rather that the intellect does not deem it impossible for the essence to continue existing or to cease to exist. As for the objection about changing essential status, he replies that impossibility and necessity are time-dependent, but the essence in itself, disregarding time, retains only susceptibility. Regarding the premise that the possible preceded by non-existence has its validity of existence first (i.e., there is a first moment of validity), Razi denies this, because supposing it entered existence a moment earlier would entail it being eternal, which is self-evidently impossible.

Key Terms
الممكن
الممتنع
الواجب
الماهية
العدم
الوجود
القبول
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'possible' is defined by actually having both existence and non-existence simultaneously in some sense.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that 'possible' means the intellect does not deem it impossible for the essence to remain as it was or to cease. It does not require actual possession of both states.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what does it mean to say that a possible (ممكن) is 'susceptible' (قابل) to existence and non-existence?
A) It actually has both existence and non-existence at different times.
B) The intellect does not deem it impossible for it to remain as it is or to cease.
C) It is essentially neither existent nor non-existent.
D) It changes from impossible to necessary by itself.

Key Takeaway
The possible (ممكن) is not defined by actual possession of existence and non-existence, but by the intellect's non-impossibility of its remaining or ceasing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own moral failures, do you treat them as proof that you are 'essentially' incapable of change, or do you see them as time-bound states that do not define your essence? How would adopting Razi's distinction between essence and temporal judgment transform your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
The objector assumed that a thing's essence could switch from impossible to possible. Have you ever assumed that your own repentance is impossible because of the magnitude of your sin? What would it mean to truly believe that possibility is about intellectual permissibility, not about your past experience?
?
Razi insists that the possible is not defined by actual states but by the intellect's permission. In your worship, do you let your actual distractions and shortcomings convince you that you 'cannot' pray with presence, or do you act on the intellectual possibility that you can? What would change if you started each prayer with the conviction that nothing is impossible for you by Allah's permission?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
Two Meanings of Priority in Refuting Circularity
Razi analyzes two meanings of priority to refute circularity and questions the inference from circularity to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining priority as 'being an effect' leads to absurdity in circular causality and why refuting circularity does not automatically refute infinite regress.

Core Idea
Priority cannot mean 'being an effect' in circular causality, and refuting circularity does not prove infinite regress false.

In refuting circular reasoning, theologians often appeal to the concept of priority. But what does 'priority' mean in this context? Imam al-Razi dissects two possible meanings. This passage clarifies that if priority means being an effect, circularity leads to self-contradiction. Yet he also warns that refuting circularity does not settle the issue of infinite regress. Let's examine his precise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style of Razi's argument.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of priority
The passage begins by distinguishing two meanings of 'priority' (tqaddum). Find and note each one.
3
Understand why the first meaning leads to absurdity
If priority means 'being an effect', then claiming mutual priority results in each thing being prior to itself. Why is that impossible?
This is a self-contradiction.
4
Consider the second meaning and its requirement
If priority means something else, Razi demands that the quiddity be explained. Only then can one prove impossibility.
5
Grasp the final concession and challenge
The passage concedes the corruption of circularity but questions why infinite regress follows. Notice the logical gap.
This challenges a common assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه مؤثرا فيه، أو تعنى به أمرا آخر، فإن عنيت به المؤثر كان قولك لو كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرا في الآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على الآخر إلزاما للشيء على نفسه ، وإن عنيت به أمرا آخر فلابد من بيان ماهية ذلك التقدم ليتمكن من إقامة الدلالة على أن الشيء يستحيل أن يكون متقدما على نفسه بذلك المعنى، سلمنا فساد الدور فلم قلت أن التسلسل باطل .
[You mean] it is an effect in it, or you mean something else. If you mean by it the effect, then your statement 'if each of them were an effect in the other, then each of them would be prior to the other' would be an imposition of the thing upon itself. And if you mean by it something else, then it is necessary to explain the quiddity of that priority so that one can provide evidence that it is impossible for a thing to be prior to itself in that sense. We grant the corruption of circularity, but why do you say that infinite regress is false?
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of priority (tqaddum) in the context of refuting circular causality (dawr). The author distinguishes two possible meanings: either priority means being an effect (mu'aththir) in the other, or something else. If the first meaning is intended, then claiming mutual priority leads to each thing being prior to itself, which is absurd. If the second meaning is intended, one must specify that quiddity before proving impossibility. The passage then concedes the corruption of circularity (fasād al-dawr) but questions the opponent's assertion that infinite regress (tasalsul) is false, implying that the refutation of circularity does not automatically prove the falsity of infinite regress.

Key Terms
تقدم
دور
تسلسل
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refuting circularity automatically proves infinite regress is false.
Correction: The passage shows that even if circularity is corrupt, it does not follow that infinite regress is false; separate arguments are needed.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is it problematic to define priority as 'being an effect'?
A) It leads to an infinite regress.
B) It results in a thing being prior to itself.
C) It requires a definition of quiddity.
D) It contradicts the concept of causality.

Key Takeaway
In refuting circularity, the meaning of priority must be precisely defined; even then, refuting circularity does not negate infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi distinguishes between two meanings of 'priority' and shows that refuting circularity does not automatically refute infinite regress. In your own reasoning about matters of faith, have you ever assumed that disproving one flawed argument automatically disproves another related claim? Where might you be committing this mistake, and what will you do to correct it?
?
The passage insists on precisely defining terms before proving impossibility. Is there a concept in your belief system (e.g., 'God's power', 'free will') that you hold without a clear definition? How might this vagueness weaken your arguments? Commit to defining one term clearly this week.
?
Razi challenges a common assumption with a simple question: 'Why do you say infinite regress is false?' This forces us to reexamine our own assumptions. What assumption about Allah's existence or attributes have you accepted without evidence? Are you willing to question it as Razi did? What step will you take today to critically examine that assumption?

Logic and Technical Terms

Impossible in Itself vs. Impossible Due to Another
The commentator distinguishes between impossibility in itself and impossibility due to another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between impossibility in itself and impossibility due to another, and its implications for divine power.

Core Idea
Not all impossibilities are equal: some are intrinsic, others are due to external factors.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction from the commentator in the Muhassal. He clarifies that something can be impossible for one reason but still possible in itself. This distinction is crucial for understanding divine attributes of power and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two key terms: المحال لذاته and المحال لغيره.
Pay attention to the commentator's concise style.
2
Identify the two categories
List the two types of impossibility mentioned and note how each is defined. The impossible in itself is described as 'not an object of power', while the impossible due to another is 'possible in itself'.
The term 'impossible in itself' refers to contradictions.
3
Understand the implications for divine power
Reflect on why the impossible in itself is not an object of power, while the impossible due to another can be within divine power. The key is that the latter's impossibility is not intrinsic.
This answers objections about God's power over things like evil or suffering.
4
Connect to the objection
The passage is part of an answer to al-Nazzam. Consider how this distinction resolves the issue: something can be impossible due to wisdom but still possible in itself and thus within God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : أصل الجواب أن المحال لذاته غير مقدور ، أما المحال لغيره ممكن لذاته فكونه مقدور آلا ينافي كونه محالا لغيره .
I say: The basis of the answer is that the impossible in itself (al-muhal li-dhatihi) is not an object of power. As for the impossible due to another (al-muhal li-ghayrihi), it is possible in itself, so its being an object of power does not contradict its being impossible due to another.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a concise clarification from the commentator on the concept of impossibility in relation to divine power. He distinguishes two types: al-muhal li-dhatihi (impossible in itself, like a contradiction) cannot be an object of power. Al-muhal li-ghayrihi (impossible due to an external factor) is possible in itself, but its actual occurrence is prevented by something else (e.g., lack of motive). Such things are still within God's power, because the impossibility is not intrinsic. This is the answer to al-Nazzam's objection: creating evil is impossible not in itself but due to God's wisdom, so it is still an object of power.

Key Terms
محال لذاته
محال لغيره
ممكن
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is impossible for any reason, it cannot be within divine power.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that only intrinsic impossibility (like contradictions) is beyond power; impossibility due to external factors does not negate power over the thing in itself.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the impossible due to another (al-muhal li-ghayrihi)?
A) It is not an object of power.
B) It is possible in itself and can be an object of power.
C) It is the same as impossible in itself.
D) It cannot exist.

Key Takeaway
The impossible due to another is possible in itself and thus within divine power, unlike the impossible in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to change, do you secretly assume it is beyond God's power, or do you trust that His power is limited only by His wisdom and will? How does this lesson challenge you to repent of any subtle disbelief in His omnipotence?
?
If you truly believed that God's power extends to everything that is not intrinsically contradictory, how would your du'a change for the 'impossible' situations in your life? Write one specific prayer you have avoided because you thought it was 'impossible' and commit to praying it now.
